The Red Page
More War
Ilindenburg''s March Into London, trans-
lated from tbe German by L. G. Redmond-
Howard (Long, per E. W. Cole, Sydney;
Is. 3d.), is, so far, tbe funniest book on tlie
war. So absurd is this forecast that the
reader almost suspects that it has been
written, as a satire on German psychology,
by an Englishman. But apparently they do
these things in all seriousness in Verminy.
And, certainly, no English author could in-
vent the examples of Hun humor with which
the Hun author has alleviated his horrors.
The war is, of course, solely due to ,Eng-
land. That Machiavellian nation even
founded, long before the war, “the world-
historical Isolation Society for the Destruc-
tion and Dividing Up of Germany.” And
“the devilish plan for the destruction of Ger-
many was settled in London, had been con-
sidered for years carefully iu all its details,
and if signs and wonders had not happened,
it must, in human judgment, have led to
complete success.. .According-to the articles
of the British Isolation Society, the torch
flamed up in Russia first. It was, in truth,
ignited two years too soon; but otherwise
everything went as laid down in the pro-
gramme.” And that programme was
thorough. “As early as 1913, in Maubeurge,
the Isolation Society had had mountains of
munitions piled up, and it had compelled
Belgium to develop Antwerp into the most
powerful fortress in the world.”
However, “Young Michael,” we are inter-
ested to learn, “in the second year of the
war possessed tbe same laughing confidence
of victory as on tlie first day of mobilisa-
tion.” But before Ilindenburg can march to
London certain “signs and wonders” had to
happen; and the German author kindly pro-
vides them for us. First, Russia concludes
a separate peace with her conquerors ; next,
tbe noble, but almost forgotten exhortation,
“On, on to Calais!” materialises, and Hin-
depburg captures that city. Curiously
enough, tbe attack ou Verdun is anticipated
by the author’s account of the attack on
Calais. “The Eastern motor-batteries and
the Essen giants” are the cause of the col-
lapse of the French and British armies. Well,
they have failed at Verdun. The final
miracle occupies only one paragraph: “A
giant swarm of Zeppelins, of whose size even
German soldiers did not venture to dream,
travelled one .foggy morning to the west
coast of England and sought out the British
navy. With a thousand bombs 50 full hits
were made. Explosions completed the work
of destruction. Almost at tbe same time a
gigantic fleet of submarines broke into the
British naval harbor 1 and completed the
work.” Evidently that is the Hun concep-
tion of “The Day.”
For eight days new giant Ivrupp guns at
Calais destroyed everything living on the
south coast of England ; then three water-
ways from Zeebrugge, Dunkirk and Calais to
England were secured east and west by a
steel wall of torpedo boats and njines and
submarines, and the Hun army landed in
England. But, of course, England did not
fight fairly. The noble Huns bad “to face a
sharpshooters’ warfare, exceeding in its
atrocities the performance of Belgian black-
guards.” It is curious to find the lie about
the Belgians still persisting in the minds of
tbe Huns after this lapse of time. So the
Huns nobly protest. “We shall repeat the
lesson of Louvain upon the shooters from
behind hedges if need be! We want an
honorable, battle with soldiers!”
At this stage the author finds it necessary
to introduce another miracle—also in one
paragraph. “Germans, Austrians and Turks
have fought the decisive battle at the Pyra-
mids ! The British army is in great part
broken up; the rest have been captured.”
But still the British will not play the game.
“The Euglish drive before them all captive
Germans, soldiers and civilians as bullet
shields.” Evidently some echoes of the Hun
tactics have filtered through into Verminy;
but note how delightfully the author trans-
poses his facts! The capture and surrender
of London is painstakingly described,' and
made more gruesome here and there, by
depressing examples of Hun humor. Even
to the author, however, the assault on London
seems a difficult job; hut then he probably
remembered that if it were a walk-oyer
there would be no credit for Ilindenburg.
The author’s mistakes are delicious. He
pictures the Indian troops as being driven
into the front ranks of the battle by their
brutal English masters. He states that the
British offered £6 “for the head of a German-
Turk delivered dead or alive to the English
army.” And he believes that it is Australian
blacks who are fighting against him. What
funny things these Huns are told!
wo are told that: “There are two words in
the German language which are possessed
alone by Germany and which cannot be
translated into English in all their meanings.
One is ‘Qemuet’ {innermost fine feeling) and
the other ‘Froemmigkeit’ ( religious seme)."
The Huns can have them, but it is difficult
to credit them with any use for them.
J*
The indictment of such a book as Towards
a Lasting Settlement, by a number of essay-
ists, edited by Charles Roden Buxton (Allen
and Unwin,_ 2s. 6d.), is its prevailing dulness.
This book is the product of minds that are
not bright. G. Lowes Dickinson is right in
stressing the fact that the mistrust of the
two groups of Powers in Europe was mutual.
“The Germans believed that the Entente was
a hostile and aggressive combination directed
against them.” ‘lt was even so regarded by
a neutral State like Belgium. “The war may
have been a war of mutual fear
Duplicity was hound to be suspected on both
sides.” The system was enough in itself to
produce war.
H. Sidebotham, discussing British “naval-
ism,” aptly calls attention to the American
opinion of our naval policy. At the begin-
ning of the war the average American was
prepared to think of British naval power
much as the Englishman thinks of German
military power. And the.author draws an
interesting parallel between German militar-
ism and British “navalism” —hardly to our
advantage. To the American British naval
policy was, and is, as much a tyranny on
sea as, to us, German militarism is tyranny-
on land. And he is not the only contributor
to this volume who points out that the ques-
tion of British “navalism” is one that
Edward Grey has declared might well form
the subject of negotiation at the peace con-
ference.
But J. A. Hobson, in his essay, “The
Open Door,” betrays an appalling ignorance
of the ideals of the Dominions. His Free
Trade proposal for settling the difficulties of
Europe after the war is delightfully naive:
“Supposing that the eight Great Powers,
together with the smaller developed Euro-
pean countries, could come to an agreement
for _ the equal admission of their trade and
capital to all colonial possessions, etc., not
merely would the gravest causes of future
antagonism be removed, but substantial new
bonds of community of international inter-
est would be provided.” He forgot to add —
at Australia’s expense. And though he has
to admit that the flooding of our markets
by untaxed imports fron l the whole of
Europe is at present barred by “the prefer-
ential tariffs of our self-governing domi-
nions,” lie cheekily asks whether that
obstacle is insuperable. It is.
The other essays range from the super-
fluous to the banal: and the miasma of stolid
dulness lies over the lot.
Australian
At last this persistent page has discovered
a cookery book that is willing to divulge the
secret of boiling an egg. This apparently
indispensable feat in cookery has hitherto
been overlooked by. author-cooks. But the
“Presbyterian” Cookery Book, the 14th edi-
tion, of which revised and enlarged, complet-
ing 200,000 copies, has been published by
Angus and Robertson for Is., has full infor-
mation on this important point, and
actually provides an index that tells
you where to find the recipe. “Put
the eggs into boiling water. Draw the
saucepan to the side of the fire, and do not
let the water boil again. In five or six
minutes the eggs will be done.” Quite simple,
after all, you see! Clara Kirkland, the hon.
editor for the Woman's Missionary Associa-
tion of the Scotchbyterian Church of N.S.W.,
regards it as significant that many discern-
ing women have made it a habit to give a
copy of this manual to every new bride of
their acquaintance. This cheaply solves
the awkward _ wedding-present problem.
Those many discerning women must have
been Scotch.
J*
Commonsense Hints on Plain Cookery,
compiled by the Cookery Teachers’ Associa-
tion of N.S.W. (Angus and Robertson, Is.),
is a kind of text-book of cookery, intended
as a guide to students in Domestic Science
Schools and “young home-makers.” It
is without recipes. Thus, it modestly re-
frains from telling you how to boil an egg,
but it informs you of the effect of heat on
eggs, the composition of eggs, and gives valu-
able information that will instruct you when
an egg is in the right state of decomposition
for use on election days. But it does give
nine rules for making tea. This work is
meant to be a companion to the Common-
sense Cookery Book.
J*
Lieut. Stupart must now rank among the
“best sellers” of the Commonwealth. He is
the author of innumerable military manuals
which explain with fuller detail various
departments of military instruction, or pro-
vide instruction in the new aspects of mili-
tary work rendered necessary by the new
tactics of this war. Since the war obligingly
broke out to increase the author’s sales, his
publishers, Angus and Robertson, Sydney,
have sold over 250,000 copies of his little
textbooks. _ Three new manuals are now
issued. Lieut. Stupart’s Hints to Young
Officers (Is. 6d.) is now in its third revised
and enlarged edition. It mentions those
minutice of etiquette and duties that most
young officers discover only by experience.
The curious development of trench-warfare
has led other experts to give the results of
their experience to the military public. Staff-
Sergt.-Major D. Fallon provides a compre-
hensive manual of How to Learn and In-
struct in Bayonet Fighting (Is. 6d.), with
55 illustrative photographs; and Lieut. G.
Dyson offers valuable information upon
Grenade Warfare (9d.).
J*
Norman Duncan’s Australian Byways
(Harper, New York, per Dymock’s Library)
is a readable American view of our conti-
nent, unfortunately illustrated by an Ameri-
can artist who quite fails to achieve any
suggestion of our characteristic flora, land-
scape or costume. The author came evi-
dently quite determined to discover Australia
and the Australians for the United States;
and, of course, he did not look for the typi-
cal Australian in the cities. Yet, what more
typical Australian could be found than our
present Prime Minister —a product of the
city? So Norman Duncan leisurely wanders
out west, on camel-back for preference, delib-
erately seeking out the more antiquated
methods of locomotion, scorning the motor
and the rail, and pursuing into the far in-
terior the last of the mail coaches. And
everywhere he chatted to the Australian, and
the kindly Australian told the stranger abo-
riginalities, and the stronger the yarn the
better Norman Duncan liked it. These yarns
make such excellent copy’ for the New York-
ers. And, after all, many of them are true.
However, Norman Duncan is a friendly
visitor: he is naturally proud of a continent
that, almost unaided, lie has discovered for
the United States. And he tries to be fair.
“Australia is a vast, inviting place: it
measures 422 miles more in area than the
United States of America proper.” And
well found is this yarn of our national in-
terest in the moke.
“ ‘There’s my beauty!’ said an Australian
stockbroker, displaying the photograph of a
sturdy little boy astride a slim horse.
“ ‘Fine boy,’ I agreed.
“‘Oli,’ said he, * that’s my son!’
“ ‘Fine horse,’ said I quickly.”
And, though naturally our visitor exag-
gerates the discomforts of our clime and the
danger of our lives, he can make amends by
a little common-sense.- “The traveller is
astonished, upon his return, to be told that
Australia has been written down by some as
an arid waste. It is an unjust and injuri-
ous fiction. Australia is preponderantly fer-
tile and rich, a pleasant country, with abun-
dantly generous rewards, growing all the
while more populous and rich ; and the dry
interior neither discomforts nor beggars the
aspiring and prosperous people who dwell in
the many favorable lands more than it
troubles the happiness of the inhabitants of
any other continent. The Australian dry-
lands, which narrow, year by year, as they
are better known, have been celebrated above
the wealthy places for the sensation they
afford —the hot winds, the burning days, the
stony deserts and waterless sandy tracts.”
Yet it is just these aspects of Aus-
tralia that this foreign author, intent on
interesting his own people, and being a good
journalist, has perforce to stress. But we
forgive him because: “All at once a shilling
copy of the Popular Verses of Henry Law-
son, that poet of the Australian bush, caught
us off our feet.”
-* • ■■
“Copabella”: What is it that confers the
title “Australian” on a poet?’ Must he be
born here, or need he only visit here, write
verses here or mention Australia in his
verse? The outstanding characteristic of
the Oxford professor’s list of our poets
(Red Page, 20/l/’l6) was their unknown-
ness to the average Australian. What Aus-
tralian ever heard of Parkes or Sladen,
Reeves or Gilbert White —as poets? Eng-
lish papers (and the “correct” Australian
dailies) always refer to Adam Lindsay
Gordon as the representative Australian
poet. AY hat constitutes representation?
Only a bare handful of A.L.G.’s verses
mention Australia and its features, and
even these are English of the English in
tone and thought. Perhaps their English-
ness makes them representative—smacking
as they do of “imported” culture, so differ-
ent from the vulgar Australian of The Sen-
timental Bloke* Pat MacDurmcr and The
Man from Ironbark. Representative of
whom —the professorial board, or Billjim of
Redgum Flat or Yarraville? This eternal
quoting of Gordon as the representative Aus-
tralian poet makes me tired. Perhaps I’m
a Philistine, hut I’ve read him and re-read
him, and never found him get within
coo-ee of the real Australian Bush—
and his sick stockrider’s reference to Jem
Hall always conjures up in my mind the
pinhead farm louts, Zam’l and Jake and
Goiles, drinking “four-half” and discussing
“Squire” and “Passon” in the taproom of
a wayside alehouse near Stow i’ th’ Wold !
Who is the representative Australian, any-
how —Bill or the professor? I never had
much truck with the latter, but I battled
among the selections, wheat farms and
shearing sheds of the Riverina for half a
dozen years, and never once did I hear
A.L.G. quoted (except unconsciously and
incorrectly in the very English “How We
Beat the Favorite”) ; while Henry Lawson,
Banjo Paterson, Brunton Stephens, with
some of Edward Dyson, Will Ogilvie and
Garnet Walcli, were household words and
recited and applauded at every bush
“shivoo.” But perhaps these aren’t poets
nor their verses literature. Contrariwise,
perhaps Billjim isn’t competent to judge
literature. In either of these cases we’d
have to classify our poets into popularly-
representative and literarily-representative
—with writers of hymns quoted by arch-
bishops in a class of their own!
London Times thanks Harry Lawson for
his book, My Army, O my Army!: —
The war has brought forth, in the Mother Coun-
try, a good deal of fine poetry in which the spirit of
the Empire speaks. We have met with nothing quite
like Mr. Lawson’s verses. They express the common
sense of the U&in man, as well as his faith and
hope. They keep their eye on the object; they will
appeal to people whom the loftier pitch of poetrv
may leave unaffected. Their long, qasy, galloping
measure carries the reader along like a good horse,
and the poems are full of color and of high spirit.
Mr. Lawson writes of Russia in a way that will
bring her nearer to many yet strangers.’ He chants
the doings of the Salvation Army. Also, he can
laugh meanwhile. He laughs at the comic side of
the activities which the war has called out, and his
laughter is the very surest proof that his admiration
is sincere. His is a convincing, an authentic, voice.
He has surrendered nothing 1 to the fashion or to the
craze of the hour, and his characteristic colonial
freedom and independence make his songs significant
and true.
The Way of the Cross
When, under the tremendous attack of a
Huns, the whole western wall of n . the
house fell in, and Russia decided to wf*
desert for the invader, no adequate - a
was made for the reception of the Sl- 10n
that, perforce, had to stream eastwnrd V6S
Russia. ‘‘The Russian tendency to Slav”?
delay fatally, always and in *
Russia innumerable lives. The 5
was, however, alleviated somewhat by 5!
work of pu '.lie relief organisations, the ,1
preceden cd mildness of the autumn at Z
time of the great migration, and the SUf JL
compulsory sobriety of the Russians “ d
The story of this extraordinary heiir, nf
fugitives is told with a marvellous vividJ
by V. Doroshevitch, whose account n rT
event is translated by Stephen traif the
The Way of the Cross (Constable; 2s Gdf
This is probably the first piece of RuS
whr literature to be translated into EnS
and the translation is brilliant. DoroSwS
is a famous Russian journalist whose iour-
nalistic style, as will be seen, is remarkable
Indeed, his style has captured the journalists
of Russia ; and this vivid, ejaculatory
must make the Russian newspapers eve
more interesting than,' say, The Sydke!
Mquninc Heealp It 4s to be remembered
too that Doroshevitch s articles and feuilk
tons are intended to be read aloud.
Doroshevitch went from Moscow to meet
the refugees, passed through it and brough
back The Way of the Cross.
J*
Have you been long on the road?
A tohole eternity!
J can’t make it out—said one of the
fugitives to me. He was serving as a
soldier.
I can’t make it out: either my home
■was all a dream, or I’ve gone out of my
mind now, and God knows when I shall
understand. My home burnt, cattle drowned
in the river, no little wife, she died on the
road; my two children also died, and ice
buried them by the wayside and put crosses
over the graves. I’ve got one son left and
one horse. That’s all we are in the world.
Is it possible that I am the same man that
I was?....
J*
What is in the carts?
You meet the very strangest cartloads.
They sometimes carry—layers of iron.
The roof!
It teas the most valued possession.
—Their cottage had. an iron roof,
When they were forced to flee they took
this most valued thing and carried it they
knew not whither.
Why?
It teas the most valuable...,
J*
Along this “Way of the Cross” takes
place:
- A selection.
A terrible “natural” selection.
All the weak ones perish.
Both of people and cattle....
Look, at the twenty-verst post of the road
gleams a white cross.
Three more crosses.
More still.
Still new ones, more new ones, yester-
day’s cemetery.
They are white, like little Georgian
crosses, crosses pinned to the much-suffering
road.
Just like Georgian orders:
For self-sacrifice.
“They” do the burying at nights. ...
In the daytime, at the stopping places, at
the relief and medical points, they
—■ conceal their corpses,
fearing that they may be delayed by for-
malities:
and remain behind!....
They carry out the corpses from the forest
where they hare spent the night and bring
them to the road.
They must bury them in a place where
the people pass by.
- Where man coming past, will cross him-
self and pray for the soul of the departed.
For you see, the dead have not had their due
singing and prayers as at a proper funeral
service. . . .
jt
I stop at one collection of graves, at a
second, at a third.
There are some which breathe horror.
Three, five, crosses, and on all:
The 17th of September.
■ —■ The 17th of September.
—• The 17th of September. _
In one day, all. In one night a cemetery
grew up....
Somebody has been buried.
Others see:
That it is a nice place. #
And lay their oivn dead with the others,
side by side. ..
And yet more come, and yet more.
the cemetery grows, stretching itself alO g
the margin of the road. .
And one reads the heart-breaking inscrip-
tions on the crosses:
Infant.
Infant.
Yes, truly, it is the province of Mogilaf-
Every three, every five versts, ana *
every two versts, and every verst, or >
crosses, crosses.
A continuous cemetery....
* “Mogila” is Russian for a grave.

rheumatism, gout, sciatica, gravel, stone or
pains in the bladder, muscles and joints are
all suffering these pains through weak or
diseased kidneys, for these are the sure and
certain symptoms of kidney trouble.
All these things mean that the kidneys are
weak and diseased. They need strengthening
and making healthy.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Scent Bazaar.
Burden of sweets on the cry-throng’d air;
Dazzling blue through the gaps o’erhead ;
Green-framed casement perching there
Glimpsing a languor on night-sweets fed.
Sleek beauty, ’tis meet your thought stray far
From your perfumed lair o’er the Scent
Bazaar.
Grey minaret, mellowed by Egypt’s sun,
Grace in your slender pose,
You. have stood through the cycles of years
that were run
Ere the syrups distilled from the Persian
rose
Were marketed round your hallowed mosque-
base !
The incense of worship to Allah ascends
With the prayers of the Faithful race;
And worshipping incense the phial lends,
Op’ning its wealth to the fair from afar,
Taking their toll of the Scent Bazaar.
Grey-beard vendor squat on his mat,
Drugged with the essence of myrrh—
The whirl of the world, what recks he of
that ?
Nothing he asks of her
But toilers, that Eastern flow’rs may blow,
And the price of perfumes, that more may
grow.
Beauty comes veiled and sits at his feet,
Breathing more wares than her purse can
buy ;
Leaning in ecstasy nearer to greet
Rich breath of attars, with sensuous cry ;
Black eyes shine tranced o’er the fair mouth’s
bar,
Drunken with sweets in the Scent Bazaar.
CAIRO.
GUNGA.
Fenced Off!
We used to come here of an evening
When all the bright west was aglow,
And life was a garden of roses —
A boy and a girl long ago.
Beneath us was movement and sparkle,
Or stillness and color and calm ;
And the breeze from the sea on our foreheads
Was sweet with a magical balm.
’Twas ever and ever a wonder
This cove, as, hand lying in hand,
We stood here and watched it together,
Its waters and sickle of sand.
We watched the brave lights on its bosom
Grow delicate, shadowed and faint —
A cameo such as no artist
But Nature the Master could paint.
Your hair was as brown as the bracken
That loses its green and grows old,
Yet hinting, when noon gives it lustre,
Of something more golden than gold.
Your eyes—did they dream of this moment,
When all is grown squalid and grey,
Of the scene that has passed from our vision,
The rock and the beach and the bay?
They have builded high walls and tall fences,
And outcasts and aliens are we;
The beauty that charmed us as children
No more ’tis our glory to see.
Where now are the calm and the color,
The waters that rippled and curled?
Was it well, was it right to deny us
This bit of God’s beautiful world?
n.s.w. j
RODERICE QUINN.
A Spring Morning.
Long ere the sun was fairly up,
While yet warm earth lay half adream,
I drank from morning’s glory-cup
And plunged all breathless in the stream.
Unwearied birds with dazzled wings
Flashed past and screalned triumphantly,
The happiest of created things;
I would I might as happy be I
Beside the silver margin heaves
A hill, where weed-buds shone amid
The welcome press of fallen leaves
And mossy turf and lichens hid.
Within the veins of every tree,
Within the heart that breathless sings,
Life’s green-fire leapt mysteriously
And stirred the deepest shadow:::~3.
All that had eyes might look and
The love-laws writ in Heaven’s Uv.e,
In simple signs so faithfully
E’en grief might understand them, too!
From haunts and hiding-holes of we; ,13
Wild creatures crawled to greet t’.c son
With dazzled eyes; by such fair meeds
He gladdens e’en the lowliest one!
In calm suburban gardens bees
Sipped cool wine-cups unhurriedly,
While blithe birds warbled melodies
Such as they sang in Arcady.
Amid the flames of languid blooms
Whose vivid fragrance dazzled one
Soft gauzy things fed on the fumes,
While spiders watched from webs new
spun.
There fair young mothers tended flowers,
And dreamed of glad, ungarnered years,
And with their babes passed hallowed hours
Too honey-sweet for sighs and tears!
If you had seen all that I saw.
Fair Cynic, you’d rejoice with me,
And understand God’s deathless law—
Love rules the world triumphantly !
N.s.w.
W. M. WHITNEY.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £1 6s. Gel. per annum you can have The
Bulletin sent regularly to you every week for a
year, including Special Christmas Number, post paid.

Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright . by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with, Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
6000 words, are specially acceptable ), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying i MS.

THURSDAY, MARCH 30, 1916.
A PROPOSAL TO REWARD MERIT
WITH CHAINS.
Now that Australia’s Prime Minister is
flooding London with torrents of patriotic
speech, and the editors of British news-
papers are leaping from their chairs to yell
“Hallelujah!” to his Imperialistic chorus,
it might be well, not to ask w r hat it is all
about, for that would be almost impious, but
to look at the things which are being
attached to the whirling periods of the little
man’s heroic rhodomontade. Chief among
these is the statement that a Constitution
must be framed at the close of the war which
will “hold the Empire closely together, giv-
ing the Dominions a definite voice in its
affairs.” Although W. M. Hughes has
denied making any remark about the
organisation of the Empire except to some-
body on one newspaper and a man who came
to interview him from another, it is just this
talk of “holding together” and giving a
“definite voice” and the like that, when
uttered by prominent Englishmen associated
with him on platforms, sets British patriots
rocking on their chairs, and causes the press
to explode in great bursts of inky admira-
tion for the man whose presence calls forth
such soul-uplifting sentiment. If Hughes
doesn’t actually say all that is attributed to
him in the reports of his speeches, he at
least sits still and endorses it with his
smile. But •what exactly does the Empire-
binding talk mean? Somewhere about 18S0
the defeated minority which represented
Tory thought in Britain invented the catch-
word of Imperial Federation as a set-off to
the policy of more progressive statesmen who
saw the strength of the British ideal to lie in
the granting of the widest possible liberties
to autonomous States, while sitting back and
allowing the bond of sympathy and senti-
ment between them to mellow and strengthen.
To the Tory mind there were immense dan-
gers in leaving free States to govern them-
selves. A new nation might easily break
away from its aged parent and even become
hostile and turn and rend her. What the
Tory wanted was a closer organisation which
would make the remotest part of the so-called
Empire an integral part of Britain —in fact,
under a mass of new and high-sounding
phrases, he wanted to reduce the self-govern-
ing British countries once more to something
like the Crown colonies from which they had
evolved. But Imperial Federation never
became more than a yell. Nobody really
wanted it. The new States and Dominions
overseas had the cocked hat of a Governor
here and there to remind them of the way
some of their ancestors used to dress, and
were content to move along their own paths,
doing their nation-building according to
their own notions, and casting a
kindly thought occasionally across the
ocean to the useful parent who, out
of. a large purse, bought most of the
things they raised from the soil. The
people of England had plenty of problems of
their own without tackling those that had
grown up. on the outside rim of the planet,
about which they knew nothing and cared
less. The people of the Dominions had
their special job to do, and had no sort of
desire to interfere in the domestib affairs of
an ancient, over-populated island at a remote
distance. So Imperial Federation began as
a yell and stayed on as a vague and unreal
murmur.
* * * *
Now, while W. M. Hughes shouts in a
frenzy of patriotic zeal about Anzac, the will
of the people, Labor ideals, German trade, the
Australian Light Horse, freight for wheat
and the coming victory of the Allies, all sorts
of people are pinning placards bearing the
decayed Tory cry on various parts of his
garments. The Dominions must be bound
closer to Britain! Why ? Because in the
extreme test of the greatest and most terrible
war of history the old loose union of senti-
ment and sympathy failed? No! This can-
not be. the reason because, on Hughes’s own
rhetorical showing, Australians and the
people of the other Dominions rose as one
shout when the test of battle arrived. They
could not possibly have done more whatever
the political relationship with Britain had
been; and the odds are that if there had
been any sort of pressure brought to bear
upon them, they would have done much less.
For the purposes of war, therefore, the
existing system of loose ends and warm
sentiment has worked perfectly; and any
closer association must be for some mysteri-
ous purpose connected with the coming time
of peace which has not yet been disclosed.
No statesman would be mad enough to run
the risk of changing an arrangement which
has so admirably stood the supreme test
except for some very grave consideration.
* * # *
This Commonwealth has already framed
something in the way of a big national pro-
gramme, based on certain ideals which are
held by every Australian who is worth his
bread and beef to his native land. The
same is true of Canada, of Maoriland, of
South Africa and even of little Newfound-
land ; not to speak of Ireland, with its joy
of coming emancipation fresh on its ver-
dant sod. These ideals and aspirations
of the units of the so-called Empire often
involve international questions, and are not
merely matters of local government. White
Australia, as a policy, involves other peoples
and touches world-wide interests; in New-
foundland the question of bait for the
fisheries is equally far-reaching and of acute
interest to at least one European nation.
In proposing that the bonds of Empire
should be drawn closer, is it to be under-
stood that all ideals held by the units of
that Empire are to be upheld by all the
rest, and that, come what will, Australia,
Ireland and South Africa will stand or fall
by the view the people of Newfoundland take
of bait to catch the elusive cod? Further,
as it is clear that the ideals of young
nations are in process of evolution, and
must continue to grow, ,is it to be taken for
gi anted that the future views of things held
by Australia, Maoriland and Canada must
forever be upheld, even to the death, by
South Africa and Britain? Such an
Imperial union is difficult enough to imagine;
but when it is remembered that in great
proposed partnership are included also qoun-
tries like India, Fiji, Jamaica and Egypt,
reason begins to reel at the thought of a
political machine which would attempt to
harmonise and make workable the external
and internal interests of the lot. Yet when
a statesman talks about “holding the Empire
together and giving the Dominions a definite
voice in its affairs,” he either means a con-
trolling Government affecting every Domin-
ion question of greater magnitude than the
working of the. parish pump, or he is merely
breathing hot and noisy air. As it is
inconceivable that such a Government could
adjust the affairs of all its federated States
without compromises which would lower or
obscure national ideals, it is clear that the
Prime Minister of the Commonwealth has
no right to countenance even by a boisterous
smile a proposal which would mean moral
loss and bitter disappointment to the people
of Australia.
* # * «
When in common with other British
nations we entered the present war, we did
so in the belief that our blow was to be
struck in the interests of small nations
trampled upon by big ones. Our wallop, if
ill-directed, was a hearty one; and good
•Australians poured out their blood freely
in the cause, while even very middling Aus-
tralians produced some cash to help the more
ardent patriots along. Viewing matters in
the. light of British oratory, Prime Minis-
terial smiles and London press comment, it
looks very much as if Australia, the small
nation fighting for the rights of other small
nations in Europe, is to be asked to sur-
render her own grip on those things which
give her nationality force and meaning. Any
organisation of the Empire, such as that now
suggested, must be more or less on a repre-
sentative. basis; and on at least one ques-
tion, which is absolutely vital to every Aus-
tralian, we stand to be outnumbered by
others who, for a thousand obvious reasons,
must be quite unable to understand our
feelings or get our point of view. To carrv
out any binding together which involved
such calamities as this would be fraught
with far more danger to the essential unity
of the English world than to leave things
as they are; with far more danger than
even to let the existing political ties slack
off further until the knots are as loose as
those on a suburban verandah blind.
* * * *
The plain fact is that, however much the
name “Empire” may be in evidence through
the influence of Kipping and other writers,
the British concern which spreads itself
over seven seas is not an empire in any real
sense, but a far bigger and worthier thing.
It is a confederation of nations, not bound
or fettered together, but one in splendid
unity through a sympathy which is far
stronger than any chain the loudest-talking
politician can forge. The proof of that
unity is found in the share we are taking
in the war —a war for the most part of free
nations against empires that are bound and
fettered together. While England remains
the first word in the language to the English-
man and Australia the first word to the
Australian, there is a very decent chance
that our essential unity will continue; but
once a serious attempt is made to load us
up with political ironmongery of however
ornamental a kind, the weight of the stuff
will cause chafing and irritation.
ONE MAN ONE VOTE AND SYDNEY’S
GARBAGE TIN.
In view of the Australian passion for pawn-
ing the family frying-pan and going on a
burst with the proceeds, The Bulletin eyes
with suspicion every suggestion for convert-
ing the baby’s perambulator into an article
pledgable by the man next door. Wherefore
this paper has only tempered enthusiasm for
that clause in the N. S. Wales Local Govern-
ment Bill which proposes to give every elec-
tor a vote on municipal and shire loan
proposals; for if municipal politics are going
to be further degraded by the frantic loan-
mongering and gross extravagances which
have become outstanding features of State
politics, settling day will be a time of wild
excitement. The best justification for the
clause is that Local Government in N. S.
Wales, and particularly in Sydney, has been
carried on without either imagination or
competence; that it has just muddled along
in an atmosphere of many odors to the great
discomfort and prejudice of the citizens; and
that a widening of the franchise and equalisa-
tion of voting values may give it life and
force and character. The worst that can be
said against the proposal is that the loan
and rating authority granted to citizens with-
out property may be abused. Probably it
will, for a time. The Bulletin expects to
see it abused. But as it has seen Propputy
abuse its privileges and neglect its obliga-
tions, the point at issue narrows down to the
simple problem whether Propputy can be
allowed to go fumbling on according to imme-
morial habit, or whether civic affairs shall
be controlled by the general body of citizens,
taking a risk of the latter being liable to
periodical fits of lunacy, but counting on
common-sense coming to the rescue. The
claim that Propputy has a monopoly of com-
mon-sense is fatuous. In national and local
affairs Propputy has always and everywhere
been a shirker. Otherwise it would not have
lost so much of its grip or have reason to be
squealing that it is threatened with the loss
of more. Neither would it abandon hope.
* * * *
Some of the horrors that are to be antici-
pated from an extension of the municipal
franchise in Sydnev. and from clauses in the
Bill authorising municipalities to engage in
trading operations, have been pictured vividly
by the newspapers. One pf them howls in
dismay about the losses that will have
to be faced on account of the aldermen
setting up in business as purveyors of milk
and retailers of crayfish, and it pictures the
terrible possibility of the Lord Mayor order-
ing someone into a citizen’s yard to orna-
ment it with a tar pavement. This agitated
waving of a demoralised gamp is utterly
useless, even if it were serious. What the
Bill does is to give municipalities authority
to enter upon certain trading undertakings,
and among them are these: —
The manufacture of gas and electricity, the genera-
tion of hydraulic power, the establishment of a ferry-
service, the conveyance of passengers by motor-’bus,
the transport of merchandise, the sale of milk, stone
and sand, the manufacture of bricks and pipes, and
the sale of fuel, meat, fruit, dairy produce, forage,
provisions, clothing, building material, biscuits and
pastry, fish and oysters.
Other expected horrors have been set forth
by traders who have passed resolutions of
alarm that they are to be deprived of their
livelihood through municipal copipetition and
compelled to pay taxes to mdke good the
losses contracted by mismanaged public
undertakings. There is, however, nothing
extraordinary in the list of enterprises which
municipalities are to be allowed to embark
upon. Most of them are somewhere or other
in full operation under municipal control,
and the rest ought to be. All over the world
municipalities are seeking wider powers in
this direction, and experience is proving the
expediency of granting them. In many places
it is only through municipal intervention
that a decent milk supply has been secured.
In others such a thing cannot be hoped for
except through the same agency. Only an
irreconcilable stick-in-the-mud could object to
the principle of municipal depots for certain
foods. . Screams of anguish from traders do
not affect the need for them. The trouble
with the average shopkeeper is that he can-
not purge himself of the notion that in addi-
tion to owning his shop and his goods lie
has a first mortgage on the public, with an
indefeasible title to profit from the necessities
of his neighbor.
If the Sydney municipality has -an un-
restricted franchise, some people are whim-
peiing that it must inevitably make a dismal
mess of whatever it touches and then impose
heavy burdens on property to replenish the
leaking caslibox. Were they to take an
active personal interest in civic affairs and
help to prevent these disasters, thev would be
discharging a useful function. The only
reason why hash has been made of local
government undertakings thus far is that the
privileged people have selected as their re-
pi esentatives only those men who are incom-
petent or worse. In many ways the local gov-
ernment of Australiancities has been less effici-
ent and enterprising than that of nnv «
white part of the British dominions Vi 1 * 1 '
with its dirty, narrow streets anri li hjdne - v
suburbs and abominable roads and W gg lng

PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Coming Demand for the Non-
striking Nigger.
In a general sort of way it may be said that
anybody in Australia who downs tool
and goes on strike north of the twenty-eighth
parallel is practically inflating his luna,
yelling at the top of hi s vLe LTbC
' abo !'- « J . K ' , st !' lke ' as » weapon in 2
hands of the toiler, may have its uses and
there probably are occasions on which it ran
be legitimately employed ; but in fully 87
out of 88 it is the wrong tool for the work
and as dangerous to those who handle it
and to the community at large as a loaded
two-edged, poisoned dagger with a lively
death-adder twined round the hilt The
White Australia policy, which five or six
years ago seemed to have dug itself in for
all time on a height of moral and national
sentiment, has begun to quiver. At least
one prominent newspaper, which stood firmiv
on the principle of Australia’s jobs for the
white man, has gone over to the enemy and
now pleads with tears in its ink for nigger
and still more nigger to develop the Northern
Territory. The situation in Queensland was
the real strength of the whole argument for
White Australia. Queensland had given
discolored labor an exhaustive trial and
thrown it aside on the ground that white
labor was cheaper. In tropical Australia
the white man proved that he could work so
much better than brown brother that,
although his wages were high and those of the
cocoa-tinted person small, his employer came
out a lot to the good. But —and the but at
this moment looms gigantic—the white man
is only cheaper while he goes on with his
work. By constantly downing his tools on
the smallest provocation, or none at all, he
is becoming too expensive a luxury for even
tropic wealth to support. Hence there is
a renewed cry—faint at present, but rapidly
gaining in volume —for colored labor. The
white man is failing to come up to expecta-
tions, not because he has become an inferior
workman, but because the strike and the
slow-up habit is making him uncertain.
Further, the cry for brown aid is no longer
centred on a desire to bring back the Kanaka.
The new demand will be for workers from
Asia, whose presence in Australia would be
not merely a menace to national ideals, bat
would forever make the mention of the White
Australia policy an occasion for wild laugh-
ter. The unionist may well give the matter
a little serious thinking, and consider whether
it might not be better to stick to his job, use
the legitimate means at his hand to improve
his position, and refrain from further
attempts to cut his own throat with a tar-
brush. A tar-brush is a very sharp instru-
ment when its handle is a strike and its
business end a cry for nigger.

The Call for Patience.
When General Focii broke the alignment
of the Hun’s western invading armies and
compelled old Yon Ivluck to get on the
reverse gear and scuttle over the Marne,
there were loud assurances that the decisive
moment had arrived and Tipperary was only
round the corner. Since then the decisive
moment has turned up about 50 times and
the last phase has been entered on a multi-
tude of occasions; yet nothing decisive or
remotely resembling it has happened any-
where, and the only difference between
yesterday and yesteryear was that yesterday
there was less muddle and more corpse, less
blatancy, and more holes taken up in the
combatants’ waist-belts. And that is about
where the disturbance stands, the thing
wdiich matters most being that the job
ahead has been brought into . better focus
and measured up for what it really is.
Germanic illusions have vanished. On the
other side delusions about the picturesque
effects that were to follow in the track ot
“steam rollers” and offensives in the spring
have evaporated. The struggle may be near
an end. It may possibly last for years.
What its duration will be no one can tell.
What looks reasonably certain is that brains
and patience will decide the matter. Hj®
German had both of these things, and is
fighting to-day with 40 years of dogged pre-
paration behind him. But it was a prepara-
tion for hacking through and winning
quickly bv sheer weight of men and metal.
That much is known. What is not known
is how long the patience of a dog will endur
when it unexpectedly finds itself tangieo
up in a prolonged fight with another anuna
and is compelled to devour its own tail a
the same time. That proposition may.bay
escaped the patient German mind. If
the German mind is in process of e P*
ment. It may respond by producing sj ;
thetic tails. If it can’t do that, it can on
win by crippling the British Fleet a
recovering a measure of maritime power
commercial purposes.

Sawbones.
Frantic attempts are being made by medi
cal men to convict N.S.W. Minister
Education Griffith of an inconsistent a
tilde towards unions of workmen ana
union of doctors. Confronted by a t
of boycott towards his scheme for sec r
medical treatment in schools, Griffit . -
in turn threatened the Medical Associ
to strike off the roll of practitioners •
member of the profession who refuses
with a State medical officer when cal j
Prior to this the Association had exp
its intention to ostracise any
took employment under Griffith s ■_
the association’s attitude being hostii


medical man treating for State pay any
child exceot “a pauper.” The argument
levelled at Griffith now is that he is an
advocate of “free labor,” yet would not dare
attack a striking union of miners or bakers
by engaging strike-breakers. The circum-
stances are not parallel, and can only be
made so by assuming the miners and bakers
to have gone upon strike to assert the prin-
ciple that the State shall not set up a coal-
yard or establish a bakery except to supply
customers who are penniless. The Medical
Association is not threatening its retali-
atory strike so that it may get an increase
of fees, but because Griffith declines to
introduce a test of pauperism into the
national, schools —an almost inconceivable
Kosition which, if conceded, would lead
ously to the further contention that
only pauper children should be received at
the schools for instruction. But if there
is nothing to be said for the attitude of the
profession, there is ground for doubt whether
the Griffith scheme is altogether without
flaw. The patient has to be considered as
well as Sawbones, and probably all would
be more fairly treated under a scheme some-
what akin to that adopted by lodges—one
which would permit of treatment being
available at the State’s expense from the
general body of medical practitioners on a
scale fixed by agreement. There would thus
be a reasonable choice with regard to the
adviser consulted, a greater degree of effici-
ency, and an equitable distribution of oppor-
tunity to participate in the nationalised
part of the profession. Griffith has a
fine ideal in view* and ought to pursue it.
But it is one that may be easily spoiled,
and this paper has an instinctive distrust
if any scheme launched on the basis of three
or four medical men and thousands of chil-
dren. Treatment on the basis of spot
diagnosis is not what a Socialist reformer
should aim at, nor are the foundations of a
building ever very satisfactory when the
architect and workmen spend most of* their
time throwing bricks at one another.

Trade—After the Ball.
Tiik Short of llans Bull -
A idt Of hot-ail is gushing froiri British and other
poiitiOiahs On the subject of cutting the German out
of the Empite after the war. The factors to be
reckoned with most are the British manufacturer and
trader; whose patriotism and intelligence may be
expressed algebraically by the minus sign.
Lest anyone should rise in haste and wrath to say
that my name is Schneider and my favorite beverage
sauer-kraut, I shall quote the British Ambassador at
Petrograd (Sir Geo. Buchanan) when addressing the
members of the British colony there about two
months ago. The diplomat with the good Scotch-
whisky name urged the necessity of at once preparing
to capture German trade in Russia. But he said: “If
we are to oust the Austro-Germans our manufacturers
and merchants must radically change their methods,
study local requirements and tastes, add abandon the
old-fashioned idea that what an Englishman likes
ought to be good enough for the foreigner.” But
will the British manufacturer and merchant get a
mental and physical wriggle on? Or will they seek
to trade on the war itself, and the sentiment that
blood is thicker than water—a peculiarity which also
applies to mud and is wholly irrelevant?
So much for sense; now for patriotism. The
Buenos Aires correspondent of the Times, telegraph-
ing about the middle of January, stated that “while
German imports may have disappeared from the
official Argentine returns, the fact remained that,
with sOthe unimportant exceptions, German firms
were still able to accept large orders, with guaran-
tees Of normal delivery;’' and were able even to
circularise British houses with Offers to supply large
quantities, at pre-war prices, of the very articles in
which these houses are their chief competitors.” The
German traders stocks were received from various
sources; by direct shipments from British firms : and
from Sweden and Holland duly fortified by consular
certificates of origin.
This enemy-trading, I suppose, is not antagonistic
to the ethics of Freetrade, literally interpreted. The
fundamental article of the gospel is to get goods
m the cheapest market and to sell them to the best
advantage of the seller. Nationalism, factory laws
and so on are merely so much impedimenta.
Invention and Defence.
The real centre of battle preparation for the
future must be the laboratory and the in-
ventor’s experimenting table. The military
person is not naturally progressive unless
there is a national whip behind him, and his
assurances in. time of peace have generally
been falsified in the actual clash of combat;
but one idea must be clear to the dullest per-
son who ever put on a uniform —that a single
transcendent invention might place tremen-
dous power in the hands of a comparatively
small people. There have been some really
gi’eat military inventions perfected within
the last 30 years or so; but in few cases
<hd a nation preserve the secret of the thing
it discovered for its exclusive use. The
wonderful French 75 gun appears to be an
exception; but the aeroplane, the torpedo,
the automatic machine-gun, the submarine,
wireless and the like have become common
property. Yet the great game of national
defence must be poorly played when the
national ideas, crystallised in invention and
construction, are not preserved as a national
secret. At the present moment Australia’s
hrst business is to aid in the defeat of the
Germans; and any marvellous thing pro-
duced by Australian brains should, of course,
be freely used for that purpose. But when
the war-cloud blows past, home defence
must become more important than the
work of expeditionary forces abroad. For
that, reason our own inventions require
\° taken seriously and made the most of.
Anything of real defence-value produced by'
Australian brains must be worth more to the
Commonwealth than to anybody else, and
should be paid for accordingly. The strength
°L su . c h inventions lies, not merely in their
enectiveness, but in the fact that they are
unknown to any possible enemy. Hitherto
they have generally been hawked from
land to land and offered to the highest
bidder, or even shown side by sicje with har-
vesters and churns at international exhibi-
tions. By encouraging research and reserv-
ing for our own use our - own discoveries,
Australia might get a long lead in military
preparation with little fuss and a very small
amount of show. Taking it as axiomatic
that most nations will continue in their
belief that boots are the first element in
war, the future for Australia should look
good if this country elevates brains to the
top place.
The Beam and the Mote in the Other
Fellow’s Eye.
Just about the moment a little man in
London was wildly demanding to know
whether John Bull was going to supply
himself with sugar from “the Empire” or
resume shipments from Hamburg, a vessel .
put into Sydney with 6000 tons of Japanese
sweetenina- grown at Formosa. About a
week before that a steamer had waddled in
with 7000 tons from Peru. A week or two
hence there will be other steamers from the
same directions —all of which is not particu-
larly convincing evidence against The
Bulletin’s theory that there was more im-
portant work for Hughes to do in Australia
than at the othpr end of the world. In the
circumstances there is something almost
grotesque about his yelp at the Britisher for
consuming a foreigner’s sugar. Hughes
knows that the country he represents at
frequent meals is doing the same thing and
always has done it. As between Britain
and Australia the greater folly is on the part
of the latter, a debby country. Britain at
least has the excuse that she is a creditor
aud a carrier and does not have to pawn her
socks to pay the bill. When the day comes
that an Australian politician can go to a
banquet in London and, instead of blithering
about what Mother should do, is able to say
what his own country does, it will be an
indication that for some years previously
Prime Ministers have been attending to their
real business. When the best that he can do
is to find fault with Mother for doing with
some justification what Australia has been
doing with no real excuse at all, the only
possible conclusion is that Australian Prime
Ministers have been engaged at blather and
shirk.

Muddle, Muddle, Muddle.
“liiMU” on the embroidery of war work :
Every now and again a wailing rises out of the
desert from some Australian who has been handed a
gross of pipes and no tobacco ; and vague tales from
the camp and plain curses from the depot suggest
that muddle continues to put a fine edge on its work.
Every week or two a commissioner is sent by the Blue
Socks Fund or some other association to inquire and
report, or to get busy before reporting and without
instituting inquiries; but the wail still rises from the
desert and the confusion appears to deepen amid the
pyramids. The impression the public has received is
that out of the various funds so willingly subscribed
by Australians, and of all the work done by enthusi-
asts in the big cause, far too large a percentage
glides away into the garbage-tin of utter waste
through incompetent or inefficient handling. The
war may or may not be at the turning point, but,
on all the evidence, it is going to last a good long
while yet; and national economy is as necessary in
those matters which may be described as the em-
broidery of the campaign as in things which pertain
to the plain, red stoush itself. What seems to be
most needed is a system which will bring the whole
of the output of good feeling and big-heartedness
under one command. It is a case where a single
truck is worth more than 27 wheelbarrows. A single
bureau of distribution might be established by the
Commonwealth; and though some of the muddle
might remain, the responsible paid heads of the con-
cern would be there to answer for their sins; and
Billjim would know the right people to hang at the
finish.
—♦
The Voluntary Workers’ Association of N. S.
Wales lias a sound understanding of the
facts. The public quickly forgets. The only
after-reward that soldiers used to get for
service for England was a general permis-
sion to beg without being prosecuted. Even
so late as the Boer War costly litigation
was necessary before returned soldiers could
get the back pay they were entitled to. The
Voluntary Workers aim at providing the
means to do justice by grabbing those means
while they are the most easily caught. Their
first desire is to provide homes for widows
and wounded; and from past experience
they know that if homes are not provided
now they are not likely to be provided at
all. Hence the Voluntary Workers’ Associa-
tion, whose membership is open to all who
will give eight hours’ service or 10s. in cash.
W. G. Layton, Deputy Town Clerk of Syd-
ney, is the hon. sec.; Wm. Brooks and
Town Clerk Nesbitt are the treasurers.
Anyone who wants to do his little bit for
those who are doing their bigger bit should
communicate with one of that trinity.

covered that people are more willing to put
money into picture shows than woollen mills-;
and he can’t get his soldiers clothed. This
means, he sententiously says, that the Gov-
ernment, in peace as well as in war, will
have to insist on having a voice as to the
way capital is employed. These place-
hunting, party politicians seem quite hope-
less. Any man of business could tell
Pearce that, all along, the Government has
had a voice in. deciding how capital should
be employed. Picture shows have paid,
because you can’t import your sixpenn’orth
of picture show —you have to buy it on the
spot. But Pearce and his Freetrade leader
Hughes (in the past “a Freetrader of the
Freetraders,” he declared the other day)
have inflicted upon Australia such a-freak
of a tariff that woollen mills have been
starved to the point of death. If Pearce
doesn’t know that let him ask somebody for
the history of the Queensland Woollen Co.,
which had to write off half its capital as
lost not so long ago. Pearce, by the way,
was in office at the time.

Seeing that Asquith, Lloyd George and
six or seven other members of the present
British Govt, are lawyers by profession, and
that some of them have replaced other law-
yers who dropped out or were pushed out,
the ruling class of politicians ought to be
truly grateful for the vigorous tongue-work
of William Hughes at this juncture. All
people and press organs in Britain were
blaming, blaspheming or politely chiding the
local lawyer-ridden Government when
learned brother Billy crossed the seas
to be greeted as a Samson, a “bombshell.” a
“crusader,” or other exponent of the pur-
poseful activity in which the old crowd of
ex-pacificists are so lacking. Thus Hughes
will be turned to account as a restorative of
public confidence in “legal acumen” in Im-
perial politics. Ilis legal diploma will be
loudly mentioned, whilst the fact that he
never was a lawyer in the popular under-
standing of the term will be carefully sup-
pressed. At the next general election in
Britain when the “No Lawyers!” cry is
heard in the land, every barrister candidate
will exhort the electors to remember Attor-
ney-General Hughes from Australia and
put their trust in brains that are devel-
oped by study of the statutes. Deakin and
George Reid will no doubt be men-
tioned also; but the most frequent apology
for the system of government by professional
sophists will be W. M. Hughes. After all.
it might have been better for Britain as well
as for ourselves if we had kept our lawyer
Prime Minister at home.
The Defence Department has at last pro-
duced an instruction to the effect that, when
good athletes and men with higherAlian-aver-
age qualifications offer for active service,
they are not to be rejected because of “teeth”
or minor disabilities. Over a year ago the
case of a champion rifle-shot rejected because
of ingrowing whiskers or something was the
standard jest, and the Department com-
menced to revolve slowly on its axis to meet
such instances. Now it has accomplished the
stupendous job and a lot of first-class
material that was previously urged to get
back to the playing fields will find a welcome
at the depot.
There is a popular fallacy that it was the
present Kaiser who discovered the German
nationality of Almighty God, and another
lesser fallacy that William 11. in
talking that way echoes the pious
self-congratulations of grandfather William
1., the blustering old hypocrite with
the mutton-chop whiskers. As a mat-
ter of fact, it was Bismarck who educated
both old William and his grandson in the
belief that Jehovah is a Hohenzollern and
a half-partner in the German Empire busi-
ness. As long ago as 1847 Bismarck said
in the Prussian Parliament: “The Prussian
sovereigns are in possesssion of a crown, not
by the grace of the people but by God’s
grace.” And in the anecdotal book —“Bis-
marck and His People”—the blood and
iron immoralist is stated to have said : “Take
away my connection with and relationship
to God, and I should pack up to-morrow and
return to sow oats at Varzin.” As Chan-
cellor of the German Empire the old chap
professed to reckon himself in close touch
with the Almighty. The present Kaiser
claims nothing that his old mentor didn’t
tell him he had inherited. As for his talk
about “Kultur,” the term was coined by
Professor Virchow 40-odd years ago, when
the clerical party was fighting the Govern-
ment on the question of the exercise of R.C.
church functions by priests who refused to
acknowledge civil authority. Virchow
called it the “Ivulturkampf,” or “Culture-
contest,” involving Germany’s “entire intel-
lectual and moral culture.” So the Kaiser
proclaimed the fact that the present war
would be a “Ivulturkampf” between Ger-
many and the natural enemies of her poli-
tical and moral culture. Which it certainly
is. But the notion that Germany has got a
Kultur inspired by God didn’t originate in
the Kaiser’s brain. He is not an author of
national “gags.” He is only the glorified
mummer who stands in the centre of the
stage and utters them.
One of the changes introduced by General
Robertson, ex-private, when he relieved
Kitchener as Chief of the General Staff,
was in regard to the method of finding offi-
cers. Robertson aims at promoting the
ranker, and has been drafting men of promise
into the instruction schools. Kitchener
clung to the older notion right up to the last.
It looks to this paper as if Australia is
swinging uncertainly between the two ideals,
and that it would be well if the Defence
Department kept more closely to the practice
of working the lift from the ground level.
Hitherto the contrivance has appeared to
start rather too frequently from the first and
second floors. The position seems to be
that by waiting long enough and getting in
at the right place, practically any passable
candidate can quant> for a commission with-
out having to go through more than a pre-
tence of the pick and shovel stage. In
practice this often means offering special
advantages to the young man who is not in
a hurry to get away, while discounting the
chances of the more eager and vigorous indi-
vidual who wants to be supplied with a gun
on the spot. If this eager and vigorous
person doesn’t apply for training for a com-
mission he, as a rule, doesn’t get it: no mat-
ter how good an officer he might make he
isn’t called out and passed into the training
school. The Bulletin knows that much
is being done in the way of training rankers
for positions of command, but far too much
is left to the chance of men applying for
training, and too little to direct selection on
merit and promise. Thus many commissions
have been granted which would certainly not
have been granted if the only passports were
efficient service in the ranks, personality and
eagerness to get away to the trouble.
“Trot” : There were eight men around it
when I arrived —six sitting down, roll-
inn' up his sleeves, the other deliberately
filling it. Then the filler sat on his heels
and lit his pipe, and the sleeve-rolling having
finished, it was wheeled off. It was a barrow
with dirt in it. The equipage was pushed
a couple of hundred yards to a hole, where
it was taken delivery of by three other men,
the contents emptied into the hole and the
touring vehicle placed on its wheels and
legs in front of its conductor. “Well, so-
long,” he remarked, and with dignified steps
pushed it back to the waiting seven and
sat down. Two of the seven upended them-
selves. One started rolling his sleeves;
another grasped a shovel, took his stand at
the dirt and lifted a bit into the waiting
limousine. Whereupon the blades were
seized again, and the wheel revolved towards
the hole where the three specialists were
waiting. That was at nine o’clock, and
they were toiling at the same job at 4 p.m.—-
10 men, one barrow, a face of dirt, and a hole
200 yards or so away. It was good, honest
toil, and each man wiped the sweat from his
brow when it was necessary. Where was
it? On the banks of where the Cotter River
runs when there is any water in it. Doing?
Why, building the National Capital of Aus-
tralia. And the job was being paid for by
a Government which is preaching economy
because it has on hand a desperate war for
the very existence of the nation.
“Quidam” : One of the outstanding lessons
of the war so far has been that it is possible
to bring up a nation on wrong ideas; and
one of the wrong ideas most prevalent is
that a Britisher is superior to anybody else
simply because he is a Britisher, and that
in consequence there is a sort of innate
treachery and dreadfulness concealed in
the fact that so many Germans are in good
positions and businesses in Australia. Hence
to a great extent arises the howl to drag
them- forth. If every Greek fish-shop win-
dow were broken, and everyone bearing a ']
German name were interned, material damage
would be done to the Commonwealth’s own
productive powers; but if every Australian
became seized with the desire to ascertain
the reason of the foreigners’ success, and to
see that in future he was qualified to win
such success himself, no damage would be
Guns.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Shaped of His iron, got of His Earth,
Men that He made gave us being and birthj
Many have died thereby. Hear of our worth!
Guns!
Trench-mortar, howitzer, seventy-five,
Hear us, the blatant, implacable ones,
\Ve the black bullies that deafen and drive,
Batter and flatten, and shatter and raze
Wall and emplacement, battalion and horse,
We the incarnadined Godhead of Force,
We the new-diademed Ancient of Days!
Down the red quivering lanes that we break
Bun, little men, for your sweet women’s
sake!
Little pale hearts in your far-away homes
Flutter and wait! Feradventure he comes,
Or, peradventure, the eyes that you kiss
Nightly, in dreaming, stare dreadfully up
Into the shifting, smoke-garlanded vault;
Should he win through, lay you never the
fault
Here at the muzzle of Creusot or Krupp !
One thing our pride and our pleasuring is—
Life-breakers, Love-breakers; all the years
give
Tear we to bloodied shreds; hence do we
live—
Guns!
Down in the veins of men
Berserker battle-thirst,
Rancorous greed of gold,
Land-hunger, treaty-scorn
{All that ice knew of old—
Aye, and shall know again),
Strained at the dam and burst,
Thus were tee born!
Guns!
White, clenching fingers, what are you to us?
Orisons pattered for lovers or sons
Drown in the din, while we answer you thus,
Heaping, where earth and trajectory meet,
Ingles and ricks and disintegrate roofs.
Grey, stupid faces, and wild, flying hoofs,
Coursing a million small twinkling feet.
Beating red morsels you knew for your men
Into the earth, that shall bear them again !
(Here gapes the shrapnel-mark, lividly-blue.
There the raw gash where the splinter delved
through !)
Masters, our masters, with infinite pain
Longtime you garnered your corn and your
oil,
Toiled with the hammer and tossed with the
spade
Till, at your bidding, we bondmen you made
(See you. sweet masters, sweet madmen, the
spoil?)
Trampled your garner to raffle again.!
M en-killers made you us; trust, and fear
hot
That we baulk your behest by the veriest jot.
Guns!
Crowning your one God
With psalms and with groans,
Sowing your one sod
With millet and’bones.
Guarding your swaddled life,
Tendon and lissom limb,
Caring, then casting it
Stark to the thirsty knife —
What would you, then?
Certes, you puzzle us!
(Haply you puzzle Him!)
Mad little composite,
Strange little men!
When our last bellowing thins on the air,
When your rash humor (in cycles it runs)
Slurs down from riot to peace—and
despair—
Whiles that you stoop, in a small sorry
day,
Plucking the scant thrusting wheat-ears
that prise
Through skull-holes that once were men’s
arrogant eyes.
Then shall you perjure—’twas ever your
way!—
Scratching your signatures, sealing them
* fair,
Each in its turn, on a clerkly white square;
"Leasing shall cease, and our iron shall
come
Henceforth to the sickle, our lead to the
plumb !”
When you have cast us, wherein was your
trust.
Shamed to the dust that is built of your
dead,
Skoda, and field-gun. and seventy-five.
Dainty machine-gun that runs on a sled,
Watching you chaffer, and prosper, and
wive,
All shall laugh silently, down in the rust
That roughens the throats of us, knowing,
O men,
We are voiceless for aye— till you need us
again!
* * * *
Slayers, we sing to you! Masters, be sure
You shall dance to our music ichile life
shall endure.
Guns!
N.S.W.
J. ALEX. ALLAN.

HIGHEST AWARDS AT ALL THE GREAT EXHIBITIONS. 2
done to anyone or anything but the alien.
The war has shown us that, owing to slip-
shod methods and archaic ideas, we have
allowed the foreigner to become supreme in
many things requiring systematic knowledge.
It has shown, too, that our boasted liberty
may be made into a fetich, till it degenerates
into a license under which the commercial
magnate thinks only of enhanced profit, and
the wage-earner of nothing but an extra
bob a day. It has also shown a serious dis-
crepancy. The former creed of the ultra-
British press and politicians was that Brit-
ish rule was so superior to German rule that
no German who once escaped from the latter
would ever go back; now the same people
assert that every German, even unto the
second and third generation, would if pos-
sible bring about German rule in Australia.
Discrepancies like this should suggest that
it is urgently necessary for the nation to get
out of the habit of allowing the daily press
to do its thinking. Nothing is less likely to
lead to national improvement than a smug
belief that we can’t be matched in anything.
“Yin” : Re the awful Egyptian Flossie.
Some of our doctors tackled the problem in
a very practical way. Outside one A.M.C.
tent was a notice reminding men going on
leave that goods could be purchased “abso-
lutely proof against venereal disease.” The
supply gave out in record time. Flo is so
persistent that she comes into the cafes to
leave her cards and photos on the tables, and
when she’s at home it’s common to see her
exhibiting her nakedness in the window or
on the doorstep. There’s a terrible reckon-
ing coming from the Poison Woman of
Pharaoh’s aucient land. Still, Australia
can’t expect Minister for Defence Pearce
to attend to it while the Band of Hope keeps
him busy in Melbourne. Nor Prime Mini-
ster Hughes, who is fully occupied having
his leg pulled in the dear old Mother Land.
Nor anybody else in authority who might
bury his faugs in father Bull and hold on
until that neglectful parent removes the
pestilence in Egypt to save the sons of a
young nation that is bleeding copiously in
his defence.
News has come of one of Siiackleton’s
ships, the Aurora; she has emerged from the
ice in a damaged condition, and there is un-
certainty about the whereabouts of 10 per-
sons, including the captain, who were on
shore when she was driven to sea. Also,
there is great lack of information about what
lias happened to Shackleton. Ilis last-
known address —in 1914 —was c/o the Endur-
ance, away round the corner south of the
Horn; and, as he contemplated waiting for
another • season before setting out to walk
over to where the Aurora was going to be
but isn’t, the problem of whether he is now
where he was before or somewhere else is
about as plain as everything else. The cer-
tain thing is that there is a cry for a relief
ship, and someone will have to find the cash.
This mania which some newspapers and
societies have for inciting expeditions to go
and do something that doesn’t matter tup-
pence urgently requires jumping on. Unless
the South Polar region is put out of bounds
it may happen that the financial resources
of Britain and Australia will be exhausted
fitting out (1) ships to go and discover how cold
it is down yonder and (2) more ships to hurry
up and bring back the details. It is one-
part foolishness and waste and the other
part love of advertisement and adventure,
with a very thin slice of scientific zeal
squeezed in between.
The pending capitulation of General Frost
on the frontiers east and west is leading to
severe disturbances from Riga to the Buko-
vina, and though only the most vague
accounts of the lighting are available the
broad results appear to be that the Bear
has had fair success at the southern
extremity of his line and is at the old
address further north. Soon bigger things
will be happening thereabouts, and as
lhe pot is beginning to boil around Salonika
and the mud is drying up in Flanders a
renewal of the uproar all round seems about
due, with the German frantically struggling
to roll his encircling enemies flat. Conse-
quently the veil of mystery which has lately
been thrown about the Australian troops is
likely to be drawn away before long, and
we may hear something definite regarding
their performances in new fields. For the
present Salonika remains a riddle; the
campaign in Mesopotamia mostly a blank.
The only thing of which we have at all
definite news is the revival of German sub-
marine activity. The revised policy of the
Hun appears to be to blow up everything at
sight—which seems to suggest that the
British blockade is hurting. Of course the
new outbreak of mania will be annoying.
It will also be costly. But it won’t make it
a bit easier for Germany to get the things
it wants from outside : and if we can shut
those things out and keep the wild wolf in,
the end must be victory. But the “if” in the
case means time and patience, men and
money.

Quite apart from the heartening influence
they have, the “Britain Prepared” films,
now showing at Sydney Town Hall, are fine
examples of artistic motion-photography.
Yards of the film could be snipped off the
reels and used, inch by inch, as full-page
pictures in any illustrated paper. There
are fleeting visions of fast cruisers burying
their sharp noses in the dark North Sea,
with Rembrandt shadows in the background
and submarines cheerfully swallowing steel
cigars at their base. Jellicoe’s bulldog
xvaddles across the screen and is loudly
cheered, while his invaluable owner seems
chiefly concerned in dodging the machine.
Mine-sweepers, yoked in pairs by their nets,
steam along in company doing their marine-
housemaid duty, and the lieutenant in the
innards of a submarine glues his eye to the
base of a periscope which he swings round
with two handles. It’s interesting to see
girl munition-workers dropping the bullets
into shrapnel shells as pleasantly as they
would shake plums into puddings; but the
better end of the show is the wild one with
salt in it.
II Sydney wants to see good modern drama
(which is doubtful), by an adequate oast
(which is dubious), well produced (which, is
open to discussion), and in an intimate
theatre (which is controvertible), then the
season begun by Frederick Ward at the
Little Theatre on Saturday evening has most
of the essentials of success. On its merits
the performance of “The Second Mrs. Tan-
queray” warrants support. The company is
an all-round one, and the weaknesses in it
are either inessential or counterbalanced by
excellencies. Eily Malyon’s Mrs. Tan-
queray is a triumph of intellect over tem-
perament. This has hitherto been regarded
as a Mrs. Pat Campbell part, for which
extremely temperamental and emotional
actress it was, indeed, written. P»ut Eily
Maly on shows another, and just as credible,
Paula —a less imposing but more ladylike
1 aula, nevertheless a Paula capable of every
emotion in that tempestuous lady’s gamut.
In the “big” scenes with which the play is
studded, she is remarkably effective. Aubrey
Tanqueray was probably supplied by Pinero
solely as a foil, or a butt, for Paula. But
the genius of George Titheradge made it of
equal theatric value to that of the tempes-
tuous and somewhat trying wife. Herbert
J. Bentley plays the part well —probably as
well as Pinero wanted it played. The chief
failure is that of Frederick Ward as Cayley
Drummle. This actor has not the person-
ality that would give the character the neces-
sary weight. J. Pluinpton Wilson, as the
drunk Baronet, George Orreyd, contributes
a delightful comedy study; but the play
would probably 7 be improved were he and
Frederick Ward to change places in the
cast. The part of Hugh Ardale, the most
difficult of the male characters, since the
actor has to take his audience by storm
without any preparatory help from the
dramatist, is quite capably rendered by
George Hewlett, who has the advantage of
looking like his reputation. Esther Mitchell,
with a few gaucheries due to inexperience,
makes otherwise an ideal Ellean. Althea
Hyde, as Lady Orreyd, fails to make her
humor sufficiently unctuous, while Margaret
Gordon lacks the manner of the society
dame, Mrs. Cortel'yon. The play is worthily
produced, and the theatre looks gay in its
new decorative scheme.

Though it is not apparent that the supply
of bombs in the box-office of Sydney Cri-
terion is running out, the heroic defenders
“Under Fire” in the British trench are not
expected to hold out more than a few weeks
longer.
“You’re the One,” the revue at Sydney
Adelphi. was put into dock for an overhaul
after a couple of nights’ run, and a few
repairs and additions have improved the
show. On Friday the profesh at present in
Sydney will combine in a matinee at the
Adelphi to give the War Chest Fund a well-
deserved lift.

ney, and will probably prance on for another

Sydney Tivoli has Ella Caspers back, and
the lady with the voice of gold is assisting

Mclntosh flying. Mildred Grover is still

advertisements, and Ula, the little dancer,
provides a graceful interlude. Wolgast and

Sisters, Musette, juggler Pollard and a few

The circus comes to town again on Satur-
day night, and . George and Philip Wirth
will lead the animals two by two into the
new ark, which is anchored near Sydney's
not-too-Central railway station. The two
descendants of Noah assure The Bulletin
that the “old man’s” show has been greatly
improved upon. For instance, he had no
performing seals that could be worked for
more than a one-night stand, nor a great
rider like May Martin Wirth. Philip Wirth
reckons the absence of a horsewoman like
May was the weakest feature in Noah’s pro-
gramme.
