Coming after the more satisfactory per-
formances of “Twelfth Night,” “liamlet” and
“Othello,” .the production of “Romeo and
Juliet” late in the Shakspeare season at
Melbourne Princess’s seems rather uncalled-
for. The name-parts are not often presented
convincingly both at once, and the rest of
the play is not rich in opportunities for the
co. Only an exceptionally suitable Juliet,
who can look very young and beau-
tiful and be very gaily emotional
before marriage and distressfully emo-
tional afterwards, or a Romeo like
the late Ivyrle Bellow, who was almost tin*
ideql boy-lover, will justify a revival of the
corpse-strewn romance. Allan Wilkie and
Miss Hunter Watts are neither of them at
their best this time. The lady, of course, is
a winsome enough Juliet, unaffected and
fairly forcible in the potion scene, and less
convincing in her kittenish discussion with
Nursey. But in the horribly gloomy last
scene both she and Wilkie were weak on
Saturday night. lie was strongest in his
burst of rage on discovering that fiery Tybalt
had made a fatal puncture in Mercutio. In
rage, despair or amorous mood, however.
Wilkie was obviously not the man for the
part. lie has no youthfulness of speech. Ilis
sombre and somewhat lumpy voice has no
caresses in it. None of the characteristics
of a reckless youth suffering from girl on the
brain are displayed. And his wooing of
Juliet from below the balcony doesn’t excuse
her hasty proposal of marriage. Clara Ste-
phenson, formerly of musical comedy, is the
sort of Nurse who wouldn’t stick at suggest-
ing bigamy to her young mistress. Walter
Hunt is not a merry devil of a Mercutio,
nor is Edward Landor an impressive Friar
Lawrence. Faulkner, Cosgrove, St. John,
Champney. Belbridge. Scully and other men
are more or less effective, and Miss Harvey
and Miss Sidney figure as the respective lady
principals of the Montague and Capulet
parties. “Romeo and Juliet” is handsomely
staged, and will, perhaps, last out the season,
which ends next week.
At Melbourne Royal the bill has dropped
from “It Pays to Advertise” to “Twin Reds”
—from good, sound, farcical comedy with a
Yankee moral to mere puerility with a poster
purpose. For the first few minutes “Twin
Beds” promises to be a lesson to lately-
married couples. A harmless young matron,
of gregarious disposition, who has the habit
of gazing invitingly at all mankind, excites
the natural jealousy of a husband who wants
a monopoly of her yearning expression and
a first call upon her flirtatiousness. Jwo
other types of couples are contrasted with
the commonplace Hawkinses, and it looks as
though a reasonable exaggeration of domestic
complications might keep the comedy fairly
interesting for a couple of hours. But the
story begins to lose reasonableness even in
the first act. The next two acts are a
Now, Mrs. Tanqueray the first
Was with an icy coldness cursed.
Her daughter, little Saint Ellean,
Was just as cold as she had been.
But Mrs. Tanqueray the second
Could not an icicle be reckoned.
Your past had not been all a blank,
Mrs. Tank !
Though Aubrey by his friends was warned,
The noble chap all reason scorned :
But when the two were safely wed,
The county simply cut her dead.
And; Paula we are sorry for:
The truth is Aubrey was a bore.
The cup of misery you drank,
Mrs. Tank !
Ellean, a priggish little chit,
Did not like Paula, not a bit.
Though Paula for her kisses yearned,
The daughter to her father turned.
So Paula into passions fiew:
It wasn’t really nice of you,
Now. was it, Paula —let's be frank,
Mrs. Tank!
Ellean then met a nice young man,
And thawed, as even cold girls can.
Hugh Ardale came her dad to see,
And met his ma-in-law-to-be.
No wonder that they stared, aghast,
For he had figured in her past!
No wonder that to earth you sank,
' Mrs. Tank !
Of course, she found out, Saint Ellean.
Pinero couldn’t miss that scene!
And Aubrey had to learn the fact—
Trust Arthur Wing to write that act!
And Paula’s speech ! She talked and talked,
And then into her bedroom stalked,
And tactfully the poison drank—-
Mrs. Tank!

succession of impossible situations in a bed-
room, which is also a sitting-room for the
Hawkins pair and a liberty hall for the
occupants of contiguous flats. A gigantic
clothes-basket stands prominent in this room
of many doors, the purpose of the basket
being to conceal a greasy, inebriated Italian
tenor when he isn’t sleeping by mistake in
the bed of Mr. Hawkins or hiding in the
bed or under the bed of Mrs. Hawkins. Mean-
while the greasy tenor’s terrible wife ram-
pages at intervals into the hybrid apartment
complaining of her husband’s infidelity, and
Mr. Hawkins wanders about in pyjamas
seeking the garments which his wife has lent
to the greqsy tenor. Roars and shrieks of
excitement make “Twin Beds” very noisy,
as well as foolish, in its latter stages.
* * * *
Much impropriety and pleasure was ex-
pected from the paragraphic allegation that
.Airs. Hawkins would disrobe and go to bed
in full view of the audience. The promise
is not honestly fulfilled. The lady’s undress-
ing is the most truly farcical incident of
the entertainment, seeing that she sheds no
garments that have been in evidence, and
puts nothing on to hide the loss. Her day-
gown and her nightie are one and the same
article of covering, apparently. The prin-
cipal things that she doesn’t go to bed in
are her boots. As an undressed character
during the second half of the time, she com-
pares unfavorably with the three men, who
have all the appearance of being clad only in
pyjamas. They, at least, leave nothing
further to be desired towards supplying the
festive scene with bedroom and bathroom
atmosphere.
Myrtle Tanneliill, as the harmless young-
wife in a harmful plight, lacks sincerity of
perturbation, as a rule. Her nice ladylike-
ness is colorless and insipid, whereas the
style of Celia Ghiloni, as the tenor’s master-
ful spouse, is unduly aggressive and pun-
gent. Mildred AA r arren gets almost full
value out of a rational maid-servant char-
acter, and Bowles is a distinctly humorous
impersonation of a young husband who runs
on polite-lunatic lines. Nancye Stewart, as
Bowles’s unimportant wife, shows more
intelligence than vivacity. She might take
her feeble part less seriously. Hale Hamil-
ton, as Hawkins, is brisk, brusque or blus-
terous according to the requirements of his
job, but he lacks magnetic attractiveness.
His personality is just plump and pleasing,
rather than distinctive. As for the Italian
singer (played by an Italian stranger) who
is accountable for the whole disturbance, he
seems an impossible person clumsily carica-
tured and void of human interest.

“Robbery Under Arms” is to put in a
week at Melbourne King’s before the AA'ay-
backs quit the premises. Melbourne remem-
bers it dimly as a bushrangeous melodrama
in which many revolvers are presented and
none are ever fired. After a week of Cap-
tain Starlight and his heroic misdeeds, the
Bailey management will resume control of
the King’s and deliver “A Message from
Mars,” with Gregan McMahon in his old
character of Parker.

The Melb. Tivoli programme of Follies
has changed not at all since last week, nor
is there an urgent public demand for change
in the immediate future. De Coe balances
himself on the same shaky structure of
chairs and tables as before, and warriors in
uniform are still admitted to the d.c. at half-
price, except on Saturdays.
** * *
Newcomers at Melbourne Bijou last Satur-
day were Lillian Skinner, a comely and
tuneful young songstress in pink; a
sturdy tumbler and somersault merchant,
called Decar, who is admirable in
acrobatics and painful in his striv-
ings after humor; and Victor, a ven-
triloquist, who introduces Little Gladys.
Victor’s dummy walks and talks better than
it sings; and, finally, Gladys, a slim and
solemn child, comes along with another
stuffed freak which supports the aforesaid
dummy in a discordant duet. Two ventri-
loquial performers working in company are a
novelty that “knocks” an impressionable
audience, all the same. Other turns, old
and net?, are mostly sung or danced to rag-
time measures, and this journal feels sorry
for them.
With pictures at the Royal and panto-
mime at the Tivoli the exchange of charac-
ters by Adelaide theatres is complete. “Dick
Whittington” is not equal in splendor to
the rival “Mother Goose,” but the comedians
are funnier —which is easy. Carrie Moore
is dashing and Dame Dan Thomas has the
necessary quaintness. The King’s gives
“revue” a rest, and has a jolly mixed bill.
The new Royal pictures introduce Aus-
tralia’s old friend Nance O’Neill; also
Theda Bara, who was so absolutely It as
Carmen that it is half a shock to see her in
another part. The “Stayed at Home” film
a;; Wondergraph is enlivened by some real-
war open-air effects that could not be got
into the stage version. This house, like
AVest’s and the Central (where A’arna recites
Dean Maitland’s final confession in the pul-
pit), shows the new tendency to supplement
pictures by a little of the human element.
Warm weather has come back to encourage
the closing weeks of the Dandies, who now
go in for operatic selections, and of the
Ideals, who have turned on the conjurer
Malini.
“The Old Soldier”: Court-martial evi-
dence at Paddington (Sydney) barracks is
not calculated to fill the public with respect
for the Defence Department. Recently a
man was tried and acquitted for desertion,
and this was his history:—
Enlisted September, 1914, and sent to the Front in
October. Returned to Melbourne invalided on Octo-
ber, 1915. Discharged from hospital in January.
Got a week’s leave to go home, January 31; but was
kept four days waiting for the money due to
him at the pay-office. He had to go some 300 miles
to see his people, and reported himself at Liverpool
camp February 12, only five days late.
Why could not the money owing to that
man be credited to his company commander
as it would have been in a British regiment,
and handed to him with his pass, which
ought to have been, according to British
precedent in such cases, a month’s fur-
lough? This is simply another of those very
numerous cases that prove how very neces-
sary it is that the Defence Department
should administer the various corps of which
its army consists separately, and indepen-
dently of each other. It would simplify Aus-
tralian-army management immensely. Noth-
ing is more important than to simplify Aus-
tralian-army management immensely.
AT POVERTY POINT.
The jam factory on the south side of
Princes’ Bridge (Melb.), which a while back
was converted into the Snowden picture pal-
ace, is now being changed to a theatre. It
will seat 1000 and allow them to stretch
their legs, and will be just the right size
for comedy. The local Repertory people
will have it for three months each year; at
other times it will be open for engagements.
Gregan McMahon, who has been appointed
manager, hopes to ring the curtain up at
Easter.
“Old ’Un’’: I am reminded by “Ami’s”
par. (B. 23/3/M6) that the late Ted
(or Ned) Farley owed his career to
the education he got from a musical curio-
sity named Rolfe, the pianist and conductor
of the sailors’ “free and easy” music-hall at
the Ship Inn, Sandridge, an old-time pub
recently delicensed. Rolfe was also organist
to the local Anglican church, and used to
slip his favorite boys from the choir into the
show as a treat. The performance was on
the lines of modern vaudeville. Every
Christmas they put on a pantomime, at
which a charge was made for admission.
Farley was billed as “The Sydney Lion
Comique,” and sang such things as “Why
Shouldn’t Paddy be a Gentleman?” in a
raucous baritone voice. Rolfe’s tuition
altered this and pushed Farley up. A
large number of the early Minstrel favorites
graduated from the “Ship.” Some, like
Farley, climbed higher. Tom Rainford sang
there immediately prior to joining the
Weston and Hussey Minstrels.
Passed into silence last month in Eng-
land, Wilson Pembroke, the robust tenor of
the National Opera Co. which ran a brief
season at Melbourne Royal in 1908. Pem-
broke had done a lot of work with the
Moody-Manners co., and when Australia
heard him his voice was on the hard side
and his figure was no longer romantic.
Melbourne is sending baritone Horace
Stevens to look after soldiers’ teeth at the
Front. Though the silver-haired singer is
under 40, his platform career began with
the Philharmonic in “Judas Maceabaeus” in
’9B, and he has carolled devotionally in St.
Paul’s Cathedral as chief soloist ever since
Gladstone Wright died at the end of ’97.
“Ho” : While rehearsing one of his hair-
raising balancing feats at Ballarat the other
day Archie Falls, of Ross and Falls, the
“Mother Goose” panto acrobats, reached the
stage so suddenly that he broke two ribs.
It looked as though the turn, which is one
of the features of the show, would have to
lapse; but Mrs. Falls, who is not here on
business, got into hubby’s clothes, imitated
his make-up and went right through the
turn.
“J.F.H.,” from London :
“Mrs. Pretty and the Premier,” by Arthur H.
Adams, had a first-rate send-off at His Majesty’s, and
has every prospect of a successful run. William
Archer, of the Star, opens his notice with the remark
that “Australia has every reason to be gratified by
the reception accorded to the first play of Australian
authorship and scene produced at a leading London
theatre.” This foremost critic credits Adams with
“scenic invention, a deft touch in dialogue, and
genuine, unforced humor.” The Daily Chronicle
calls it an “ideal war-time play, a delightfully frivolous
young comedy.” The Daily Telegraph sums it up
as a “breezy business, productive of plenty of laughter
and applause.” And Walkley of the Times discusses
it in good-humored, chatty style: “Australian public
opinion is so very squeamish, and (hang it all!) you
must have a political crisis over something. So
why not over a veiled lady and a cat ?”
A series of recitals has been arranged by
William Laver, Australian professor of
mostly-German music at the University Con-
servatorium. Three piano programmes, to
be presented by Edward Goll in the Melba
Hall, are to deal respectively with the fugues
of Bach, the sonatas of Beethoven and the
musical products of Brahms; and Goll is
also to give three Chamber-music concerts
between whiles —altogether a good, bold
start for the new German year at the music-
school.
“S.S.”: Quaint the trivialities to which
men will devote their gifts even in the riot
of a tumbling world ! I have just met a
Tommy, back from the Front with a Per-
forated spleen, who discovered his true voca-
tion in the long intervals between scraps al
Anzac. It seems that Providence blessed
him with a peculiar talent for the manipula-
tion of cigarette smoke, and his intention
is to display it on the music-hall stage as
soon as his punctured organ is half-soled
and heeled. He can blow smoke rings in
rapid succession, he can blow your initials
in recognisable script, he can blow primitive
flowers, he can produce a very passable out-
line of an infuriated cat out of one mouthful
of smoke. In addition to all this he has an
extensive repertoire of chains, spirals,
arabesques, etc., and as a public enter-
tainer should be a great success in a com-
munity where two-thirds of the young men
flatter themselves on the ability to blow a
single ring.
“J.F.H.” (from London) : The Drury Lane
panto., “Puss in Boots,” in which Florence
Smithson warbles and scores triumphantly,
is being played afternoon and evening to
thronged houses, and is expected to hold good
until close on Easter. It needed capacity
houses to pay much of a dividend, seeing that
the preliminary outlay was £25,000 and the
weekly expenses exceed £4OOO. The princi-
pals divide a thousand, and there are 500
supers and subordinates drawing an average
of 30s. a week.

The one-sided cables didn’t give much
attention last week to the way the Germans
wiped out the reverse at Verdun. With a
single torpedo they achieved a glorious vic-
tory over the entire lightship at Harwich.
Lieut. Bill Denny left Adelaide for the
Front last week. He is the lawyer who was
Attorney-General in the Yerran Ministry,
but was omitted from the present Vaughan
Government. Joining the ranks as a private,
he quickly worked up to N.C.O. and then
to a commission. His farewell message whs
one more hint to the Vaughans, his nominal
leaders, that they might do a lot of good
by getting into khaki themselves. Another
departing kangaroo is IT. S. Hudd, who was
Liberal member for Torrens till he was
dropped at the last general election.
It isn’t safe these times to mention that
there’s money lying around loose. Last
week The Bulletin drew attention to the
unclaimed balances in the banks, and in pro-
cess of gradual absorption through the
charge of ss. a half-year made for keeping
an account open ; and it was suggested that
the Commonwealth should lift the money for
war purposes. But before Treasurer Higgs
could wriggle Ilolman jumped at the sugges-
tion and announced that he was going to take
the money available in N. S. Wales and invest
it in war stock. S. Australia led the way
in the matter of these balances nearly 20
years ago, and Vic. also has a law on the
subject. But apparently the other States
haven’t; so unless the Commonwealth moves
four more sets of legislation, passed by eight
Houses of Parliament, will be necessary to
finish the job. And then it will most likely
be a patchwork.
The prize for the most original contribu-
tion to the Speck’s early-closing controversy
goes to a sport trading under the historical
name of W. H. Lecky. He wants six o’clock
closing as a means of boosting the birth-rate:
What the publicans lose after tea if early closing-
comes in will be gained largely by proprietors of
picture-shows, doctors and midwives.
As the six o’clockers won by an overwhelm-
ing majority, the little Tasmanian will pre-
sently no doubt be like the sand on the sea
shore for numbers.
Probably there isn’t a man living who has
brought more vessels through Torres Straits
than Captain McArthur, a Goulburn native
who died very suddenly in Sydney the other
day. As a youngster he sailed in the Circe,
Black Diamond, Lady Franklin and John
Wesley, as well as the well-known woolship
Gladstone; and in his long career with the
E. and A. he had commanded the Menmuir,
Tannadice, Guthrie, Australian, Empire,
Aldenham and St. Albans. He never had one
single serious mishap all his seafaring days,
and never made anything but friends.
That restless individual, ex-constable
Crowe, has at last got himself to a place
where everything is quiet and well-ordered,
and the dull monotony of life is broken only
by the occasional visits of the Inspector-
General. If he deserved gaol at all he cer-
tainly earned all his three years for about
the hottest parcel of libels on Macmanamny
that a man could perpetrate, .including
charges of murder, robbery, procuration
blackmail and the Lord knows what else’.
But it seems certain that Crowe’s obsession
has developed into mania, and the question is
whether he doesn’t want a doctor rather than
a warder.
SCIENCE TRIUMPHS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now to the opera matinees
In happy confidence I go,
And in the stalls I spend my days
In calm enjoyment of the show,
Though women’s hats with plumes and bows
Arise before in Alpine rows.
There was a time when all in vain
The ballet girls I sought to see,
And I was driven forth again
By stacks of floral millinery ;
For I am short, and hate the cats
Who hide fair legs with hideous hats.
But nothing passes me to-day:
I see the wriggle of each heel;
The merry calves’ symmetric play
The highest tile cannot conceal.
The whole stage vision in I rope
With my cute little periscope.
VICTORIA.

MUCH WORSE.
MOTHER: “If you marry him in haste you
will repent at leisure.”
DAUGHTER: "Well, I can’t bear to think of
another girl repenting at leisure with
him.”
“Dumper”: “Ironworker’s” case (B.
23/3/T6) may be the “unholy limit,” but
what about this? My brother was employed
by the Defence Department. He wished to
volunteer, but was told not to, because he
was an expert stenographer and could not be
spared at the time. Later on he, with
others, was given the hint to don khaki. He
took the afternoon off and spent hours at the
recruiting office (it occurred at a rush
time). Next morning he reported to the
Red Tape boss that he was accepted for ser-
vice and would go into camp the following
week. He drew his pay at the week-end, and
the Department—the Defence Department—
carefully docked him the half-day he spent in
enlisting !
“S.S.”: I lingered in Swanston-street the
other evening to hear out a fierce argument
between a lad and his donah of the tobacco-
factory type. The lad had the last word.
The girl had poured the vials of her wrath
over him. He stepped back. “Garn!” he
said. “Fer gorsake get a move on —ycr
spoilin’ the look of the town!"
* * * *
On present appearances Australia and
Bolivia may soon be locked in a terrific
death-grapple. Sometime in last Novem-
ber H. J. Nilen. an Australian, took a hand
in trying to recover a wolfram mining claim
in Bolivia which had been jumped by a citi-
zen of that land named Frieke. The Aus-
tralian was shot dead in. the argument which
ensued. The British Consul made the usual
representations to the Bolivian Government;
but up to the present the latter has only
answered “Yah ! Beat the Germans !” or
words to that effect. Under the circum-
stances it looks as if Australia ought to send
a warship across the Pacific to uphold the
dignity of the Commonwealth ; but as Bolivia
hasn’t any coastline such an enterprise
would be mere waste of coal. Unless the
new aviation school can solve the problem
the inland republic of the Andes may pre-
serve its insolent attitude in perpetuity, and
thereby establish a precedent that wander-
ing Australians may be shot at sight if they
get out of range of the sea.
* * * *
The world is a very small pill. Corporals
W. E. F. Daley and G. Duncan, of Bendigo,
waded ashore together at Gallipoli on April
25, 1915. Daley got a sprinkling of shrapnel
and Duncan helped to carry him to the
beach. Then Duncan ran back to pump more
lead at the Turks and saw no more of Daley
till he found himself sitting beside him in a
motor that was taking both to a welcome
home in the Bendigo Town Hall last week.
*• * * *
The Defence people have issued an invi-
tation to creditors “whose claims have not
been met for what may seem to them an
unduly long time” to communicate with the
Department. They should have stopped at
that; instead, they went on to suggest that
the delays are due to the claims being
lodged in an irregular way. The real
trouble is the discovery that payments have
been made in an irregular way. The scan-
dal probably reached its limit the other day
when, an “irregular” payment having been
discovered, the person who should have
drawn the money on behalf of an absent
soldier was asked to sign an indemnity
which amounted to a confession that he had
received the money previously. If people
with long-due claims are asked to sign
“indemnities” they had therefore better read
them carefully. Meantime a thorough over-
haul of the Pay branch of the Department
by some business organiser is urgent.
* * * *
“Fish” : Perhaps there are too many holi-
days now ; but when grandfather says that
things were different in his time —well, don’t
be rude, but change the subject. According
to the V.D.L. Almanac for 1828 the follow-
ing were the bank holidays “appointed to
be observed in Tasmania in this year of
grace”: Jan. 1. New Year’s Day; Jan. 29,
King George IY.’s Accession Day; March 1,
St. David’s Day; March 17, St. Patrick’s
Day; April 23, St. George’s Day; May 14,
anniversary of Governor Arthur’s arrival;
July 19, King’s Coronation Day; August 12,
King’s Birthday; Nov. 30, St. Andrew’s
Day; Dec. 25, Christmas Day; Dec. 26, St.
Stephen’s Day. Besides this little lot there
were Good Friday and the following Satur-
day, Easter Monday and Tuesday, and
Whit Monday and Tuesday, or 17 bank
holidays for the year.
* * * *
Mayor Isaacs, of Adelaide, has done a
graceful act in persuading that city to at
any rate seem a little excited over the peri-
odical home-coming of wounded soldiers. The
common-sense community has rather got into
a way of priding itself on never getting
excited. You never hear of a riot in
Adelaide, for instance. It is a good quality,
but has its defects, and the invalids felt a
little hurt by the silence of the crowds that
welcomed them. So now each batch is
paraded through the chief streets, in readily-
lent motor-cars, and the Mayor announces
the route in advance, and asks that it shall
be decorated with flags, and that all
employers shall let their people come outside
to cheer. Also he has the Town Hall bells
rung to announce the arrival; and all there
is left for the onlookers to do is to try to
make a noise like a really enthusiastic
crowd.
* * * *
“Tibia”: We’re still breeding the kind
that did the things at Sari Bair. A young-
ster of about 10 was brought in to the
surgery. He had fallen through a window
and had gashed his leg severely. Six stitches
were necessary to close the gaping wound.
Young Billjim took it without flinching. It
was such a plucky performance that I told
him he was a brave little fellow. The
young stoic was quite unconcerned about
that. What he said was: “You got any
cigarette cards. Mister?”
MAKING IT RIGHT FOR DOCTOR.
FIRST VOLUNTARY AID: “This patient's temperature is 105deg. What shall I do?"
SECOND V.A.: “Put him down 100. The doctor gets so nervous if it’s more."
Another Rev. Yarrington has upended
himself in Sydney. In “thanking God” the
other day to a pi’ess interviewer that he
knew nothing about the Red Plague, Rev.
J. G. M. Taylor, general secretary of the
Methodist Home Mission Society, remarked:
What do we find ? Girls are frequently drugged
and taken to houses of ill-fame and the mischief is
done.
Now, what is the Police Department doing
about the information which the missioner
has, of course, handed in?

“Elburg”: One result of the nme-to-nine
pub legislation in Perth has been the serving
of joy-juice in teapots in a more or less
swagger Graeco-Roman or Catch-as-catch-can
restaurant. For some weeks all went merry
as a marriage swill; then a Sunday journal
blew the froth off the pot. Police in plain
clothes, and with their feet disguised, were
among the paying guests on a recent Satur-
day night. When they revealed their iden-
tity by peering earnestly into the tea-
pots and taking other deep measures
to prove that the liquid was not
made in Ceylon, the many dear young
things who should not have been there had
visions of cells and sermons. But the only
arrests made were of pyramids of bottled
English beer and some hundreds of dozens
of German lager. Demetrius subsequently
appeared before the P.M., who declined to
believe (1) that Demetrius didn’t know he
was fracturing the law; (2) that the liquor
was only kept to slake the thirst of the
washerlady; (3) that the beer had crept in
through the keyhole when he wasn’t looking.
Demetrius is £3O poorer, besides suffering the
confiscation of the wet.
* * * *
A big and hot discussion is proceeding in
Adelaide over a Criminal Court case. The
public had read with some amusement the
evidence of the prosecutrix on a charge of rape
—that after a tramcar flirtation she strolled
down with accused, an ex-bank-clerk of 28,
to the Botanic Park, a ouiet spot, but with
houses quite near; that her protest against
his attack was, “Don’t be silly, think
what you are doing” ; that they walked home
together, stopping on the way to buy fruit,
and parted with an appointment to meet
again ; and that she made no complaint till
next day. Then a sentence of four years’
hard labor made the public sit up and rub its
eyes. The daily papers jumped in with
leading articles demanding reconsideration
and a correspondence has boomed ever since!
The apparently savage sentence, it is ex-
plained, is the least allowed for rape; to
which the dbvious reply is that in that case
the verdict itself was wrong. Lawyers point
out that accused swore obstinately that
nothing at all beyond a brisk flirtation ever
occurred; so facts alone were argued, and
the question of Consent was not raised. But
on a rape charge that question ought to be
raised, even if the Judge has to do it —grim

married at Easter, but Bertie went away
to the tear.”
THE OTHER ONE : “I see —cold feet!"
old Boucaut would assuredly have done it. Tlie
immediate outcome is a widely-signed petition
to the Governor for pardon —the clumsy
machinery which the absence of a local Court
of Criminal Appeal makes necessary. The
foreman of the jury has publicly expressed
his willingness to sign the petition.
TO HIS LADY ON HOLIDAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The morning is sweet, and the singing
Of birds that are fleet in the blue,
Or up in the branches are swinging, is stinging
My heart to new sorrow for you.
What use are the birds and the breeze and the trees?
There’s nothing can please when I miss you, Elise !
So here, with fine fervor poetic,
I long for you, ache for you, call.
Without you life’s but an emetic, prophetic
Of Death’s draught that comes to us all.
But I don’t want Death’s cup and his fingers that
freeze,
While still I can snuggle to kiss you, Elise.
Come back from your holiday, bad one !
Return, or I’ll pull down the flat!
With you in my arms life’s a glad one, a mad one,
Despite your distempers, sly cat.
So why should you tarry tormenting, you tease?
Be quick! or, by James, I’ll get married, Elise!
I love you, coy witch, and you know it.
My blood is enriched by your kiss.
You say that you love your crook poet. Well, show
it!
Come back, and restore me to bliss.
There’s a flapper next door who is meek at my
knees—
Sqys I’m “so sweet with women,” you dawdling Elise!
When our star after midnight shines cleares„
I sigh for the nights I have known.
Does the star after midnight that’s nearest, my
dearest,
Not tell you I'm restless, alone?
They miss you, they miss you, the birds and the
trees !. ...
Have you never a thought for the widowed, Elise ?
N.S.W.

GROWS NEW HAIR IN TWO WEEKS.
MONEY BACK IF IT FAILS.
Most men take baldness as something in-
evitable, as though it were a kind of income
tax or water rate —though why, it passes
understanding. There is no more reason
why hair should cease to grow on the top
of the head than on the sides of the face—
and most men have to shave to the end of
their days, or wear a heard. If you are
finding the hair growing grey or getting thin
in patches, buy a bottle _of Lavona Ilair
Tonic and use it according to directions.
As a business man you know the value and
the meaning of a guarantee. Well, the
chemist who sells you the bottle will give
you his own personal guarantee that Lavona
llair Tonic will prevent your hair from
falling out, grow new hair on the bald places,
and turn the grey and faded color to its
original shade, or he will give you your
money back. Lavona Hair Tonic is put up
in attractive sprinkler stoppered bottles, re-
tailing at only 4s. per bottle. It is pre-
pared from the well-known old-fashioned
formula calling for loz. Lavona de Com-
posee, 2oz. bay rum, and i-dram menthol
crystals, which can be mixed at home or by
your chemist; but if you wish the guarantee
of satisfaction or money back, always ask
for Lavona Hair Tonic—“the kind that’s
guaranteed.”-— Advt.
From “The Oldest Inhabitant”:—
When Jack Shearston was going to school his
father had a butchery at Surry-street and Glenmore-
road. Young Shearston was junior messenger at the
Mint at the time he changed his plans and undertook
the care of the Tar ashore. He got a good grip mainly
through the 1.0.G.T., of which he was a high honor-
ary officer. At the old Temperance Hall in Pitt-
street in the early ’Bo’s Saturday-night lodge meet-
ings were held at which the “handy man” was the
main attraction. Lodges were next opened on board
the men-o’-war visiting the harbor, the stokehole
being usually the “lodge-room.” Big John Wright,
an Ulster man, training-master at Fort-street School,
was then “Grand Chief Templar.” Presently cap-
tains complained to Wright that his good-templary
was doing an injury to some of the men. The reason
was found to be that Shearston’s sailors gave their
grog-ration to their non-teetotal mates. Thereafter
the pledge was altered, and the never-touch-it Tar
did not draw his tot. Shearston was fearless
in his devotion to# his job. You could see him
EYES RIGHT.
“What do you do when a soldier tries to
kiss you?"
“If he is a nice soldier I tell him to find out."
diving into the old “Crooked Billet” in “Lower
George” and convoying two tipsy sailors to safety, re-
peating the feat at other Lower George-street pubs
night after night. By and bye, as people became in-
terested, he established a home ashore for naval men
at Trafalgar House. It succeeded so well that the
Loyal Naval House came into existence, and
“Johnny” was given the management as a matter of
course. If Jellicoe died he would get more space in
the papers; but the news wouldn’t sadden more
sailormen’s hearts than the word passed round last
"eek that Johnny Shearston had gone aloft.

, ‘‘Pint Pot”: Never in my wildest jim-jams
did I imagine a union secretary calling a
Chow “brother.” But I have
just seen a typewritten let-
ter, signed by the secretary
of a big trade union, men-
tioning the employment of
Chinese artisans, and com-
plaining that “these
brethren” are not being paid
at award rates. It may be
urged for the defence that
the secretary did not type
his letter himself, nor dic-
tate it to a stenographer, but
scribbled a rough draft, writ-
ing “these heathen,” which
the typist by mistake read
as “these brethren.” Any-
way, the letter was sent out
as quoted above, and the old-
time secretaries of 20 years
back are gyrating in their graves.

“309”: This is for your collection of close
shaves. At Cape Helles last May one of
the Naval Division was crossing behind our
battery at right angles to the line of fire
when a Turkish 4.2 shell passed a few feet
over our heads, knocked the passer-by for-
ward on his face and exploded in the ground
about 20 yards further on. We picked up
the crumpled heap of man, and found to our
amazement that he wasn’t ready for a
funeral. His tunic was torn and his back
rather badly bruised, but that was all. The
point of the shell had room to clear him,
but the driving band at the base had rubbed
across his back without breaking the skin.
He had been massaged by a shell!

“D.F.”: The Lemon Springs property has
just been offered for sale in Tasmania, and
a pretty story goes
with it. Mark Lemon
was the first Tas-
manian bushranger.
Lie took to the bush in
1808, when the V.D.L.
settlement was only
five years old, and he
had a run of seven
years before he came
,to his end. With a
mate named Brown he
was camped near what
thereafter was known
as Lemon Springs
when an Irish assigned
servant named Mans-
field resolved to earn
the reward for the
brace of heads, and
enlisted another con-
vict, one Plielim, for
the campaign. Mans-
field and his corporal
joined the two out-
laws, alleging that
they wanted to take
to the bush. Then
one night when Lemon
and his mate were
asleep their weapons
were abstracted and
the pair were roused
and told to surrender.
Lemon offered fight
and was shot. Mans-
field cut the head off
and compelled the
other bushranger to
carry it to Hobart, a
distance of about 50
miles, as proof that
his recent host was as
dead as any Govern-
ment could reasonably
ask.
“Fairfield”: “Leo Lear” (B. 9/3/’l6)
made a good point when he suggested that
a Minister for Names and Titles should be
appointed and that in him should be vested
for the nation the patent rights in the word
“Anzac.” The farewell-to-soldiers commit-
tee to which I belong had about 400 sou-
venirs made bearing the word “cooee,” and
we distributed them to local men enlisting.
Then the bomb exploded. An Adelaide
woman fired in a claim for 10 per cent,
royalty, alleging that she held exclusive
rights to use “cooee” on jewellery, medals,
pottery, fancy goods, name plates for houses,
etc. Our solicitors advised “Pay up,
and curse.” We did both. It was all we
could do; but I wish somebody with more
money would test the female’s rights.
A Kangaroo at the Court of rharaoli: —-
It has been raining here again to-day—three wet
days in two months, which in Egypt is a flood. But
we still don’t have enough water for a bath. If we
want a decent wash we have to go to Cairo. And
Cairo is a dreary place after a while. No one there is
ever in a hurry. They can’t even die in a hurry.
They start soon after they are born—as soon as they
have any sense—and they keep on dying all their
life. When they finish the job, it takes all day to
bury them. If the lamented is wealthy the procession
is headed by a dozen mourners mounted on donkeys.
Then he comes next, and the unmounted mourners
follow, some of them waving their handkerchiefs to
him. They all make weird noises, which the older
and more experienced inhabitants say are chants, but
CALL THE STEWARD.
HOGAN OF THE BILLABONG: “Gentlemen, I don't know whose toot’brush I’ve been usin’
this wake, but it’s just slipped down the drain, an’ I’ll buy ’im a noo one."
which might be anything from cholera to toothache.
The coffin —a deal affair—is covered with hessian and
has four legs, like a table. They always seem to
carry it home again; evidently it does the whole
family. So when little Mahmhoot dies he takes the
trip in the coffin that Moses snuggled in 12 months
ago. It can’t be said to be unhealthy, because it
really doesn’t matter if Mahmhoot does catch what
Moses died of.
The Gyp. never wastes soap or water, and would
die at the sight of inseetibane. If an ill-mannered
flea bites him harder than usual he sits down in the
middle of the footpath and makes a tour of inspec-
tion, and if he can’t find the flea that way he
undresses himself till he does find it. No one pays
any attention to him —not even to stop and ask him
if he’s had any luck. When it rains they lift up as
much of their clothes as they can spare over their
head, and it serves as an umbrella.
Most of them have only one eye left. The for-
tunate ones who have two are usually crosseyed.
This is due to their habit of always trying to take
down two people at once.
In Cairo the houses are mostly made of old stones
stuck together with mud. They are full of donkeys,
children, goats, fowls and vermin. When the mud
dries and the wind blows the walls start to fall
down. But no one minds that. If the drawing-room
wall falls and kills Imshi they bury him and go
back home again until they have to look for the
remains of little Yaller among the ruins of the
smoke-room walls. And so it goes on until the neigh-
bors get tired and don’t go to the funerals any
longer. Then the family collects what’s left of it
and its donkeys and pianos and goats and moves on
to another house.

If £3 12s. Bd. per week is adequate pay
for a man who has to juggle with £72,000 a
year, nobody need feel any sympathy for the
ex-postmaster who was recently awarded
two years’ hard labor in Sydney for em-
bezzling. The unfortunate attributed his
peck of troubles to ill-health and horseracing,
but charitably omitted mention of his pit-
tance of £lB9 per annum. It wouldn’t re-
quire many sparks of either horseracing or
illness to burn up that dole. The Judge
talked the usual easy burble about the
man’s serious offence, but hadn’t a word to
say concerning the department which first
starves a man and then puts the temptation
of a meal in his way. Generally in such
cases it is the mention of horseracing that
puts the victim’s pot on. But mostly the
so-called horseracing is a distracted man’s
foolish way of trying to make the few extra
shillings that his employer ought to be pay-
ing him. Morally it proves the man not a
bit worse than the widow who tries to add
to her insufficient mite by a flutter on the
Stock Exchange. This paper knows even a
Judge who has been guilty of that much.
This is the time for strenuous endeavor,
when every ounce of energy must be put
forth. Which was the reason why the Mel-
bourne wharf-laborers, having chosen March
16 for their annual picnic holiday, didn’t
hold any picnic, but proceeded to the
wharves prepared to work. They didn’t
work, though, because the shipowners
declined to pay the ss. per hour demanded
for “holiday pay.” So they spent the day
looking at goods waiting to be loaded, while
some of the wheels of commerce were
spragged in consequence. It was an inspir-
ing episode.
The most vindictive witness against a
burglar in Melbourne one day last week was
a man who had lost a collar-stud.

“T.P.R.” : A patient consulted me with a
gumma (a syphilitic abscess) just beneath
the skin, near his chin. I noted that it was
broken, with a little discharge. Inquiry
elicited the remark, “07/, the barber did
that this morning with his razor!" We
must have barbers ; but Lord help this coun-
try if the syphilitic patient thinks so little
of his trouble that he holds his face up to
the common brush and the communal razor.
IN THE SPRING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“There will be a great offensive in the Spring.”
Scatters the northern cloud, and happy spring
Comes dancing up the valleys of the world.
The wreath of March has blossomed, streamlets sing,
The banners of the sun are all unfurled ;
And now men pause, while daffodils are strewing
Their garden beds, for something may be doing.
They told us in the spring the clash would come,
The great offensive, spreading fields with death ;
And, waiting that, the myriad tongues grow dumb,
And pallid nations speak with hard-held breath.
We in our purple autumn wait and wonder,
Straining our ears to catch a louder thunder.
Always the spring, the spring! And still we wait,
Dreaming of that tremendous march begun;
Yet they who spoke affixed no certain date
For the high day that sees our triumph won.
The Zeppelins float on with easy motion,
The big guns boom and Britain rules the ocean.
All as they were! And fast their spring moves on ;
Their dimpled April with her showers is near.
Last year we worried when the time was gone,
And nothing German seemed to shake With fear.
Of hope we needs must be a little thrifty—
The spring they mean may be in 1950.
N.S.w.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Brigadier-General G. S. Richardson, who
has been given a military adviser’s job in
London, has ballooned from the ranks. He
started as a gunner in the Garrison Artillery,
and, getting through the long gunnery course,
came to Maoriland to become master-gunner.
Later he was given a commission, and when
the present defence scheme was launched he
became Inspector-General of Artillery. He
rose to be major, and went to England on
exchange to take the Staff College course.
He’d done this when the Hun commenced,
and he was able to get into Antwerp before
Winston’s adventure ended. He did well
there with the naval divisions, and after-
wards the authorities heard more about him
at the Dardanelles.
HAD A REPUTATION.
S HE: “I’ve decided to go to Adelaide for my
holidays.”
HE: “Good gracious! I didn't know you
knew anybody there.”
SHE : “I don't. That's why I'm going.”
AEneas Gunn, a son of William, till lately
of the P.M.G.’s department in Victoria, has
turned 18 and gone promptly into camp.
Young Gunn’s grandfather was the late
Peter, one of a group of five pioneer parsons
who in 1842 formed the first Presbytery of
Victoria. An uncle of 18-year-old JEneas
is that Backblocks pioneer, AEneas Gunn,
whose widow wrote “We of the Never
Never,” and thereby built a monument to
her husband’s memory.
Asquith’s doctor is T. W. Parkinson, son
of an old Canterbury (M.L.) squatter king.
The young man began his education at
Christchurch, did brilliantly at Edinburgh,
and later bought a partnership in llarley-
*treet, where he soon became famous. lie
was knighted the other day.
Harry Jackson, at one time Speaker of
S.A. Assembly, and now Commissioner of
Public Works in the Vaughan Ministry, is
seriously ill in an Adelaide hospital. There
is a complication of troubles that seem to
date back to lead-poisoning in his old
mining days. It came to an operation last
week.
“Yumbeena” : There are 28 survivors of the
50 delegates to the Federal Convention which
met at Adelaide 19 years ago this month.
They are : —N.S.W.: Barton, Carruthers,
Reid, McMillan, Walker and Wise. Vic.:
Deakin, Higgins, Isaacs, Fraser, Peacock,
Quick, Trenwitli and Turner. S. Aus.: Cock-
burn, Glynn, Gordon, Howe and Symon.
Tas.: Clarke, Dobson, Fysh, Lewis and
Moore. Westralia: Forrest, James, Hassell
and Loton.
Some other Australians who have sat in
the House of Commons (B. 23/3/’36) : —-
A. W. Young, one time Sheriff of Sydney, sat for
Yarmouth 1877-9; Henry Moor, solicitor, an early
Mayor of Melbourne; J. J. Dalton (a native of
Orange) ; Alexander McArthur, M.L.A. for Newtown
(N.S.W.) 1859-61, and M.L.C. 1861-3, went to Eng-
land and represented Leicester in the Commons 1874-
92 ; his son, W.A., was in 1886 elected for Buckrose,
York, by one vote over the friend and chum of the
Prince of Wales (Ed. VII.), but was unseated on
scrutiny, and got a seat for St. Austell, Cornwall;
and T. C. Anstey (Tas.), elected for Youghal (Ire-
land) 1847.
“Sequah,” an itinerant quack who made
the rounds in these parts when controversy
got him down in older lands, ended up penni-
less lately in a Johannesburg hospital. He
was a picturesque figure with Samsonian
hair, and was reputed to make great way
with many Delilahs when he visited their
towns in a gorgeous chariot from which he
invited the maimed and the halt to come and
be put right. One incident of his job was
some particularly rapid tooth-jerking, and
if occasionally he took half a jaw as well,
that was all in the day’s fooling. Originally
a London policeman, lie went to the TJ.S.A.
and converted himself into a towsled Indian
freak. Thereafter he reputedly made sev-
eral fortunes, and flung them away again.
Died last week, old Sergeant .Tack Buck-
ley, who was in a Lancer regiment in the
Crimean war in the middle of last century,
and had some big stories to tell. For some
time past he had been in a home for Invalids
in Launceston (Tas.).- When one or two
who knew Buckley heard he was gone, they
said: “The brave old sergeant will have a
big funeral now that everybody is talking
of wars and rolls of honor.” But actually
“the funeral cortbge” comprised three men
—the undertaker, the sexton, and the priest,
Father J. J. Barry. The old flag on his
coffin? No fear! “And,” said the priest
by the grave, “this is the last of a Crimean
hero!”
After wrestling with the claims of 81
applicants, ranging from 22 to 73 years of
age, and belonging to nearly all the profes-
sions under the sun, the Tasmanian Uni. has
appointed a brilliant graduate of the Chosen
Race, Montague Moses Ansell, as its new
Registrar. Monty, wffio is now 41, has
played many parts. He took his degree in
Arts and then went into the law. After
that he drifted to Melbourne and worked
on the first “Commonwealth Year Book.”
After a spell of press work and odds and
ends he got into the Ordnance branch of the
Defence Department and was still there
when the Registrar’s job became vacant.
Melba has been created by King George
a “Lady of Grace” of the order of St. John
of Jerusalem in England, but the distinction
does not entitle her to print “Lady Melba”
on her visiting-cards. This is one of the
minor orders, though a very ancient one, and
is used for recognising the services of people
who have done good work for the Red Cross
and the hospitals. Melba is enrolled in
gratitude for the thousands of pounds she
raised for the Red Cross in Australia, Can-
ada and elsewhere, and Lady Muriel Paget,
Lady Butler and Lady Phillips were yarded
at the same time. The Order was origin-
ally Roman Catholic, and its “Grand
Priory,” or headquarters, is still in the
rooms above St. John’s Gateway, Clerken-
well —all that is left of a splendid monastery
that flourished there in pre-Reformation
times. The R.C.’s continue to maintain
an Order of St. John of Jerusalem, which
they hold to be the only orthodox one,
regarding the Royal one to which Melba has
just been admitted as a usurpation. Inci-
dentally the bird has a grievance against the
careless compositor who set her up in the
Official Gazette, where she was printed
as “Helen Porter (Madame Melba).” Her
full style is Helen Porter Mitchell, but old
dad’s name was dropped out.
From a camp in England :
There’s one Australian colonel —Courtney, of Court-
ney’s Post, Gallipoli—who has got very tired of the
current English fiction that the Australian is a rowdy
drunkard. During a recent church parade the chap-
lain urged the men to abstain from the Curse, and in
the conventional parson manner exaggerated their soak-
ing powers. This was too much for Courtney: it was
bad enough to have irresponsible outsiders spreading
these tales. So he scribbled a note and passed it
up with the request that it might be read among the
“notices” at the amen end of the sermon. It was to
the effect that during the preceding month, in a
camp of 2000, only four drunks had been before the
Colonel. Then the congregation grinned behind its
hat, and Courtney is good for a unanimous cheer
from that 2000 whenever he says “Come on, boys!”
Australian Anglicans are still clinging to
the idea that the war will produce a religious
revival in this country, and to fan the
feeble flame they are planning “missions.
Local pilots will be cast for the minor parts
in the Queensland campaign, but the four
“stars” are to be imported from the Cold
Country. In the lead comes C. 11. S. Mat-
thews, who as “Brother Charles” of the well-
liked Brotherhood of the Good Shepherd
used to labor in the arid portions of the
vineyard between Dubbo and the Sunset.
A plain talker on social subjects, Matthews
made himself unpopular with “right-think-
ing” Anglican churchwardens who voted the
Liberal ticket, and eventually they raised
Cain to such an extent that he went “home.”
His crowning offence was to share a pub
balcony with a Labor candidate and join
him in exposing the fallacies of G. H. Reid’s
Anti-Sosh campaign. He has several books
to his credit, including his racy “Parson
in the Australian Bush,” and lately
has been doing his bit as a chaplain

in Flanders. Canon Peter Green, of
Manchester (Eng.), the big gun who
comes to support him, is also an inky
wayfarer, and grinds out a regular column
for" the Manchester Guardian over the
pen-name of “Artifex.” Green, who is an-

other two members of the revivalist troupe

Take off your hat to the memory of this
man
Died at Sydney, John S. Shearston, after a life
mainly spent in helping Jack to steer a straight
course while ashore. Born on Trafalgar Day 63 years
ago on the old Sydney “Rocks” —the district in
which he spent his whole life—Shearston began as a
clerk in the Mint, but slung his billet to have more
time for his hobby of taking care of Jack. In 1872
he started Trafalgar House, in Prinees-street, as a
home for navy men ashore, and from then onwards
“Johnny Shearston’s pub” at its various addresses
has been the safe port for which Jack
has beat up under bare poles after a
stormy passage through Sydney’s byways. In
course of time he amalgamated Trafalgar House
with a similar institution founded in memory of Com-
modore Goodenough, once in command in Australian
waters; and he ran the joint concern. In
1888 Henry Pqrkes and the Admiralty agreed to sub-
sidise an officially-recognised naval house, and Shears-
ton was the obvious man to place in charge. The
N.S.W. Government weighed out £lO,OOO and gave a
site in Grosvenor-street, and the present fine building
went up in 1890. Through good management
the place was popular from the jump, and is reckoned
the most successful of its kind in the world. Shears-
ton was made a “lay reader” by Bishop Barker in
1878, and studied for the Anglican priesthood, but
never took out his ticket. A childless man himself
he counted his “boys” afloat by tens of thousands,
and every mail brought him cataracts of letters
from them, the writers ranging from admirals to
stokers, and their addresses from Halifax to the
Falklands.

Tasmania’s Commissioner of Police,
Colonel Lord, has gone into khaki and is to
command the battalion which Tasmania,
with a hand from Victoria, is contributing
to the new army. He has long been keen
to go, but the State Government would not
let him off the chain before. This is his first
look at the real thing, though he has com-
manded the Derwent Infantry Regiment for
years past.
“309”: Colonel Quilter, the big, good-
hearted Grenadier Guardsman who used to
throw his shadow over G.-G. Denman, was
yarning with Australian artillerymen at
Gallipoli on the day he was killed, and Ms
tombstone was erected by Australians. Ser-
geant Bradwell, who is well known in Syd-
ney, and the writer cut the stone with our
pocket knives. We first of all prised out
the sill of the bread oven at “Zimmerman’s
Farm,” much to the disgust of a Frenchman
who was sleeping inside; but after all our
trouble we found it too hard to work, so
we ripped up a piece of the bedroom floor,
which was softer, and cut thereon: "In
memory of Col. T. A. C. Quilter, V.C., Bri-
gade-Major Wilson, and ,” a staff cap-
tain whose name I, the mason, forget.
Killed in Mesopotamia, a promising Aus-
tralian, Hugh G. Howes, who left Sydney
with the first expeditionary force as a ser-
geant in the 3rd Battalion and finished up
as a lieutenant with the 2nd Rajput Regi-
ment. In 1912, at the age of 17, young
Howes was in command of the 2Sth Batta-
lion of compulsory trainees who won that
year’s championship.
Gregg Macgregor, the Newcastle (N.S.W.)
parson who brought back a D.C.M. from
Gallipoli, has been given a step up by the
Church. In his curate days before the war
he harassed Satan as a mere deacon, hut
Bishop Stretch gave him his full priest’s
orders the other day at Newcastle Cathedral.
Colonel Baillie, who has occupied a seat
in Maoriland’s Leg. Council for 53 years,
fought at Chilianwalla, in the Second Sikh
War (1848-49). Another officer who sur-
vives is General de Barry, who is in Britain.
These two were in the 24th regiment. De
Barry is 91, and Baillie made merry on his
87th birthday a few days ago.
Dr. E. Angas Johnson, who inherited much
of the Angas money in S. Aus., owns prob-
ably the finest private museum in
the Commonwealth. He collects all
sorts of things of historic interest
old Australian relics, curios, documents,
books, coins, rare articles from many
parts of the world. His original letters from
past celebrities in England he would not part
with at any price. But he is no scientific
recluse : a public-spirited citizen, he keeps an
alert eye on all matters affecting the public
health, is a progressive city councillor, bono-
rary physician at the Adelaide Hospital, and
has examined thousands of recruities.
THE DREAMER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On an old tune that haunts my brain
These words seem borne to me again.
I sold my youth for a dream,
And the dream has been blown to dust,
My own heart’s gold, for a tale that is told,
I have paid, as a dreamer must.
And what of this dust of roses?
And what of the grief and pain?
I have no regret. I am dreaming yet
The old dream over again.
It seems as if a rough voice said
Across that old tune in my head:
“Ho! wot are you doin’ at Anzac,
You dreamer of gaudy dreams?
Wot are you doin’ at Anzac,
’Ere where the death-shell screams?
You an’ yer dusty roses!
You with yer blighted pain!
Wot are you doin’ at Anzac?”
I’m dreaming mg dream again.
Here in the dust at Anzac,
Here, where the shells fall free,
The dreams that died in the city
Are being re born for me.
My hope died out in the city
As sunset dies on a plain,
But here in Gehenna at Anzac,
I am making myself again.
ANZAC.
A. I’ANSON.

The half-year’s margin in excess of the
dividend is so narrow as to be almost
invisible. But it is probable that something
substantial was written off before the
accounts were given to shareholders. At
any rate, full provision has been made, the
chairman says, not only for the deprecia-
tion of securities as at August 31, but for
the further depreciation which was disclosed
by a valuation made just before the share-
holders’ meeting at the end of January. No
doubt this writing down was the proper
thing to do, because the bank might con-
ceivably have to market its investments.
Still, if it doesn’t have to sell them, it
doesn’t matter a straw, so far as income is
concerned, what they are worth in the
market. But having written them down so
that they can be sold without loss, the bank
is now in a position to exchange them for
gilt-edged securities bringing in 4f p.c. or
other high-class securities bringing in up to
6 p.c. In other words, though the visible
funds of this bank may be moving up hardly
at all —may even be less than they were a
year ago—that reduction is due to the higher
value of money; so that, unless it has actu-
ally to sell at a sacrifice, the chances are
that the bank is really much better off,
especially if some of its funds are used in
ordinary trading. Here, for instance, is a
bank with £2,000,000 of share capital and
(after paying current dividend) £2,030,528
of reserves. Of these sums only £2,161,103
is invested in public stocks. The balance is
used in the business; and it is probably
earning on the average 1 p.c. more than
the bank’s funds brought in 18 months ago.
Certainly at times like this a bank needs
to make extra profit in order to put some-
thing by for the losses that usually follow
a period of disturbance and high rates. It
isn’t certain what will happen to Australia
after the war. Only one thing seems sure:
money will not be available except at very
high rates. Some banks are already pre-
pared to pay over 5 p.c. for deposits for
upwards of two years, and the Victorian
Savings Bank is paying 5 p.c. for money
fixed for 10 years. In such circumstances
the stoppage of State loans is inevitable,
and it is going to give us a bad jolt. But
the big question is, How will our staple
products behave? Will our customers of
the past be able to buy our products? If
the demand eases off, as it is likely to, prices
must suffer. It is hard, no doubt, to judge
just what is likely to happen: the disturb-
ance is so vast, we can’t see the wood for
trees. Let us therefore diminish the num-
bers. Let’s make it a row between Bruce
Smith and Archbishop Wright. They start
throwing bricks at one another in August,
1914. Usually industrious tinsmiths, they
knock off work and keep throwing bricks at
one another all through 1915, and well on
into 1916. All this time they don’t earn a
bean. They have a bit saved up; they use
this in buying tucker and beer for them-
selves and the Smith and Wright families
and in buying bricks; and when they have
used up their ready cash they start giving
lOU’s to the local money-lender, who pays
the butcher and the baker and the brick-
maker ; and when the two want bricks at
the same time the man who makes them puts
up his price, and Bricky Brown, who works
in the kiln, demands an extra 2s. a day.
Then Smith and Wright pull themselves out
from under a pyramid of broken bricks, and
meet the pawnbroker. They have earned
nothing for two years; they have not only
been living but also wasting by pawning
future wages. Well, what wrill Smith and
Wright feel like? They have emptied their
pockets, they have a heap of lOU’s to meet,
and they are not, because of their hurts,
nearly as well able to earn money as they
were before August, 1914. The plain fact
is that Smith and Wright will be in for a
cold time; and we are Wright, multiplied
by several millions.

money owing to the public is represented
by cash, public securities or other liquid
assets.

The Standard, which does a considerable
marine-underw’riting business, ought to ask
the British navy to come and have a milk-
and-soda. True, the British navy hasn’t
done if all. Whenever the Potsdam madman
has broken out into worse Frightfulness
than usual, the underwriters have responded
by charging a bit more for war risks, and
they have managed to keep so well ahead
of losses that the cold eye of hungry
Treasurers is looking that way. Maoriland
hasn’t mentioned the appropriation of war
profits yet, but in England the drag-net is
already out, and the Commonwealth has a
bill on the stocks. Further, it may be made
retrospective. If so, this co.’s true profits
for the past year may turn out to be less
than they now seem to he.
The legislation foreshadowed in Australia
will take from the co. making the war profits
a percentage of the net earnings in excess
of the average of the three years preceding
the war. This will not be equitable in the
case of Australian and Maoriland insurance
cos., which have been steadily appealing to
the patriotism of the people of these parts
and getting a greater proportion of the
business offering. For instance, the average
premiums of this co. in the three years prior
to the war were £90,(124. whereas the pre-
miums for last year were £127.081. It is
plainly unfair that the co. should not be
entitled to make a greater profit out of
£127.081 of risks than it did out of £90,624.
Further, in the case of a small insurance co.
especially, a fair average profit cannot be
ascertained by taking only three years’
figures.
The splendid growth of this co.’s business
is shown in the next table: —

In England it is thought that an insurance
co. offers adequate protection to the policy-
holder if its funds (paid capital and reserves
combined) equal 165 p.c. of the year’s pre-
miums. The Standard’s funds are £245,225
and the year’s premiums £127,081, so the
former amount to no less than 193 p.c. of
the latter- —a position which ought to give
the policy-holders a very comfortable feeling.
Also it ought to calm some of the fears
which shareholders may reasonably have
when they remember that they are liable for
£9 ss. per share. They have paid up only
£75.000 and may be called on for another
£925,000.
The weakest point in the generally very
strong position is in connection with the
investments; no less than £116,915 is in
mortgages and £69,719 in a vague thing
called debentures. The mortgages are doubt-
less chiefly on Maoriland property; and in
the bad times which are very likely ahead
of us prices of our staples may fall heavily,
and with them will fall land values. The
co. has been earning exceptional interest for
many years on its investments, and it ought
out of those profits to have built up a for-
midable reserve for contingencies. So far
as can be seen these will have to be pro-
vided for either out of the ordinary reserves
or from current profits.
The Life Insurance Co. of Australia :

1915.. 36,892 22,986 10,277 33,263 16,517
It isn't easy to put the case in a table.
“Deficit, including goodwill” merely means
that the shareholders put up £44,000, of
which £7OOO was paid for the goodwill of
a little side-line insurance business and
£9517 has gone in etceteras. But that
doesn’t mean that the co. has paid its way
except for £9517. For four years the co.
has been issuing policies, and against the
policies still in force the office should have
accumulated considerable reserves. We
don’t know how much, and won’t know until
after the first actuarial examination at the
end of the current year.
The whole matter has to be looked at
with two different pairs of eyes—(l) the
shareholder’s, (2) the policy-holder’s. Take
the shareholder first. To bring the business
to its present position it has cost him the
£16,517 mentioned above plus whatever
amount should be to the credit of the insur-
ance fund—the reserve which must be accu-
mulated to meet policies as they mature.
Whether the business is woi’th this total
sum (£16,517 plus the amount required for
an insurance fund) The Bulletin doesn’t
know. No doubt policies which have bfeen
caught at great expense, and especially
those which have been in existence for a
couple of years, are worth money to a
proprietary insurance co.: they are the raw
material out of which it may make profits.
But yoH can’t possibly value them. For
one thing, though a proprietary insurance
co. may have the policies, it mostly depends
upon the co.’s management whether it will
eter make anything out of them. Usually
the plan is to take a small percentage of
the bonuses; and whether there are any
bonuses depends chiefly on the economy of
the management. In return for the right
to take some of the profits the shareholders
give the policy-holders certain guarantees.
There is no assurance that the whole capital
will not be thus absorbed. In the present
case a good deal of it has been absorbed.
The shares are therefore a gamble. With
good management and fair luck the prin-
cipal may all be restored and large profits
earned ; on the other hand, the co. may be
glad to crawl from the busiriess by selling
its policies to some other office and paying
shareholders a liquidation dividend of some-
thing or other in the £. or confining itself
to some other branch of the insurance busi-
ness.
The policy-holder for his part has two
things to consider: (1) “Can the office meet
my claim when it matures?” (2) “What
bonuses is it likely to pay, thus reducing
the net cost of my insurance?” In an
inquiry of that sort a young office is always
terribly handicapped. One can point to
the past record of an old office and show
that its management has more or less proved
itself. But with a new office one can only
trust; and in this life insurance one natu-
rally wants more than faith —one wants
proof. The only thing a new office can do
then is to say: “We are so confident in
ourselves that we will put up a guarantee.”
This co. has done that, to the extent of
£44,000; and shareholders have made them-
selves liable for another £27,500. This
seems to give policy-holders ample protec-
tion for the present. As to bonuses —well,
they may come by and bye. But obviously,
since bonuses ensue largely from economical
management, big distributions are practi-
cally impossible so long as a co. has to fight
hard for every policy it gets and is then
very likely to lose it within the first year
or two.
But while the shareholders have sub-
scribed a guarantee for the benefit of the
policy-holders, a large part of it is no longer
available for the benefit of the holders of
life policies. Besides the life (ordinary and
industrial) department the co. now has an
accident branch, and it has lodged the
necessary deposit to enable it to take work-
men’s compensation business in Victoria.
It also runs a provident department. The
capital is therefore no longer -wholly avail-
able -as a protection for the simple life
policies. It is doubtful w’hether an office
which takes life business should be permitted
to take any other kind of business what-
ever. But for that very reason it is pos-
sible that the co. may make a good deal
of money out of its side-lines. In other
words, these side-lines being more or less
speculative, a co. with luck may make a
punch that way.

During the past 12 months a pound or two
hasn't mattered in the shipping business.
It has been easy come, e-asy go. We have
been living partly on our own fat and partly
by discounting our future labors; and the
shipping people more than any others have
benefited by the demoralisation which always
accompanies a borrowing orgy. The year's
profits must not therefore be taken as a
trustworthy basis on which to estimate
future profits. If the actual profits were
the £21,890 disclosed, then the £lB,OOO divi-
ded for the year was on the liberal side.
It would have been much safer to put by
a larger sum than £3890 for contingencies.
The chief of these is the probability of a
severe shrinkage in the value of stocks
when the war is over. But, as a matter of
fact,* the, stocks in hand may have been
taken in at a very low valuation. There
is no way of telling whether it is so or not.
In the balance-sheet they stand at £122.545.
But it is quite possible that, before the
accounts were dressed for their annual
parade, the profit stood at. say, £26.890,
and the stocks at £127.545, and that, to be
on the safe side, £5OOO was written off
stocks to provide for accidents, which of
course would mean striking £5OOO also off
profits. These are things that you can't tell
from the surface of the published figures.
Anyway, that’s where the possible weak
point is. Look at the figures:—
4910. 1913. 1914. 1915.
Share capita].. £74, 652 £llO,OOO £120,000 £120,000
Suinlrv creditors 47,911 53,373 45,230 37,348
Premises . . 17,7 7 5 21,4 7 5 2 4,575 26,825
Stock-in-trade 84,6 7 5 1 25,09 7 1 21,293 1 22,54.)
Hook debts . . 26,375 35,209 38,808 36,324
Sundries .. 4,245 5,265 5,617 6,.13
Thus the entire capital is represented by
stock, which, because of high freights, high
labor and other charges, and bare markets,
has probably cost far more than it would
under normal conditions. The time may
come when, no matter what they cost, theco.
may have to realise its stocks at less even
than normal selling prices. Apart from that
possible bump in the near future, prospects
are good. While the co. has the advantage
of having no goodwill or other similar loan-
ing, it has undoubtedly a valuable connec-
tion, and things were steadily on the up-
grade even before the war sent them sky-
rocketing.

ALUMINOFF.RRIC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.
Sole agents: William Adams & Company Limited,
Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane and Perth. —Advt.
From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. pet-
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd.— a saving of 2s. sd.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.—Advt. [2

Working expenses, which ran up quickly a

a turnover of more than three times that
amount, without recourse to the bank. To
give a further little help the co. was able to
collect no less than £1892 in trade discounts.
# * * *
“N.” : Re the slackness in the coal trade.
“Jaques” (B. 16/3/T6) blames the extra
bob per ton charged by the colliery-proprie-
tors to make up for the increases paid to the
miners. The Maitland field, which supplies
a “fat” coal, suitable for making gas, is still
working fairly regularly. But the Newr-
castle district collieries, ■which furnish only
a steam coal, are going very intermittently ;
somewhere about half the pits are in opera-
tion, and then for something less than half
time. The inter-State markets still require
the gas coal, for which the freights were
contracted in many cases before the war;
anyhow, there is plenty of demand for it in
this and other States. For steam coal the
case is different. There are fewer steamers
running, and there is an embargo on export
to the American West Coast, as the stuff
is likely to get into the hands of commerce
raiders. In all directions freights have gone
up pounds compared with the shilling rise at
the pit. Which, then, is to blame for the
slackness—the miner who is getting a few
pence more a ton, or the shipowner who
has put up his price by pounds? The col-
liery proprietors, as a rule, are also steam-
ship owners, and as there is plenty of
demand for freight they are on velvet
whether their mines work full time, half
time or no time.
* * * *
It isn’t surprising that Australian shell
steel failed to satisfy the British War
Department. For some time past the
Engineer and Dr. Stead, F.R.S., have been
saying that the specification is too stringent.
Stead, after testing German shell steel, in-
sists that the Huns are far less faddy with
regard to the proportion of phosphorus, sul-
phur and carbon. He thinks that the
niechanical test for tenacity, etc., is all that
is required. Apparently the Hun relies more
upon that, too, than upon the chemical for-
mula. Some years ago a British metallur-
gist condemned Lithgow steel because it
contained a shade more phosphorus than the
technical limit. Hoskins stuck out that the
steel would stand the mechanical test, and
it is highly probable that the Australian
ironmaster was right.
* * * *
Carpathia Tin (N.S.W.) is so neglected
by the Stock Exchange that the present
market value is only £20,500. Last half-
year the directors distributed £5203, although
the real profit was less. Various faces at
the mine show high milling values, and dis-
patches of ore have been resumed. From
January 10 to February 17 1000 tons of
tailings were treated with 101 tons of ore
for 15 tons concentrates worth 63 p.c. and
35 tons inquiring secondary treatment. The
half-year closed with a credit of £1605. The
thing wants shaking up. It is no time to
leave tin in a mine when it can be rushed
out and sold for close on £2OO per ton.
* * * *
The N. S. Wales Government is adver-
tising for an engineer for its colliery. He
is to take charge of the erection of all plant
in connection with the development, to pre-
pare descriptions and estimates of the
machinery required, and generally to super-
vise the surface arrangements. All this at
the beginning of the concern ; and after coal
is raised he is to act as mechanical engineer.
And the salary offered is £4OO, with £5O as
allowance! Where on earth does the Gov-
ernment expect to find a man for £4OO who
is capable of undertaking all this initiatory
work? It is necessary to secure the best
brains at the start, so that money won’t be
wasted either in raising the coal or in in-
stalling machinery of the latest and most
efficient pattern, and it is absurd to expect
it for £8 per week. A private co., with its
own money to lose, w T ould know better than
to risk it on an engineer who_can’t command
more than that.
* * * *
Corinthian North (W.A.) hopes to have
a second roaster going in May and to make
the market buck up about the same time.
Recent work at the 100 ft. level is reported
to have exposed good ore for a width of
30ft. and a length of over 200 ft. The 200 ft.
level has been driven 200 ft., and a 30ft.
face is said to be ready for sloping. Most
of the shares are held by the Doolettes.
