Associated Northern had an annual roll-
up in London last month and put its cards,
so far as its Westralian and Mexican ven-
tures are concerned, on the table. Iron
Duke (Kalgoorlie) w T as stated to be not yet
in need of a coffin, the weakness of the
Victorious leases at Ora Banda was briefly
alluded to and the hope expressed that the
recently-acquired Slippery Gimlet (same
neighborhood) would give the co. badly-
needed ore reserves. The disturbed state
of the country still prevents the co. doing
anything with its Mexican show, but the
Board hopes to make a mine of it some day.
Meantime neither the co. nor its representa-
tives can get within cooee of the property.
The accounts show, with £3697 brought in, a
credit balance of £13,890; but with develop-
ment, diamond drilling and depreciation
chipped off only £3607 remains.
Developments at depth in the Commodore
(W.A.) don’t show anything of importance,
so the co. is holding on to a few thousands of
accumulated profits. The mine has been
persevered with quite long enough, and the
Board should seriously consider the ques-
tion of looking round for a new mine. At
present the co. is treating the 17,000 tons of
slimes on hand, worth about 14s. 6d. per
ton.
Great Boulder (W.A.) announces: Ore
reserves, 494,564 tons; approximate gross
value, £1,510,000. A year ago the estimate
was 560,647 tons, and although during 1915
almost 200,000 tons were extracted the mill
has only gained on it to the extent of 66,000
tons. According to official returns 195,524
tons were treated last year for a return of
£584,901. Divs absorbed £262.500. The co.
is still craning its neck for a new show.
* * * *
Edna May Deeps management threatens
to drag a Perth newspaper into court for
asking impertinent questions relating to the
survey of the bore which was reported to
have discovered 26ft. of ore. The paper
wanted the core produced for check assay,
and also a check survey made of the bore.
The Deeps management replied that such
demands were impudent, and the newspaper
invited it to prove them so. The public
interested in Westonia stocks are anxiously
awaiting developments. Meanwhile the man
in the street, who always knows more than
the people inside, is circulating the interest-
ing story that the portion of the core sup-
posed to contain the values has been pol-
ished as paper-weights for directors’ and
admirers’ writing-tables.
Three brokers and two laymen constitute
the Board of Leonora Gold Blocks (W.A.),
and in the prospectus the broker’s reticence
pervades everything. Seemingly one James
Cale sold to F. W. Were, who in turn
handed the show on to the Leonora Gold
Blocks Syndicate, represented by F. W.
Were, “Sandy” Oliver, Frank Savage and
lawyer Snowball. Now comes the Leonora
Gold Blocks Co., with a capital of 60,000
shares of 10s. cut up thiswise: 10,000 paids
to the vendor, 20,000 paids to the Syndicate,
and 30,000 at ss. offered to the public, 10 p.c.
of which goes to the underwriters, whoever
they may be. With vendors and promoters
taking half the shares, it doesn’t suggest
that Treasurer Higgs’s censorship of mining
floats is over-keen. The reason for the
float isn’t that £61,600 gold has been got
out of the claim down to 250 ft., or that the
reef is in tight country, but that Marvel
Loch Williams, after making a “careful and
searching investigation,” commends the
mine. He has been given control of the
show, and thus has the opportunity of prov-
ing the value of his forecasts.
* * * *
L. Hansen, who used to manage Birthday
Tunnel, now reigns at Ajax North, Dayles-
ford (Vic.), and should make good by intro-
ducing the cheap-working methods of Ber-
ringa. The mine is tipped to have a year’s
divs. in ore in sight, though Hansen’s report
doesn’t show where it is. He has eight
levels to play with, but the gold seems to
make best down from No. 6 to No. 7; the
bottom is of poorer grade. Costs run at
about 20s. per ton, including ss. 3d. for
development. So any stuff over sdwt. will
bring grist to the mill. There is a little
7dwt. dirt available for the stampers in No.
8 level, but not much. Ajax North is a
freak for having hit on a shoot of stone
just where it did ; but it has put out £39,000
of gold in about two years, and paid £lO,OOO
in divs. Going north at No. 5 level Hansen
owns up to a blank lode track, but he in-
tends pushing on further in search of fresh
shoots, and if he has any luck North Ex-
tended should be worth watching. Stopes in
No. 6 level only carry decent stone up
40ft., and the new make there has been hard
hit by a fault, on the fringe of which gold
doesn’t hang. Below that level the new
make has dropped into the main lode and
disclosed a shoot about 200 ft. long, and it
is from here that most of the current term’s
divs. will come. If prospects were only as
good at No. 8 level everybody concerned
would be smiling. As it is Hansen is going
shaft-sinking and crosscutting for all he’s
worth. His worst fear must be that Dayles-
ford, like Berringa, has yet to find a decent
mine below 1000 ft.
A tug-of-war is in progress at New Langi
Logan, Ararat (Vic.), over contract work.
Miners won’t have the system, on the plea
that it means sweating. What part the co.
will take in the argument depends a lot on
the value of the ground to be stoped. If it
carries 2oz. dirt it would be foolishness to
court a strike, but with loz. stuff two or
three months’ hold-up might be welcomed if
Bill Toole could keep the pumps at work.
But it is very unlikely that the engine-
drivers would remain at their job. A
general strike, of course, would mean the
swamping of the mines. The disagreement
threatens to be an awkward one for both
parties.
With a big gutter and 12dwt. ground to
work, Duke Extended, Ararat (Vic.), went
back £l4OO last half-year and seemed to have
a fair prospect of getting into the machinery-
man’s hands. Then shallow ground to the
nor’-west was touched. This led to a revival
of all the old yarns about the co. having
missed the gutter by branching away to the
north-east, instead of going dead ahead into
Morey’s paddock from the big hole in the
bend of the lead. Work to the west, there-
fore, is pure prospecting, but the game is
worth playing, as any development would
mean a new mine.
“D.F.”: The Tasmanian w r ater-power
scheme is going to boom if all the inquiries
made for power result in business. If all
the people who are asking for quotations
take what they ask for, the Government will
have to find over 130,000 horse-power. The
Great Lake scheme can ultimately supply
about 85,000, possibly more, but the new and
still optimistic Hydro-Electric Department
has got an idea that it may want more than
that before it is through, and is already
looking at Lake St. Clair and other possible
sources of 'supply. Besides the Amalgamated
Zinc there is talk of woollen mills, pottery
works and chemical works, and there are
inquiries about hydro-electric power for
working iron deposits. Of course some
of these things are right up in the air, but
some of them are likely to get down to dots.
The Gillies zinc process and the reported
big works at North-west Bay seem, by the
way, to have faded a little into the back-
ground since big Baillieu bustled in and his
Amalgamated Zinc Co. took the limelight.
* * * *
Tasmania is getting a little excited over
the prospect of having big zinc works estab-
lished there in connection with the State’s
new hydro-electric scheme. Two months ago
its hopes were fixed in Gillies; now it is
Baillieu who has swept the islanders off their
feet. Gepp, of De Bavay’s (Amalgamated
Zinc), who was hurried off to find a market
for the co.’s zinc concentrates when the
Belgian and German buyers failed, brought
back word of electrolytic success at Ana-
conda. It is on this that all the talk is
based. But nothing has been said so far
about costs; and that was the cockroach in
the butter last time. The last time was
when Ashcroft was able to put such figures
before Moreton Frewen, who put them
before the Gibbses, that the Sulphide Cor-
poration was formed, and a third of a mil-
lion was wasted at Cockle Creek. The costs
of power in Tasmania will be muck less, of
course; but until it knows much more than
it does at present Tasmania had better put
ice on its head. It is much better to wear
it there than around the feet just at present.
* * * *
Last year Cloncurry (N.Q.) produced
minerals worth £758,748. The Duchess is,
of course, the big mine, and its reserves are
now estimated at 80,000 tons. Last half-year
19,000 tons averaged 16.4 p.c. copper. With
the 850 ft. and 1000 ft. levels under develop-
ment, reserves may be replenished quickly,
but it will be a marvellous mine if it con-
tinues to average 16 p.c. So far only crude
ore has been smelted on the field, but
Hampden has large quantities of low-grade
oxidised ore at the Macgregor and low-
grade sulphides in all its mines ; hence the
future will see leaching from the oxides and
concentration of the low-grade sulphides.
* * * *
Last year Day Dawn P.C. was wound
up; now Mills United has decided on the
same course. Both worked the Day
Dawn reef, and between them paid over
£1,200,000 in dividends. It was a party of
Germans who opened the Day Dawn P.C.,
which was a big low-grade quartz formation
near the surface.

BOTTLED IN FRANCE.
How to Have a Clear
Complexion.
It is quite easy even in a hot climate like
Australia to prevent the wrinkling, drying
and blotching effect of the dust and heat, but
it is absolutely necessary to use a pure soap
without any added coloring matter or excess
of soda. Puritol Wax Soap is such
a soap, with just a little fine per-
fume added, and some Puritol wax,
which has such a imputation for keeping the
skin soft and for removing pimples and
blotches. A very fine film of the wax is left
on the skin after using this soap, which
effectually protects the skin from heat and
dust. Its healing effect is well known on
blotchy skins, and undoubtedly many of the
fine complexions are due to the use of Puritol
Wax Soap.* [1

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, March 27, 191 G.
My Dear Arini, —
A Conservatorium audience spent a recent
evening with the Verbrugghen quartet.
Mozart and Schubert were amongst those
present. Director Henri V., having waited
for the company to settle down, began his
discourse on music, good, bad and worse,
lie means to illustrate the last, as a Dread-
ful Example, in the near future. The lec-
turette having finished, the Master retired.
Then a vassal came forward to request that
“no knitting” should be part of the next
item. This blow was accepted in a spirit
of Christian fortitude. A few loud “Hear,
hears” came from those irritable members of
the long-haired tribe who don’t propose to
march to Tipperary. The most dramatic inci-
dent was the forerunner of a brilliant per-
formance of Schubert’s quartet, “Death a-nd
the Maiden.” The maiden —a student in azure
blue flounces —dashed on to the stage, and
began her “Touch me not!” ditty to Death
—presumably Nelson Illingworth at the
piano. This done the fiddlers got to work ;
and the work was admirable. Verbrugghen
and his tuneful three fit each other like the
pieces in a mosaic. It is not a world’s
masterpiece, but a small slice of perfect
harmony.
Why is rmisic so conducive to the garden-
rake coiffure? The first lecture-concert in
the Conservatorium brought together a record
assemblage of tousled locks. Even a curry-
comb would have done something for the
high thinkers. Two grey heads of beauti-
fully-dressed silky hair were an oasis in a
field of tangled haystacks.
Verbrugghen comes down with the weight
of a guilty conscience on the toes of the
concert-goers who glue their noses to the
score. He feels, as ordinary people do,
though they didn’t like to mention it before,
that the person with the music up against
his knee is losing the general effect of a
performance. In opera, for instance, the
score-reader is only concerned as to whether
the chief soprano expires on a high C or a
half-tone lower. Then there’s the rattling
of the leaves- —a special nuisance to adjacent
nen trals.
The three chubby-faced, cheery Tennyson
boys, who used to go out riding with Dick
Nevill, were the bright spots in a dreary
regime at Government House. The most
frolicsome of the lot. Harold, died lately
on active service. The Benjamin of the
family was a junior lieut. somewhere in
the North Sea when the call came. The
two older boys are both in khaki.
The temporary lull iu gift evenings is
due to the battalion ladies —or some of them
—wondering where they are. Battalions
and brigades and reinforcements are in the
melting-pot. No, I don't mean Egypt. The
week has been sprinkled with private cables:
“Transferred to this,” or that; “Battalion
renumbered” so and so. And there have
been other cables which were quite harmless,
but told all that w T as of any importance in
the heading. “I can’t tell you where I
am,” one ingenious young man wrote a while
ago, “because, being the Censor. I would
have to blot it out. But I can’t censor the
postmark.”
The Munro Fergusons, originally timed to
get here about Easter, came nearly three
weeks ahead of the first schedule. So the
covers of Admiralty House chairs were
hurriedly folded away last W'eek-end. His
Ex. makes forestry his hobby. Ilis own
place in Scotland is one of the best-timbered
20,000 acres in N.B. Since he settled
down here he has collected every kind of
Australian wood he can put his hands on,
and one room at the Federal humpy is as
much like a timber-yard as the sub-regal
woodman can make it.
Lady Helen is to open a new Free Kinder-
garten in the backwoods of Rozelle on April
12.
The State vice-royalty is. as usual, en-
trusted with the opening of the Easter
Show. It’s not a rosy job. It means, to
begin with, a solid midday meal, heavily
weighted with agricultural oratory, fol-
lowed by a brief competition in bellowing
against the beasts in the show ring. The
beasts usually win, and the crushed Excel-
lency’s concluding remarks are merely whis-
pered to the reporters.
Heaton Rhodes, the M.L. magnate who
has been doing time (just for love) at Red
Cross work in Egypt, is on his way home
to the mansion near Christchurch and the
famous garden where they talk of flowers
by the acre.
The Darnleys have given Cobham Hall,
in Kent, for the use of Australian wounded
during the war. She was one of the Morphy
family of Victoria before she stepped into
Dehrett by marrying cricketer Ivo Bligh.
All the sporty young Australians who
have got into khaki in England are not
going for commissions with the airmen. One
youngster, whose tastes lay more in shooting
’em on the wing, having won a commission,
is going through a course of anti-aircratt
gunnery. This lad, Fitzgerald, has a
mother in Launceston (Tas.) ; but a year
or two ago he was one of the brilliant boys
at Riverview, on our own Lane Cove.
The spinning-wheel (nearly called it
jenny) is now a household pet in woolly
homesteads. At Glanmire Hall, the Ivatts’
bis humpy beyoud Bathurst, they spin the
wool off tlieir own mutton. An ingenious
station-hand not only scours the fleece but
dyes it khaki. The obliging gumleaf sup-
plies the mixture.
Moyra Maclntyre, a Sydney girl—she’s a
daughter of Donald Mac, who lias been in
England for a while —is to marry young
Atkinson Willes. He’s a dashing lieut. in
the Hussars. But Hussars have now to
dash in khaki instead of in crushed-straw-
berry breeches with an upper terrace of
gold ropes. Willes is a son of an admiral,
and mother used to sign her name Mort.
She belongs to the old Sydney family that
still grows bacon and cheese down Bodalla
way.
Adrian Knox, Iv.C., is putting in over-
time telling us about the socks-and-pyjamas
side of war. He went up to Killara fete
to help tlie War Chest, and improved tlie
shining hour with Egyptian reminiscences.
Mrs. Budden, wife of the man who keeps
count of War Chest comforts in the sandy
kingdom, was in charge of the piracy.
Genial Captain McArthur, of tlie E. and
A. line, crossed the bar the other night. The
sudden call came in his own home at Rusli-
cutter’s Bay. The sorrowing widow used to
he Miss Annie Brownlow. In the days that
are dust the Brownlow family lived at
Eaton, the big square house with green
ficus whiskers, which the Fosberys after-
wards moved into.
Bernard Tripp, one of Canterbury’s squat-
ters, goes off to Bharaoh’s country next
month to look after M.L. parcels and com-
forts that have been astray mostly since the
strife started. He travels on his own cheque.
The Women Bainters’ show is still
patiently angling for public support, and
will keep its line out till about the second
week in April. Till then the wares will
hang against the crushed-mulberry walls in
the Education palace. Visitors are putting
Betty Solomons on their list of promising
artists. Gertrude Williams tosses off a
charming Norwegian sketch. E. M. Scott
sees the cheerful red blob ou a quaint bit of
old Bruges. Mabel Barling sticks to the
Zoo for inspiration, and prices at 80 guineas
a picture in which tlie lion is leading gentle-
man.
Australian soldiers go the whole hog with
regard to their commanding officer. If they
like him they keep on telling him so. If it’s
the other way about —well, the O.C. is
equally well informed. The welcome-home
to Colonel Burnage, of the 13th Battalion,
was starred with the flattering remarks of
the boys. Randwick Hospital had given
them an evening out. Some of them were
so afraid of being late for the welcoming
that they hobbled off to catch a tram before
the motors arrived to rush them easily to
town.
Burnage, C. 8., is a tall, slender man with
a still-useless left arm —a souvenir of Gal-
lipoli—and a smart, soldierly presence. Some
of the 13th Comforts Fund ladies w r ere in
charge of the Wentworth Hotel winter-gar-
den party. The welcome from the khaki visi-
tors was so vociferous that Captain Brache
—also damaged in the ISth’s Gallipoli rush
—had to use the serpent’s guile. To enable
Cullen, C.J., to finish reading the Batta-
lion’s address to the guest, Brache would
occasionally call a halt for three cheers.
That gave them a chance to all let off steam
together. Amongst the mothers of the Bat-
talion present w'ere Mrs. Louis Henderson
(her boy enlisted, and is now a captain at
the Front) ; Mrs. Stevens, wife of the
Farmer and Settler’s editor; and Mrs. A.
Fox. The address rolls up in a wallet —a
handier thing than a framed and glazed
trophy. It shows watercolor bits of the
peninsula that tlie 13th isn’t likely to forget.
Dean Talbot reappeared with his copper-
haired lady at this party. Fie stuck to his
silk hat for some time, but finally removed
it (yet not to sing its Maker’s praise) as
his gaiters confronted the State’s vice-excel-
lency. Albert Edward, wlio looks more like
a smart young commercial traveller than a
cleric, lias come back from Gallipoli with a
velvet finish on his former slightly aggres-
sive manner.
Wireless w T ill tell Melba of her father’s
death. Brobably the Niagara’s skipper will
have the wretched business of breaking the
news. Our skylark takes her emotions m
desperate earnest. She was devoted to the
old dad who has just passed out in Mel-
bourne. The thing has happened that she
feared —lie has died and she absent fiom bis
bedside.
A one-time S’Australian, Ethel Maud
Brookman, recently said she would to Bat
Kirkpatrick, at Sukkur, India. The groom
is in the Indian police. The bride s parents
have been domiciled in London . since,
striking it rich in gold, they said good-bye
to the vine-growing State.
The autumn show of the Horticultural
Society filled St. James’s Hall with dazzliug
color; for, in spite of a fine_ tally of roses,
in was the day of the brilliant dahlia.
Heavily-whiskered enthusiasts gasped joy-
ously at the wonderful evolution of our old
friend whose skirts were tightly drawn
about its sides. The gorgeously-plumaged
cactus —its modern rich relation —was a
notable exhibit in which F. C. Love grove
and Sons gathered in a hatful of prizes.
The meek bouvardia’s first prize exhibitor
was Mrs. E. Sliortus. C. T. Gosper showed
the rich man’s pet. the orchid. Its mauve
velvet frock, flecked with deep amethyst,
always tempts the brush of Mrs. Ellis
Rowan. As it has been ever since time
began, the patriarchal Bradley was over-
seer-in-chief of the show.
With the “Beg-o’-my-lleart” Co- that’s
coming for inspection here at Easter, a Syd-
ney maid will line up. Beatrice Yaldwin
returns after years spent in getting hall-
marked abroad. A niece of the expensive
Mrs. Buckland Taylor, who used to belong
to Queensland and now inhales the Fog, Miss
Yaldwin got the needful aid to stagecraft at
Tree’s Dramatic School. Another aunt of
note, Mrs. Dudley White, of Vic. Barracks,
is doing her hit at the Rose Hall Soldiers’
Lodge, Darlinghurst, and directing a rifle
corps between shifts.
A Gunnedali man. W. B. Heffernan, is
engaged to May Nash, of Grenfell. lhe
Best Girl’s grandpa, the late James Tunney,
was a well-known N. S. Wales squatter in
the early days.
The death of tlie gentle Mrs. Deas-Thom-
son —an invalid for years—is queerly an-
nounced. The complacent little gentleman
who survives is surely responsible for the
following: “Sophia, wife of Edward Richard,
eldest son of the late Sir Edward Deas-
Thomson, C. 8., Iv.C.M.G.” After this, there
is a mention of the late lady’s father. He
was William Lawson, one of the pioneer
family, of Veteran Hall. Brospect. The
announcement is so heavily weighted with
the grandeur of the Deas-Thompsons that
it reads as if the late Will Lawson was 75
years of age. But it must refer to the de-
parted and highly-esteemed Sophia.
St. Ignatius College had a breezy summer
afternoon for its 30th annual regatta. A
mosquito fleet of small craft buzzed up the
Lane Cove for the event, and an inter-
mittent steamer-service picked up and
dropped passengers at the College wharf. The
late afternoon picking-up—for inter-river
passengers—was of the kind that drove des-
perate women to interview" astonished priests
with such questions as: “Are you the traffic-
manager?” One of the clerics was better
than that. He turned a sympathetic ear and
enlisted the help of private launch-owners.
The race of the afternoon, the unclassified
club eights, made a swift spider’s progress;
and the friends of the Leichhardt rowing-
men threw up their hats at the finish. A
Government launch, with the George Blacks
and the College chief, T. Gartlan, S.J., on
board, pulled up to collect more passengers
at the little wharf. Judge Backhouse’s
cherubic countenance showed under a
panama leaf in this politicians’ craft.
The fragile John Shearston, Superinten-
dent of the Naval House, died in the pre-
mises he loved so well. For years he looked
as if a squally southerly would blow him
away; but a dogged determination to hang
on to the work of making Jack happy in
his Sydney home kept the shore-skipper at
the helm. Ordered by various doctors at
various times “to get inland and stay there,”
Shearston only shook his head, took a week
or two amongst the gumleaves, and hurried
back to his boys.
What an illuminating record of admirals
who have come and gone Shearston could
have written! Beaumont w r as the toughest
proposition he had to handle. That out-of-
date autocrat snarled at what he called the
luxury that “spoilt” the sailors in the Naval
House. Sanded floors and deal tables, ship’s
biscuit, a tot of rum and the hard tack that
did in Marryat’s day were Beaumont’s
notion of what agreed best with Jack. John
Shearston, however, generally found the
Admiral’s lady on the side of the “spoiling” ;
and she served as a buffer between Jack’s
shore-godpa and her own autocratic sea-
lord. Before her marriage she was one of
Boston’s beauties.

That very mixed coterie of women wlm
belong to the London Lyceum Club seem
to be covered with cobwebs. They have
just refused to accept trained nurses as
members. An Australian correspondent
apologising for this pitiful snobbery, makes
matters worse. “Australian nurses, at anv
rate in peace time, have a higher social
status than their English sisters,” she savs
Yet even London W. has heard of Florence
Nightingale, and she was as English is
they make ’em.
Miss Birch, who was resident school-
marm to the Strickland kindergarten for
eight years, is now in Victoria attending to
the Stanley youngsters’ struggles with
’ri th me tic.
There are compensations for the blast of
the patriotic furnace. The Queen’s Club,
which frankly amuses itself all the year
round, plays bridge for various battalions.
This week it gets out the counters for a
“fund” party organised by Mrs. Tom Rolin.
Lady Jersey, now the Dowager Countess,
has broken out in a fresh literary effort.
Only a countess would dare to give the
modern world (which loves brevity) a long
title like this: “The Surprising Story of
John Alexander and the Little Man.” The
most surprising thing to the critics is that
it is not quite so dull as they expected.
The opening session of the competition
for Grace Bros.’ Conservatorium scholar-
ships begins forthwith. Minister Griffith
took the chair at the first event.
Mrs. Lyster Ormsby—who was Ynys Sut-
ton Tatham a while ago—-has been thrilling
the ladies’ Chatriotic Club with tales of
tlie fresh-egg scramble in France. Helping
in a soldiers’ canteen for months, part of
her daily round, which began at 6 a.m„ was
to fry countless eggs for hungry Fran-
cois. British peeresses, who rushed the lady-
cook billets in the early phases of the war,
soon found that the voluntary-aid business
was horribly greasy.
The war may, indirectly, raise the status
of cooks. Girls who used to turn up their
pretty little noses at such things as chops
and steak are now turning up their sleeves
to grill the same. The voluntary aid who
handles raw beef in a convalescent hospital
is on the track to give her prospective hubby
a well-fed expression. Rose Hall (Dar-
linghurst) has done itself very well with the
help of a competent lady cook. Now The
Mill, the Throsbys’ old home near Moss
Yale, is a haven for invalid soldiers, and
Sylvia Waugh, the practical lass who wanted
t > take a temporary job as tram collector,
is its lion. chef.
The old Spanish comb, or its modern imi-
tation. appeared here last year at a fashion-
able assembly in the Town Hall. Since then
it lias gone on appearing with such per-
sistency' that now it sticks out askew under
every' other feminine hat. The big curved
comb —you see it in the Canaries —is meant
to bolster up the fleecy mantilla. Hence its
wild gyrations when thrust under a hard
straw thatch.
A former Sydney girl. Margaret Cramp ton,
married an Englishman named Herbert rois-
ter a few weeks ago. The English winter
provided the usual January weather.
The brigandiers who command the Old Cold
and Silver Fund are thinking of having a
bargain counter-sale every day. At prese
they dispose, at slaughter prices, of anei
jewellerv. etc., once a week. The o
morning, at Baling’s, the buyers concen-
trated their best adjectives ou filigree s
goods. Generous- citizens who help the
chest by tearing the rings off their &.
still maintain a fairly steady dribble o
kets to meet the demand.
Yours affectionately,

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, March 27, 1910.
My Dear Yarrie, —
On Friday last Melbourne searched its
pockets for small change to silence the Hen-
nessy dame. Boys with scarlet and white
banners carried the invitation round town:
“Come to tea at the Town Hall.” And
there was another yearning note: “Tea all
day at the Town Hall.” The portico and
main stairway were packed from 11 a.m.
with a swarm of females, fighting as bravely
as soldiers for the shilling tickets that
admitted the owners to the tea and eatables
within. One of the Godfrey girls had a
vegetable pitch at the foot of the steps, under
a big striped umbrella. Mrs. Tallis held
the windy Town Hall corner, garbed in black-
and-white stripes and surrounded by apples
presented by country patriots. The cases
came along so fast that the stall girls won-
dered desperately how to get them brought
up from the railway. A first-line matron
who pulled her car alongside was asked:
“Would you bring some apples frorp the
station?” The dame recoiled amidst the
cushions. “ What! Apples in my new
car? Certainly not!" She wouldn’t think
of being seen abroad in the company of one
apple —much less a case of them. There’s
nothing of the “true daughter of Eve” about
an expensive matron in a costly motor-car.
Vice-regal figureheads have now taken to
killing time together. The other day, the G.-G.
and one-G. Stanley linked arms and went off
to inspect the Co-operative Fruit Company’s
place at Mildura, which is now humming
with war orders. Ben Chaffey later con-
ducted the two illustrious factory-inspectors
to his Moorna homestead at Wentworth, and
showed them what wonders a 10-acre-blocker
on the Murray’s banks could work with irri-
gation. While the two men were express-
ing polite astonishment in unison, their wives
went down together to Buchan and installed
a Bush Nurse who had already installed
herself and done much good work. Still sher
good-naturedly found a spare hour or so
for the little function, and the locals
applauded the visitors and then showed them
the famous caves.
The Ferguson and Stanley dames adroitly
timed their return from the country ti’ip for
Friday night, driving through town when the
Hennessy Button Day was all over. Their
husbands got back from the North on Thurs-
day and had to do the round of the kiosks,
swallow much tea and make speeches at the
Town Hall.
The Quamby Clubbers indulged in some-
thing they called an Egyptian Bazaar on
Saturday afternoon and evening. The
women dragged out their old Kismet Ball
dresses from tissue paper and scent sachets,
and arrayed themselves till their menfolk
dashed down their papers and kicked up a
row. The widowed Bridges dame declared
the show open, and crowds of returned sol-
diers helped all they could. Secretary
Miss Rennie had a barrowload of jewels
hung about her. It would have done the
heart of any burglar good to have had a
peep. Tickets were very carefully doled
out, only the suburban peerage gaining
admission. The spoil went to the Wounded
Soldiers’ Club. The heavily-respectable
carnival was as stodgy as one could desire,
though stout dames waddling in silk
trousers, amid incense fumes, seemed to
think they were doing SO miles an hour.
Young Franklin Peterson was the one male
in a batch of sweet young secretaries. He
varied his secretarial duties by raffling a
small dog—the sort you don’t treat as a
dog.
The Mount Schank Clarkes wore gorgeous
clothing as usual, Roma being the prize item
in a cross between Egypt and America. The
Argyles and the Outhwaites shoved in the
crowd, and Mrs. Carr Riddell wore diamonds
and black. The show of jewels was start-
lingly good. If the Crown Prince hears
about it he will pack up his gold-embossed
jemmy and start for Australia at once.
Rumor mutters that pianist Percy
Grainger is an idol in New York, outshining
even the long-established Paderewski in
popularity. Well, Percy deserved to get on.
He was the most teachable and persevering
little boy in knickers that ever infant-pheno-
menoned. What his German master told him
was law. He had no childish ways of look-
ing at things. When he had finished a per-
formance at Melbourne Exhibition Building
in the long, long ago, a gushful matron
stroked his mop of yellow hair and asked
him which of his pieces came most easy to
him. Percy looked reprovingly at the frivo-
lous inquirer and said, “Nothing is easy."
Stella Lewis Marks, Australian miniatur-
ist, has been taking tea with the Very Best
in Canada. On February 10 the Duke and
Duchess of Connaught gave a ducal luncheon
party, and Stella was an honored guest sit-
ting at the right hand of his Grace. (Sounds
almost Biblical, doesn’t it?) Our old friend
Dick Nevill sat next and told everybody
in the loudest possible voice that Melba
dubbed Stella “the Cosway of the Twentieth
Century.” (Note. —Look up Cosway in the
directory.) She is now engaged on a minia-
ture of Princess Patricia, the prettiest thing
in Britain’s royal family, who is posing in
pearls that were given her by grandmamma
Victoria. The artist showed several por-
traits in the Women Painters and Sculp-
tors’ show in New York during February.
Husband Montagu Marks, who does some
painting on his own account, is with his
wife.
Another Australian music-maker driven
home by war conditions is pianist Mabel
Muntz, who spent six months in Berlin just
before Germany broke loose. When the
crisis came the pianist had the luck to be
in Norway on holiday, and so escaped to
London. She is a bachelor of music from
the Melbourne University Conservatorium,
and in 1913 won one of the Orient scholar-
ships then for the first time handed to the
University’s brightest. When the Australian
girl got back to England from Germany
she filled in a year and a half trying to
broaden her soul by getting acquainted
with Russian music. Now she is a hot-
gospeller forv Russian culture, the Russian
concerto and Russian opera. This, coming
from a distinguished product of the Ger-
many-obsessed Melbourne music-school, must
be ill news to the present Ormond Professor.
Mabel is a daughter of a well-known Mel-
bourne roads-and-bridges expert, and there
are quantities of brains elsewhere in the
family.
The light has gone out for one bright,
smart little bride of last year. Maudie
Grantham, who married Bertram Graham,
a colonel of the Queen’s Own Corps of
Guides, India, has been informed that lie
was killed on March 11, at Latema Hill, in
British East Africa.
The semi-rural soldiers’ hospital at Caul-
field, built on a 25-acre block at the crest of
a, gentle slope, was put together under the
joint supervision of Horace Mackennal and
Major Murdoch. Glen Eira, the old house
around which the wings have been
built, is to be used for the nurses; and the
hospital itself —a series of “hut” wards —
will have a capacity of 520 beds. The pro-
perty, which used to belong to squatter
Ricketson, was bought by Home Affairs with
funds rounded up mostly by the State-school
children. 1
Stanley Argyle, X-ray artist with the
A.A.M.C., has finished his furlough, and is
off again for the Front. In peace time
Major Argyle’s practice is round town ; but
the family is well remembered by old Kyne-
ton. Reginald for some years represented
the purer Kyneton merinoes in the Vic.
Assembly.
The sewing-circle run by the Embling
widow at The Marsh, St. Kilda, has adopted
the making of kid-glove waistcoats for sol-
diers as its particular hobby. The discarded
cover, dismembered and multiplied by 20,
provides a light chest-protector when pro-
perly treated, and the demand for the com-
pleted product now considerably exceeds the
supply.
A girl who has just made the voyage to
England writes back advising a she-friend
to take a library of dainty pyjamas if she
is travelling that way. The tripper says
that she was requested to wear pyjamas
nightly from Suez to England instead of the
flowing garment. So that impossible old
Von Tirpitz has merely altered the fashions
with his torpedoes.
Mrs. Herbert Syme is surrendering her
Kew house and grounds for a fete on Satur-
day, April 8. Bags of every description are
to be sold to help the outburst, and Mrs.
McDevitt has offered a prize of a guinea for
the most original one sent in to the Ladies’
Work Association before the festive date.
But it won’t be all bags. A complete model
kitchen is to be raffled.
Fashionable Macedon is to have a Red
Ciossing on April 8, for which date Maggie
Stirling and Boffard are plotting a concert.
Maggie is hanging her half of the proceeds on
the local branch of the Red Cross, and
Boffard hands over his to the Macedon
Y.M.C.A. The Hodges are putting Boffard
up, and Mrs. Bawdon has Maggie Stirling
as guest.
Sylvia Little, a South Yarra belle who has
both beauty and a bank balance, was the
other day married to James Webb, a Cana-
dian doctor who, after the honeymoon, in-
tends carrying on the bone-setting industry
at Northcote, where he has bought a practice.
Bookmaker Barney Allen is now the proud
father-in-law of a baron, hight Gerald
George De La Riviere, who last week went
through the Wilt-thou-I-wilt business with
Barney’s pretty daughter Doris. The baron
is rumored to own large pieces of Banana-
land. He is 24, and Doris has only lately
put her hair up.
Captain Guy Miller, killed in action some
months ago, will be only a legendary hero-
father to the small son who was born last
week in the dark of London. The cable
reports mother and child both well.
Having our kultur, too, we are acquiring
a Music Club, my dear, which hopes to
“encourage love of music” and study _ the
works of eminent practitioners, especially
those of British composers.” # There will be
lectures, recitals, and a lending library ot
musical works. Fritz Hart, of the
servatorium, who does the concert notices
for the Age. is a moving spirit and a vice-
president; Melba is president, and a com-
mittee on which Art and lucre are about
equally represented has been formed.
Fritz Hart is inviting folk to a lecture
recital” on Friday, March 31, at 8.15. Hal
Elvins is to play, and somebody else is to
explain all about it. At least, I think
that is the idea.
When the Bert Bailey co. puts on “The
Message from Mars” the appearance of
Albert himself in the heavenly-visitor part
is not to be looked for. He widy lazily roll
over to see the first-night production, but
just now he is taking it easy in the sun
amongst the chickens at his Lake I arm at
Morrisset (N.S.W.). The footlight selector
has just about roared himself hoarse as Dad
at one-night stands in two States.
The vice-regal Stanleys quietly opened a
show of water-colors in the Fine Art Society’s
Rooms, at the Centreway, the other day.
The works are by Charles? M. Here and
Francis Dodd, of London. There are 50
paintings altogether, and they are all worth
looking at. Gere is best known in London as
a portrait painter. The Stanley dame has a
portrait of herself by him, and a head of a
child named Adelaide. She brought both
and had them put in the Exhibition as fine
examples of his delicate style. The picture
of the pretty little lady herself looks like a
Rossetti. Dodd has a heavier method, and
gets his finest effects in a species of color-
frenzy.
Cousin Adelaide’s wicked gossip : —■
Races at Port Adelaide, an outburst of Irish
enthusiasm in the streets and at the Oval, and the
first of the Ashmead Bartlett lectures, provided really
wild dissipation on Saturday. At the races soldiers,
many of them war-worn, were in the proportion of
about four to one. Which reminds me that the first
Australian training farm for returned Kangaroos is
an established fact. Of course we’ve taken the lead.
Returned men who work there will get ss. a day
and their keep, and will put in from three to 12
months’ training. We’ve established it at Pompoota.
When I’ve got time I’ll apologise for the name.
The outgoing mail steamer took E. A. Wilcox,
who is booked for honorary Red Cross work in
Egypt.
Major Darvall has stepped into the Commandant’s
shoes here. He’s a Bananalander by birth, arrived
in Brisbane in ’73, and has soldiered in all the
States except Tasmania. For about 10 months he
was in charge of the camp at Broadmeadows.
The Mount Lofty flower show, which is one of
our best-scented fixtures, broke a few of its own
records in spite of a summer so dry that we
wouldn’t know the drought was over unless we were
told so. Mrs. E. C. Stirling was responsible for the
“opening,” vice the Bonython dame, who was busy
being ill. Professor Stirling, by the way,. has been
appointed to the Public Library Board in Professor
Jethro Brown’s place.
A will providing for the development of “kauri
and other valuable forests, for establishing forests of
hundreds of varieties of bambusa and filling them
with local and foreign song and useful bird life in
the districts of Australia,” has been occupying the
attention of a harassed Court. The estate is to be
used for that purpose for 20 years, and afterwards
is to be divided amongst various legatees. Unfortu-
nately it is only worth £5OOO. The Chief Justice
has summed the scheme up as one for the establish-
ment of a “beautiful plantation in Central Australia
in an inaccessible locality to provide a home for
birds.” Incidentally, of the committee appointed to
deal with the matter, three (Dr. Holtze, Mr. W r . Gill
and Mr. H. Chewings) have washed their hands of
the whole affair, and the fourth (Mr. Caldwell) is
dead. I’m afraid the birds will never build their
nests in the whiskers of the legacy.
Lieut.-Col. Maurice Hankey, who has got a
K.C.B. and has been made an officer of the Legion
of Honor for distinguished conduct in the war, can
be partly claimed by this State, seeing that his
father held land in the North and his mother was
one of the Bakewells—a daughter of the late Mrs.
W. Bakewell, of Payneham.
Our motor-’buses are going into temporary retire-
ment, partly owing to the price of petrol and partly
because they don’t like our roads. Also someone
says the management was wrong and the type of
’bus was no good. Generally you’ll conclude that
the carburetter has boiled over.
Constance Waugh writes sweetly from
London to a faithful Melbourne friend about
parties for convalescent soldiers, Red Cross-
ings and other good works. Rupert is doing
war-work handy.
The Loongana, running from Launceston
to Melbourne last week-end, shared a droll
scare with the Kakapo, of the Union line.
Each took the other for a German cruiser
and puts its lights out. A lot of passengers
on the Loongana were in a state of appre-
hension, and King O’Malley, who lost his
pince-nez overboard whilst looking for
trouble, prepared himself for the worst.
The Alec McCullochs, of Adelaide, are
bringing niece Eileen Beresford to Mel-
bourne for her trousseau-gathering. Eileen’s
grandfather was a first cousin of Admiral
Charles, and there are quite a lot of the clan
scattered hereabouts. Her fiance is a wealth.)
Adelaide doctor.
George Chirnside, who convoyed daughter
Audrey to Egypt and then on to London,
just waited to welcome Mrs. George. Then
he said he’d better go and see what the
Labor people were doing, and took ship for
Australia.
Elinor Thornhill and Lieutenant 11. L.
Swinburne, son of the Gisborne Swinburnes,
have decided that one surname will do them
both in future.
The Best dame’s carnival raged for three
days at the Canterbury Gardens, finishing up
late on Saturday night. It was like all the
other carnivals that ever happened in his-
tory. I won’t even know if I attended it
until I look at my engagement ledger. If
one of the side-lines had been the burning
of a suburban mayor at the stake I might
have remembered the show and set it apart
in my mind. Why can’t the organisers of
these things show some originality and give
a literary bud a chance?
After waiting for years, Florence Ilood,
the modest violiniste daughter of the Vic-
torian Judge, has just made her London
debut in a big and encouraging wav at
Albert Hall, with Landon Ronald’/ orcht
tra. I lorence was severely handicapped bv
the same Landon, who insisted on her plav
ing at a fortnight’s notice a difficult concerto
she had never tackled, likewise refused her
a rehearsal, and also made her get on terms
with a new violin because he thought the one
she brought hadn’t lungs enough f or the
monster edifice. She made a hit in spite of
these trials, and may be said definitely to
have arrived—if there’s much in that when
some of the most famous British musicians
are clutching eagerly at the casual guinea or
guinea and a half.
Wellington (M.L.) recently parted reeret-
fully with Natalie Allen, B.Sc., a clever girl
who bagged her degree at Victoria College
in the home of the winds. She comes to this
State to hold the job of demonstrator in
Natural Philosophy at Melbourne Uni.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Moana” : The prosperous cookies of North
Canterbury (M.L.) awoke the other morning to find a
lot of their best horses in the grip of a strange epi-
demic. The nags had been attacked by a type of
paralysis in the legs that caused them to fall down
in agony and snuff out. The symptoms are similar
to tetanus without the lockjaw, and the sickness is
highly infectious. When I was out there just now the
cockies were leaping frantically in the direction of
the telegraph office with urgent wires to Premier
Massey asking him what he intended to do about it.
Probably the first thing he'll do is to try to raise
a few quid on the disease at the pawnshop.
* * * *
“Blank File”: Let me whisper at the top
of my voice to “Two Seas” (B. 9/3/’l6) that pigs
when swimming do not cut their throats. Recently
a full-grown son of the Kaiser fell off a steamer in
Sydney Harbor, and for over five hours swam round
Circular Quay and adjacent regions. After dodging
the ferries and exciting the interest of everybody who
crossed the harbor, the Crown Prince was landed
on the Mosman ferry wharf in good order and con-
dition, without the slightest sign of a wound on
neck or anywhere else. In brief, he brought home
the bacon unimpaired. •
“Mulga”: I’ll vouch for “Barcoo’s” re-
marks on the rosewood of Bananaland (B. 24/2/’l6),
excepting when he claims it to be the straightest tree.
In this respect it will not bear comparison with the
ironbark that surrounds Barakula, the timber village
out from Chinchilla on the Western railway section.
Euclid's axiom of a straight line can be applied to
many specimens up to 80ft., while frequently the
trunk has no projecting limbs lower than 100 ft.
Practically the whole of the timber required for rail-
way purposes on the Western line comes from this
part, a fine sample of its gun-barrel straightness
being seen in the large piers that comprise the
Warra coal stage.
* * * *
“Franklin”: 1 cannot say whether other
instruments act similarly, but, for telling 24 hours
beforehand of the breaking of the local drought, all
the rain prophets that were ever born are not in it
with my upright grand. As soon as the touch
becomes crisper and the sound more “singing” than
usual, I can guarantee that if I’m out of bed early
enough I 11 see the milkman coming down the street
with the lid off his can.
“D.F.”: A Swan Hill snake had some
fun with a party of Victorian motorists the other
day. The serpent allowed the newchums in the car
to see it lying on the road ; but, when they got out
to do violence to it, it slithered rapidly to the car
and, uttering a wild laugh, undulated into it. The
motorists weren’t travelling with snakes that day,
so they wearily hoofed the eight miles into the near-
est town, while the driver gingerly steered the
machine after them. Arrived in town the whole
party got crowbars and things and turned the motor
inside out, finally discovering the sausage of death
under the hood. It died with a smile on its lips,
waving its hands and assuring its persecutors that it
had intended to commit suicide anyhow.
* * * *
“Tropicus” : Amongst the mountaineers of
the Owen Stanley Range (Papua) a form of religion
exists which is based on the worship of the dead,
combined with cannibalism and various horrid rites.
Every human is alleged to be the home of many
spirits in the various phases of life, the spook who
occupies his body as a babe giving place to that of
the boy, and in turn being succeeded by various
other older spirits. These spooks are supposed to live
in the jungle, and are greatly feared by all the dis-
colored brethren, for some are bad and liable to
cause trouble to the person they enter. Often I have
heard the blame for a crime handed to the wraith
who was alleged to be in possession of the criminal’s
body when he committed the offence. Consequently
the average hillman dislikes to sleep with his mouth
open, because a hostile spook might take possession
of his body while his resident spirit was away visit-
ing his brother spooks. Sleep is the period when the
spook in residence has time off. Hardly any decent
mountaineer will ever wake another man without
first gently calling to the sleeper and gradually rous-
ing him so as to allow the wandering spirit time
to return into the sleeper’s body. An old maneater
once informed me that his son was a good warrior
and a killer of men because he had the same spirit
that attended him when he (the 0.m.) was a young
buck. This belief in spirits has, of course, been the
means of making sorcery a fine art, for some of the
more intelligent men, who wish to live easily, con-
vince their weaker-minded brethren that they (the
sorcerers) have communication with the dead and
can cast out (for a suitable fee, say, a pig or a
woman) unwelcome devils.

THE EJECTED GROGAN: “Won't gimme another drink, is it, yer lot o' — cows! Oi'll
get even wid yer fer this! Oi'll take out a probishun order aginst mesilf!"
“D.F.”: The dingoes were very bad a few
years ago up towards the head of the Murrumbidgee,
and some of the squatters let bloodhounds loose
under the fond delusion that they would kill out the
dingoes. Instead of that they intermarried with
them, and the last state of the squatters is worse than
the first, for they are now harried by increasing num-
bers of a bloodhound-dingo cross—huge, fierce brutes
that will pull down a good-sized calf, let alone a
mere sheep.

“Warre” (B. 24/2/T6) asks for
the bag-sewing record. I don’t know, but would like
to put this amongst the starters. I saw a man here
(near Maroona, Vic.) two years ago average 225 for
the season. He also cooked the meals for himself
and two others. There were 3000 of these bags, and
they went over 1951 b. each. I know because I had
to lump them.

“A.G.li.”: W anted, a remedy for weevil
in barley. The past year was, in South Aus., emi-
nently suitable for the breeding of the pest, and
holders of the grain suffered heavy loss from its
attacks. This was quite contrary to expectations. It
was thought that after such a hot year as 1914 the
grain would be too dry and hard to allow the weevil
to thrive, but as it turned out the better class of
barley contained 10 or 11 per cent, of moisture,
which is only slightly under the percentage in a
normal season. Also, can anyone settle the question
as to whether the weevil bores into grain or whether
it eats its way out? It is common to find a full-
grown weevil inside barley, the interior of which has
been eaten while the husk is sound and apparently
unperforated.
Cleveland”: The leathery substance
“J.H.L.” (B. 17/2/’l6) has seen growing on the
sides of trees is not included in the bill of fare of
the snake, nor do I know of any other thing that
creeps, crawls or flies which browses on the fungoid
growth. There is plenty of it in Maoriland, and
there it prefers to attach itself to the towai, puketea,
or totara, the first-named being most favored. The’
name given to it by bushmen, i.e., snake-bread, is as
wild and imaginary as the term “whale feed” or
“whale spawn” that is usually given to the thick
brownish scum that is frequently observed in tropi-
cal waters. • This stuff is only seen when the day
low-waters go lower than the night ones (April to
October), thus exposing the reefs to the rays of the
sun long enough for the slimes to dry. The rising
tide lifts them and the winds do the rest.

* *
•Papua” : Wandering along the coral reefs
in the Western Pacific and New Guinea the person
with keen eyes can observe some weird and curious
scaled creatures. Of crustaceans about the most
horrible is the armored and spiked giant-crayfish,
which I have seen up to 32in. in body. This devilish
animal is the cause of horrible wounds on brown
brother. But, served with mayonnaise, it is a dish fit for
a middle-aged bookmaker. Another fiend is the stone-
fish, the spines of which are said by the simple
savage to be the home of unmanned women after
their decease. The pain of a puncture from the
dorsal spines of this demon in every case I have
observed—and I have seen scores—drove the victims
clean crazy within lOmin. A highly-religious soul-
saver of my acquaintance, who trod on a tiny speci-
men of the curse while we were bathing off the beach
m front of his residence, was so overcome by the
agonising pain that “he knew not what he did.” The
spectacle of a tubby, chin-whiskered and unclothed
gentleman rushing madly about a mission compound
emitting scalding language amidst crowds of giggling
boy and girl savages is not an uplifting one. A good
second in rank to the “stone” comes the unicorn-fish,
a horror with a triangular head, armed with a long
horn fixed between its eyes. Its sting is not so last-
ing as that of the black sea-egg. The latter is the
tottering brink in marine horrors, and its wicked
spikes bring on a sort of blood-poisoning if not
quickly dug out by the village medicine-man. Quite
a humorist in a quiet way is the box-fish, which,
while giving its victim merely a swollen leg and
lumpy glands for a week or two, causes some innocent
mirth to the ingenuous cannibals. Tickled on the
stomach it gives an imitation of rotundity nearly as
developed as George Reid’s, and when playfully
smitten on the tummy it bursts with a loud sound
and spreads its internal clockwork about the land-
scape.
‘‘S. C. McL.” : On the Peak Downs, Cen-
tral Queensland, old blackboys say that the favorite
abo. method of combating snake venom is to cut
round the wound deep enough to open the larger
veins immediately after being bitten. They claim
this to be a certain cure for the poisoning, without
any risk of serious consequences from the operation.
Pure blood will flow first, and as the poison reaches
the cut, instead of passing on through the veins to
the heart, it is discharged. The jags made by the
knife are then plastered with wet earth, and before
long black brother is as good as new.

“Montalex”: The Australian Museum at
Sydney has a fine specimen of the sun-fish. There
is no more grotesque-looking' creature in existence.
Shaped like the prologue of an ordinary fish cut
square off amidships, it has for tail a fringe, from
the upper and lower corners of which project the only
two fins, dorsal and anal. The fish reaches a great
weight; I have shot one that went very close to
6cwt. Off the west coast of Ireland in hot weather
they often lie basking close to the surface, with the
big dorsal fin sticking about 2ft. out of the water.
The flesh would readily pass for veal.

THE PHANTOM FIRES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Campfires redly beckoning
Out of the Past for me ;
Fires beyond all reckoning,
Gleaming from sea to sea.
Fires a-strangely glimmering
(The wearied dog beside) ;
Embers for ever shimmering,
Out on the paths of pride.
Fires beyond all perishing ;
And oh ! they brightly brought
Dreams that were dreamt for cherishing—
Dreams that have come to nought!
Fires that were lit in my season
Of youthful might and main ;
Fires that were lit in my ease on
Tracks where I’d loaf again !
Time, the glutton, has eaten them ;
Winds have blown them away ;
Rain and the hail have beaten them,
Leaving the ashes grey.
Quenched —and yet they are shining still,
Early at night, and late;
And in their glow I am dining still,
Swapping yarns with a mate.
Aye, far away on the lonely tracks,
By mulga, gum and pine,
That once to me were the only tracks,
The long-quenched campfires shine!
N.S.W.
R. J. CASSIDY.

VANILLA SOUFFLE
Ingredients - l /-2 P»nt Nestle s
Condensed Milk, 2 ozs. but-
ter, vanilla essence, 2 ozs.
flour, 3 eggs.
Method Melt the butter in
a pan,add the flour, then
milk. Stir until it boils, then*
let it cool, and add the van-
illa essence, beaten yolks and
stiffly - whisked whites of
eggs. Turn into a prepared
souffle tin, and steam gently
for fifty minutes. Serve with
sherry sauce.
COCOANUT PUDDING
Ingredients 2 slices of stale bread,
1 pint Nestle’s Condensed Milk, 2 eggs,
1 oz. butter, 2 ozs. desiccated cocoanut.
Method Butter the bread, cut into small
square pieces, and fill up a pie-dish with alter-
nate layers of bread and butter and cocoanut.
Keep back about a teaspoonful of cocoanut,
and colour it pale pink with one or two drops
of carmine or cochineal Beat the yolks of the
eggs, add the milk, pour over the ingredients
in the pie-dish, and bake in a moderate oven
for thirty minutes. Whisk the whites of
the to a very stiff froth, pile it
on the pudding, replace it in
the oven for a few minutes
to set, then sprinkle
with the pink
cocoanut.

INVALIDS’ BLANC-MANGE
INGREDIENTS: 1 pint NESTLE’S CONDENSED
MILK, *4 oz. isinglass, 1 tablespoonful brandy, yolks of
2 egg*
METHOD : Dissolve the isinglass in the milk, put
into a saucepan to get hot, pour it over the beaten eggs,
and place inajar standing in a pan of boiling water. Stir
until the custard thickens, then add the brandy, and
pour into a wetted mould to set.

APRICOT EGGS
Ingredients 1 pint Nestle s Con-
densed Milky 2 ozs. cornflour, a small
tin of apricots.
Method - Mix the cornflour smoothly
with a little of the cold milk, boil the rest
and pour it on to the cornflour, return to
the pan, and boil gently for about ten min-
utes. Have ready some saucers rinsed in
cold water, and pour the blanc-mange into
these, taking care to only half fill them.
When set, turn out and arrange them on a
dish. Place an apricot on each shape
to imitate a poached egg, and pour
some of the syrup round. If liked,
the blanc-mange may be flav-
oured with a little almond
or vanilla essence.
Cornflour Pudding
INGREDIENTS . 2 oz. cornflour, 1 pint NESTLE’S CONDENSED MILK,
2 lemons, 2 eggs, 1 •$ ozs. butter.
METHOD : Mix the cornflour with a little of the cold milk, boil the remainder
with the thinly-pared lemon rinds, then strain on to the cornflour, return to the
pan, and boil gently for ten minutes, stirring alf the time. Allow it to cool, then
add the butter, the juice of one lemon, and the beaten yolks of the eggs. Pour into
a pie-dish, and bake in a moderate oven for twenty minutes. Pile the stiffly-whisked
whites of the eggs on to the top of the pudding, put it back into the oven for a few
minutes to set, and serve either hot or cold.
Orange Pudding
INGREDIENTS : Biscuit pastry, * pint NESTLE S CONDENSED
MILK, 2 eggs, 2 penny sponge cakes, 2 oranges.
METHOD : Line a shallow pie-dish with the pastry and decorate the
edges with pastry leaves. Peel the oranges very thinly and boil the rind in the
milk, then strain it over the beaten egg yolks. Pour into a jar placed in a pan of
boiling water, and stir until the custard thickens, then pour it over the crumbled
sponge cakes, and beat until smooth. Add the juice of the oranges, pour into the pie-
dish, and bake in a moderate oven for from twenty-five to thirty minutes. Whisk the
whites of the eggs to a very stiff froth, pile it on the pudding, and put it back in the oven
for a few minutes to set.

“Mulga” : Here’s an effective way of keep-
ing a bullock down if, after throwing it, there is no
rope handy to tie it. Divide the end of the tail
in two, give one strand a twist round the near hind
leg, run the other through the cloven part of the hoof
and tie together. As cattle always rise from their ,
hind props the beast will remain a prisoner until
liberated.
“Pick”: “Bush Builder” (B. 24/2/’l6)
says that buildings with pis 6 walls would suit only
in a rainless land, and that in any case they are much
more expensive than wood. Well, along the stretch
of the South-Eastern tablelands (N.S.W.), where I
ornament the scenery, and where this summer I have
seen rain spilling at the rate of 24in. in 35min., there
are many pise buildings. Occasionally they are util-
ised as dwellings, but more generally as stables,
dairies or outhouses. Many of them have withstood
the battering of all weathers, without decay, and at
Jugiong I knew a pise handball alley’, with cement-
plastered face, that remained intact for 15 y’ears.
The durability of a pise wall depends, firstly, upon
the class of material used in the mortar ; secondly, on
a solid foundation to prevent sagging and cracks;
and, thirdly, upon finish of the exterior with cement
wash or paint.
* * * *
“Wanko”: There may be something in
“Pick’s” saltwater cure for snakebite (B. 24/2/T6).
A Queensland halfcaste blackfellow showed me four
scars where Eve’s fancy had attempted to devour him.
He said that all he did after tearing the crawlers
limb from limb was to cut a small cross fairly’ deep
into the punctures with a penknife and rub salt well
in—coarse salt is best —filling up the wound by
pressing the salt in with the knife. Then he put
about a handful of salt on a handkerchief and
wrapped it over the injury. Two of the bites were
made by black snakes, so, provided he was not the
Book of Ananias, his cure was given a good try-out.
* * * *
“Will G. Buck” : When a N.Q. aboriginal
drew first horse in Tattersall’s about six months ago
he decided to go on the land like all phella white
man, and bought a holding' for £9OO. Of course, he
had to have a buggy’ to drive to town and a boy to
navigate him. During half a year on the land he
did no work, and then getting tired he went to the
ex-owner and asked him to buy the section back.
“How much y’ou want ?” “All same what I been
give y’ou.” “No.” “You givem me half?” “No.”
“All right. Well, you givem me £100.” Then the
deal was fixed up to the satisfaction of both
parties.

“5.0. C.”: Can any wanderer place the
Australian lilac? A friend obtained a fern from
Gippsland district, and growing out of the side was
a small shoot, which he removed and planted. In
four years it has grown to about 15ft. The flower
is not unlike the ordinary English lilac, but much
smaller, aiid the tree is something like the silver
wattle.

“Myall” : The sporting instinct is strongly
developed in the jumbuck barber. I remember one
wool-hawk who had all his combs and cutters named
after prominent racehorses- —Poseidon, Mountain King,
Lord Cardigan, Hibernian and so on. One morning
the boss noticed that Bill was “giving them a hit
up,” leaving great ridges of wool on the backs and
around the necks. Bill accordingly received a “wire.”
“Well, boss, it’s this way,” explained William;
“I’ve got old Hibernian on this morning. He’s a
bit slow for the flat, so I’m giving him a trial over
the sticks.” Bill kept his stand, but he had to
dispense with his hurdlers.
The aboriginal is heroic at times. Some
few y’ears ago in North Queensland Charlie
was arrested for stealing a horse on which
he was mounted at the time. In his com-
pany was Pollie, a young aboriginal woman,
who disappeared like a black vapor as soon as her
lord was gathered in. Charlie was
taken to Georgetown and remanded
to Normanton. On the way to the
latter place the troopers with their
prisoner and the horse in charge
camped for the night at a wayside
pub, placing the stolen animal in a
small paddock at the rear. Pollie,
who had followed on her own two
bare, murky feet, cautiously ap-
proached the y’ard and took the
horse. Then, without saddle or
bridle, she rode the incriminating
animal through 130 miles of wild,
stony scrub country to the coast.
Arriving there she slew Exhibit A
and burned the body’, after which
she returned to Normanton again on
foot through an entirely uninhabited
country. Charlie’s trial was put off
from time to time, but in the con-
tinued absence of the horse he was
released, and found 18-year-old
Pollie beaming outside the court. It
wasn’t till long afterwards that he
learned of her exploit. Pollie just
dropped into her old position in the
Charlie household and said nothing.
“Tarwin”: “O’Phimerty’s” remarks (B.
24/2/’l6) re the teetotal hermit, reminds me of a
similar lonely soul who planted himself in the hills
between Bacchus Marsh and Sunbury (Vic.). His
i eal name was not known, but the locals soon found
a label for him, “Beardy Harry.” An expert archer,
he subsisted principally on birds and rabbits, though
occasionally he would appear in the town-
ship about dusk, and, after regaling himself
on bread and sardines, make back to the hills. When
he was missed from his usual haunts and brought in
to hospital to die (the usual end of the bush hermit),
he swore the rescuing Hop had sold his body for dis-
section, and went over Jordan denouncing the hospi-
tal bath. When man breaks back to the primitive
he seems only to use water inwardly.
“Maginnis Magee”: I hasten to inform
“H.G.” (B. 10/2/’l6) that the late lamented Pat
Minahan, of D'onnybrook (Q.), was not the only
publican who dealt in “No Mercy” cheques, as the
following verse from a very old shearing song will
prove:— ■
Up on the Barwon the old shearer stands,
Grasping the bar with his strong, bony hands;
Fixed is his eye on a green painted keg,
Says: “Till you’re emptied I won’t stir a peg;
Come along, landlord ; come along, come.
Fill up the glasses. What’s yours ? Mine's rum.”
He passes his cheque which is seized in a crack,
And the landlord takes pen and writes “No Mercy”
on the back.
A few years ago I was acquainted with a very well-
known Queensland politician who in his pre-Parlia-
mentary days ran a pub Outback. He didn’t trouble
about consulting the owner of a cheque as to whether
he wished to “knock it down” or not. His method
was quicker and much more certain. When a cheque
of sufficient calibre was presented his eyes gleamed
like a tomcat’s on a sinful night. Grabbing a bullock-
bell which always hung handy he would rush on to
the verandah and shake it to such purpose that in a
very short time the entire boozing population of the
township would assemble and “cut out” the cheque
quick and lively’.

of the system so quickly by the blood stream th t
applications to the unbroken skin are waste of e ,

thing else just dries off on the surface and cannot
possibly influence the injection. The onlv tr
ment for the intense pain is morphia or some potent
anodyne, and, for collapse, strychnine in therapeutic
doses. The above applies somewhat to bites hi
snakes. But C. J. Martin, of the Lister Institute
a distinguished Australian and the world’s authorin'
on snakes and snake venom, says that up to th
present only one treatment is of use in snake-bit? 6
That is permanganate of potash. The scarification
should be ruthless. Playing with the thin* j s
calling tenders for an inquest.

“Sassafras”: I range them this way.
The best timber to stand in the ground is bloodwoo'd.'
I know a fence that dates back 74 years on the
Lower Manning (N.S.W.). The hardest chopping
timber is teak, otherwise faudosa. It is also the
best burner, but too fierce if used by itself i n a
furnace. I never saw a fireman use it exclusively
White beech is the worst burner. It refuses to be
burnt off on a clearing job, but by piling other
timber on to it it can be melted. I have seen a
bloodwood split 947 mortise posts; a teak girder
from the head of the Richmond River (N.S.W.)
16in. x 16in., 98ft. long, shipped to Japan; and a
beech pit-sawed to 9800 super feet on the North
Coast line (Q.).
* * * *
“Tropicus” : The natives of the Engineer
Group, in the Louisiade Archipelago (N.G.), have
their place for the souls of the dead on the summit
of the highest mountain on Normanby Island, which
is the most southern island of the D’Entrecasteaux
chain. When a citizen of Tubi Tubi (an island in
the Engineer Group) departs from this warful world
the time of his arrival in spiritland is governed
by the amount of weeping and eating his friends
indulge in at his removal. Deceased’s pigs are
slain and eaten by his friends and relations, who
howl and scream their worst, the women show-
ing additional “respect” to the corpse by scratching
their faces until blood flows. Amongst the most
select families in the Group it was formerly the
fashion to destroy everything owned by the deceased
- —canoes, spears, nets, cocoanut palms, houses,
etc.—but this custom is no longer practised. After
the spirit leaves the body of the dead it walks into
the sea and journeys along the bottom until the
beach of Normanby Island is reached. A tunnel
leads thence to the summit of the mountain, but
unless the dead man has been well cried for and
much “respect” shown him by his relations, the
spirit will not be able to find the mouth of the
tunnel, and will for ever wander about the
foothold of the ocean. The natives firmly believe
this happens to people who die away from their
relations. In the bad old days before the white
men came along with their laws to prevent various
flourishing unpleasantries it was the duty of the
deceased’s wife to live and sleep on the dear
departed’s grave for two moons. As the corpse wjs
covered only by five or six inches of sand, the pro-
hibition of this simple but respectful custom did not
meet with very much opposition from the widows.

“ J.G.” : I’m into “Barcoo’s” (B. 24/2/10)
straightest-tree competition with the mountain ash
which is found about the ranges of southern I ic.
This is the favorite tree for timber splitters owing to
the fell nature of its- wood. It is the easiest splitter
we have. “Barcoo’s” Queensland rosewood is only a
shrub beside it for size, and it is not possible to fin
in the Australian bush a straighter vegetable or one
so clean in the trunk. It is quite common to stub your
toe on one measuring about 200 ft. from the ground to
the first limb and varying little in thickness for at
least the first 100 ft. The timber is being increasing.'
used by furniture-makers and picture-framers an
others who want an easily-worked but tough bar -
wood. Blackbutt is cousin to the mountain ash, but
is not so straight in either trunk or grain.
SUNDAY MORNING ON THE BEACH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Sunday beach was all at peace
And everyone was gay ;
It seemed as if joy could not cease
On such a sunny day ;
The garments that each bather wore
Were short behind and brief before.
Large men behaved like little kids,
And women did the same;
Nobody cared for hard-won quids,
For battles, boots or fame;
In fact, they’d reached the happy stage
We treasure as the Golden Age.
But suddenly a shadow fell
Athwart the sunlit strand,
Stilling the clear salt-water yell
One heard on every hand ;
Each wife reproved her sprawling mate
And sanded lovers sat up straight.
Silent and sad they watched It drift
Around the sun-bleached rocks,
Looking in every cave and rift
In search of moral shocks.
(Some bathers risk a police-court doom
By using Nature’s dressing-room.)
The law-abiding humbly plank
A hoarded tuppence down
To tear their clothes off in a rank
Of bathers bare and brown.
The freeman plucks his shirt off where
There’s naught but sea and wind and air.
And so the Sunday copper comes
To catch him in his skin;
While the free wind of Freedom hums
He waves the sinner in,
And presently a fine’s imposed.
(Note: Nature’s dressing-room is closed.)
N.S.W.

“ Jaspilite”: Re the Narra Tarra mine
(P». 3/2/TO). Between 1870 and 1884 it
produced £OO,OOO of lead. It was then closed
down, and very little work was done till
1913, when the Fremantle Trading Co. com-
pleted the sinking of a new shaft east of the
old workings, and opened out a level at
250 ft. vertical. The lode was cut at 64ft.
west of this new shaft, and to the end of
July, 1914, 700 tons of high grade lead ore
were shipped. An up-to-date reduction
plant, manager’s residence, and other build-
ings have been erected, and operations were
suspended solely on account of the difficulties
arising out of the war. Operations have
now been resumed.. “Cristo’s” yarn about
a miner being drowned in the Narra Tarra
is a myth. He may be thinking of
the Geraldine lead mine, 40 miles
north of Northampton, on the Murchison,
which was worked from 1857 to 1878. When
the Murchison came down, a hurried exit
had to be made from the mine, the main
shaft of which was over 300 ft. on the in-
cline. The co. had blackfellows stationed 20
miles up the river to give notice of an
approaching flood. According to local mytho-
logy a miner, after climbing up, discovered
that he had left his waistcoat and watch
below, and in spite of warning went below
to secure liis property and never returned.
* * * *
“Borgne”: The February gold yield in
Victoria shows a decrease of 10.7720 z.
compared with that of February, 1915
= a drop of 36 p.c. The ideas of
the Mines Department as to the best way
to revive the decadent gold-mining industry
are everything except brilliant or practical.
During the month advances for prospecting
were given to 10 parties of miners, and the
total amount was £466. Of what earthly
use is that sum spread over 10 mining fields?
Its obvious purpose is to catch votes. Yet
the Mines Department will not recommend
the Government to subsidise mines or miners
W’orking lodes of wolfram or antimony or
molybdenite or scheelite. In Victoria there
are big deposits of manganese, tin, gypsum
coal, brown coal, pigments and iron ore, and
small deposits of nickel, and these ought to
be developed w r ith the assistance of the Gov-
ernment. But the officials of the Mines
Department will not advise the Minister to
subsidise any persons willing to work rare
earths or base metals. The heads seem
obsessed with the theory that gold is the only
payable metal Victoria contains, and applica-
tions for help to prospect any mineral except
gold-bearing are turned dowm rapidly and
finally. Thousands of pounds are available
to re-open worked-out gold mines, but not
a penny to open any of the increasingly
valuable metals known’to exist all over Vic-
toria. Picture the official stupidity which
will not aid the establishment of a brown
coal industry!
