“Quidam”: Ever and anon hopeful souls
put cash into the re-opening of some aged
derelict. Mostly the action is based
on the popular delusion that the only
man in the world incapable of run-
ning a mine is invariably in charge,
“lie got off the gold,” “he drove
away from the shoot,” “he sunk through the
reef.” etc., they tell you. Large bodies of
highly payable ore, of course not payable in
“them days,” perpetually exist below. The
truth is that, with free milling ores requir-
ing battery treatment only, they could work
cheaper in “them days” than now’. Labor
and fuel, two important items, are far
dearer, and stores are not much cheaper in
many localities. Anyway, the stores about
a battery are not very 7 expensive. One mine
in the Rockley district (N.S.W.) worked
for 2s. a ton, according to the statement or
the manager. Even then he couldn’t raise
over Is. 9d. out of the ore for a considerable
period before he closed down. Yet local
residents aver that it is a good mine still.
It paid miners 7s. and pretty w T ell nothing
at all to boys, and there w’ere very feu
conditions in regard to hours, certificate
drivers, and such items. Probably some da
someone will come along and pump out tne
shaft, and endeavor to beat the 2s. cos,
under impossible difficulties: labor higher,
wood higher, everything higher —barring 1 1
values. Of course, once it crushed tar mou
than Is. 9d., and that is alw’ays the glittei-
ing bait. The fact that a once-paying num
is generally carried on far below the payau
margin before plant and machinery a -
dismantled by the disgusted proprietary
seems impossible of recognition.
Business, Bobbery, etc,
Sample of the profits of ship-selling.
R.M.S. Oriental, once a high-class P. and
O. liner in the Australian trade, was built
in 1889. She was due to be scrapped, but
soon after war broke out the co. received an
offer from some child-like and bland heathen
in Hongkong and sold her for £32,000. Last
mail the simple child of Confucius wrote to
say that he had resold the ship for £65,000,
which is about 50 per cent, more than her
original cost.
* * * *
No wonder the price of steamers for quick
delivery is soaring. A few years ago a
firm in these parts had a certain steamer on
a lengthy time charter at £1075 a month.
The same vessel is now’ running under char-
ter on the Atlantic at £6700 a month. That’s
one reason why our Peruvian boots and the
pipes we get from Greenland’s icy moun-
tains are bounding up.
“Thraso”: A big combine is being
arranged amongst the Dutch shipping cos.
ostensibly with a view to annexing the trade
formerly done by Germany. The Holland-
America Co. during the war has earned 80
per cent, of its capital. Besides, it has
bought out the German shareholders, who
held one-fourth of the stock. Three other im-
portant Dutch cos. have been making huge
profits—the Royal Holland Lloyd, the
Royal West Indian Mail, and the Royal
Dutch. All these concerns are now associ-
ated and backed by a group of Dutch banks.
Already new services have been started to
South and Central America, Panama and
Morocco, and there is great danger that the*
combine may get a strong grip on what
hitherto has been British business. The only
way to presently meet that sort of grab will
be for the British Empire to boycott both
Dutch and Germans as to coal. In fact,
coal-supply is the key to most post-bellum
problems of trade and commerce. If the
Empire—including, of course, Australia —re-
fuses to supply coal to any nations except
our Allies, not only will it be impossible for
our German and Austrian enemies to steal
our trade or dump goods into Australia, but
the neutral nations which have been busy
annexing our trade will find their schemes
defeated and their activities paralysed.
* * * *
“Murkah”: A time-saving device used in
the Maoriland telegraph service might well
be inquired into by P.M.G. Webster, with
a view to adoption on this side of the Tas-
man. It is the Christmas-greetings telegram.
The text, “Wishing you a merry Christmas
and a happy New Year,” is printed on forms,
the sender adding the address and
signature. To encourage the public to adhere
to the words, such telegrams are admitted
at a cheap rate. The telegraphists then
merely transmit “Greet’gs msges,” and rip
through the addresses and signatures. The
receiving forms are printed to match, and
150 such messages can be squirted through
in an hour. In Australia the average speed
of working is about 40 telegrams an hour
and the lines are literally choked with
Christmas greeting traffic, causing all classes
of business to be heavily delayed.
* * * *
“Quid”: The wire-netting rabbit-trap is
coming into favor with fur-hunters, and
some now prefer it to the steel variety which
grips bunny by the leg. The wire variety
is of conical shape, about 4ft. long, with a
light swinging door at one end, and a remov-
able piece at the other end for taking out the
catch. The small end is inserted in a bur-
row, and all the adjacent holes are then
blocked up with paper. (Bunny won’t bur-
row through newspaper, so long as the organs
of public opinion are dry, so as to rustle
when he strikes them. Damp paper is no
good.) If kittens are about, the first few nights
will only result in crops of these youths,
Pa and Ma lying low and sayin’ nothin’.
Frequently it is a week or more before they
come out, but as 20 or so can crowd into
one trap there is profit in waiting. In a
lucerne paddock they will keep dowmstairs
for a long time, living on the roots. The
principal objection to the system is the
amount of literary matter required; one sta-
tion near Rockley (N.S.W.), with 150 wire
traps going, uses about scwt. per week. At
a time when sufficient fodder for postage
stamps threatens to become unobtainable, it
looks as if the wire trappers will have to go
out of business or take to bank notes.
* * * *
“X.”: The ragtime press is making a
patriotic noise about soldiers’ wives and
widows being harassed by grabbing trades-
people. But there is another side to it.
I live in a coal-mining village where the
pit has been working very irregularly.
One miner with a large family appealed to
the charity of the small storekeeper and
cheerfully ran up a bill of nearly £4O. .While
he was doing it he spent in cash at the pub
as much as he strapped up at the grocer’s.
Now he has enlisted, amidst the applause of
the townspeople, and the storekeeper is
unable to get any money out of him or his
wife. He can’t be got at by legal processes
and his wife says she won’t pay. I wish
some of these cheerful journalists had the
book-debts problem of the storekeeper in a
district where strikes are popular. It may
be foolish of the storekeeper to provision his
customer on credit while he quarrels with
his boss; but it is unjust to taunt him
with lack of patriotism when he asks for the
money he needs to keep him out of the
Insolvency tlourt.

Egypt is learning its English advertising
patter as fast as it can. One of the numer-
ous taverns has a leaflet out which raves
about the “luxerious and well-aerated rooms”
and “breakfasts special for the British
army.” Just round the fourteenth dirty
corner to the right is another establishment
which begs for custom in this fashion :
Don’t loose of time! Go and see as you have the
good occasion to be in Cairo the new Australian
Officers’ Embroideries Shop. If you wish to have
best and very nice presents, don’t forget to remem-
ber our new shop here on at Shareh Wagh-el-Birket
where a special draftsman is under your disposition.
And your names can be embroided on your hand-
kerchieves while you are smaking yet. Also a special
engravour is occupied by doing on silver and gold
medals, hearts different kind of brooches and brace-
lets with a very beautiful rememberies of the Pyra-
mides, the Sphinx and the Nile and a several things
very nice and artistique. A very moderated price
should be exposed for officers and soldiers. Don’t
loose of time!

“Sarto”: The other day when the old
horse and cart arrived home from town with
a bundle of dress stuff and sundries for Mrs.
Sarto I found amongst them two or three
cards of those domes that I fasten her up
the back with. The bottom part of each
card was sawn off and on the top was
printed, “Made in U.S.A.” Now’, why didn’t
they make the lie complete and write “Made
in Australia”? But I suppose they
reckoned that that would put the show
away, because we know such things can’t be
made in Australia.
“D.F.”: Hydraulic sluicing has been
applied to potatoes as well as tin on the
North-East Coast (Tas.). A farmer there
had four acres of crop ready for digging
on a steep hillside, when there came a cloud-
burst and stripped the ground off, spuds
and all. Luckily there was a paling fence
across the bottom of the paddock and the
potatoes—the big ones, anyway—fetched up
against the fence, while the dirt went through
and left the spuds behind clean and ready
for bagging.
* * * *
The banana as a sideline to the cow seems
a payable possibility of the future on the
North Coast (N.S.W.). One grower at
Mullumbimby expects to be shortly hauling
down £lBOO a year from nine acres of palms.
The fly in the salad is that the Tweed Rivers
fruit is sold in Sydney as Fijian. The
N.S.W. growers claim that their average out-
put is superior to the island average, and if
ir were properly marketed the superiority
would tell in the account sales. Moreover,
they argue that there may be an odd Aus-
tralian about who would purchase the local,
white-grown vegetable for patriotic reasons
if he had a chance to recognise it, and that
he ought to be given the chance. The bed-
rock of the trouble is that the banana has to
be plucked green, and ripened afterwards in
“sweating-rpoms.” In Sydney this business
is monopolised by a few Chows who don’t
care a coolie what color the stuff is as long
as it can be yellowed up for the market.

Yet another job for the returned soldier.
Leslie _ Gaze, an experienced pigeon-fancier
of Brighton, believes that the breeding of
Bordeaux squabs for the table may be found
a suitable occupation for the man who can’t
plough or feed pigs—particularly in a coun-
try which will shortly be obliged to find a
substitute for mutton-chops and corned beef.

. “F.N.T.” One of Victoria’s large sawmill-
ing concerns is now busy turning timber into
explosives. At its mills refuse is charcoaled
and the pyroligneous-acid content of the
eucalyptus-parings is condensed into a
liquid. Thence are separated a number of
by-products, one of which is acetone, a con-
stituent of cordite. The works are wholly
Private Enterprise; yet. if ever there was
need for State exploitation of State
resources, this is an instance. It is a fine
thing for Australia to have a new industry,
but the logical sequence of events in tlie
establishment of such an enterprise should
include the planting of a young eucalypt
at the spot from which the old one was
taken an item which has never interested
any private forest-clearing co. Tom Living-
ston, M.L.A. for Gippsland South and Min-
ister for Mines and Forests, is presumably
so used to observing large, imposing, virgin
areas of giant gums in his own electorate
~a / . his mind fails to grasp the pos-
sibility of their ultimate exhaustion. Still,
Gippsland South itself contains a mvriad
object-lessons in the folly of tree-slaughter.

n 7? ne , least . of tll€ trade s affected by the
\V heat Scheme is on a good wicket this year
milling. Under the pooling scheme mill-
owner sub agents, who hold wheat in trust
for the Board, pay weekly for the wheat
they actually mill out of their trust stocks.
In ordinary years the miller lias to invest
a huge sum early in the season, often
enough to secure his year’s requirements.
This year he practically has only to invest
in a week’s supply. A vast difference look-
ing at the . matter from the financial aspect.
Uftset against this, of course, is the restricted
profit he is allowed to make owing to the
price of flour being fixed, and against that
is the absolute protection he has against
loss. He cannot take advantage of market
changes and soaring demands, and honorable
understandings are, of course, out of the
question ; but there should have been a fine
lot of millers’ free money to invest in the
war loans. Was there?
THE COUNT OF NOSES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A little trade dispute is on,
And Michael Callaghan proposes
The thing be settled pro or con
By virtue of a count of noses.
’Tis done, and Mr. Mike McFay
By just one vote has got his way.
Now Minsky rises to explain:
“Dot cound of noses ain’t nod true—
I tink ve cound him vonce again,
Und giff to effery man his due.
McFay cound von. Dot’s onjust, see,
Because you cound but von to me!”
“And why not?” cries the angry Chair.
“That’s one to each. It’s right and just.”
Poor Minsky ruffles up his hair,
And stares at them in sheer disgust.
“Vy not!” he thunders. “Holy Moses!
Say, vill you shust gompare dose noses?”

A GIRL’S COMPLEXION.
Very few girls get through their ’teens
without eruptions or pimples showing on
their faces.
These may be due to indigestion resulting
from improper diet. If so they will quickly
disappear when the proper food is adhered
to. If they are accompanied by pallor and
listlessness you must look to the condition
of the blood.
Because these eruptions on the faces of
schoolgirls are so common they seldom
receive the attention from parents that
they require. When they indicate thin blood
or anaemia there is a ready remedy in Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills. These tonic pills, free
from harmful drugs, are pretty sure to help
growing girls because they build up the blood
at a time when the demand on it is great-
est. As the blood is made rich and red*
fickle appetites become normal, lassitude
gives way to vigor, the cheeks and lips show
the glow of health, and the whole system
is toned up. The nervous system is invigor-
ated, and with proper attention to diet many
dangers are averted.
Your own dealer sells Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills, or they will be sent post paid, on re-
ceipt of price, 2s. 9d. per box, six boxes
3d., by the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co.,
Sydney. The pamphlet, “Diseases of the
Blood,” free on request.—Advt.
HOW TO AVOID SICK HEADACHE.
Many people suffer at regular intervals
from sick headache. Generally they can tell
hours in advance when an attack is coming
on. The trouble is caused by accumulation
ot poison in the digestive organs, and when
a certain point is reached this poison pro-
duces nausea and the attack follows.
If sufferers would take a dose of Pinkettes,
the new laxative, at intervals these distress-
ing attacks may be avoided. Pinkettes are
dainty little, sugar-coated ovals that arouse
no feeling of repulsion, and they act so gently
that they are the ideal laxative to use in cor-
recting a tendency to sick headache. They
have no unpleasant effect to make them ob-
jectionable to the extra-sensitive sick head-
ache patient. Old-fashioned harsh purgatives
will not do at all in such cases. If you would
like to try them send for a free sample to
the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Sydney,
mentioning that you read of them in The
Bulletin. Sold everywhere, Is. per bottle.

POLITICAL
POINTS
LIGHTS O’ LONDON.
LITTLE BILLY: “I had no idea I teas so big.”
Pleas for a piebald Australia are again
very frequent in Melbourne, where the Com-
monwealth Parliament meets. Regarding
the agricultural development of the Northern
Territory the Argus denounces as “cow-
ardly” the attitude of those who would “leave
the problem to future generations.” The
Argus proposes, as an alternative to putting
the burden on posterity, that we should “re-
adjust our ideas as to the kind of labor to be
introduced.” That goes beyond cowardice—
it amounts to kneeling down and putting up
our throat to be cut.
A firm of contractors tried the Tas. Gov-
ernment with the Norton Griffiths bait the
other week; but, even if Earle was that
sort, it was too near a general election.
Evidently they knew they had missed a
good thing, because at once they talked of
an action to recover £15,000 for “breach of
contract.” Where the Tas. speculators-
made the mistake was in relying on a con-
tract. They should try little bridge parties
next time.
Alost of the Alaoriland newspapers are
politely discussing the great national cadge,
which receives editorial- benediction only
through columns sacred to the cause of Fat.
It is a very barefaced cadge even in a
country where the trail towards Cohen was
blazed by such sturdy pioneers as Vogel,
Seddon and Ward. Alaoriland is not find-
ing a solitary copper for war. Every
month the dreadful Cold Hash Government
touches harassed John Bull for half a
million. Incidentally the, inhabitants are
gathering in large quantities of cash from
John for wool and butter and meat, but they
are carefully stowing that lot away, in case,
apparently, something goes wrong at the
Front.
Professor Allen, quarantine director
Cumpston, and Jeffreys AVood, the children’s
specialist—the committee of three medical
men appointed by the Federal Administra-
tion, with Alathews, M.H.R., as chairman, to
inquire into the causes of morbidity and mor-
tality in this country—have handed a pre-
liminary report to the Alinister for Customs.
Figures show that the death-rate for Alel-
bourne city is more than one-half greater
than that of Alalvern ; and “it is hoped that
when an explanation of these differences is
arrived at, a remedy will suggest itself.” The
explanation seems simple enough. Alalvern
lives a more spacious life and has less dust
with its meals. And the remedy lies in wider
land settlement and less of the cursed conges-
tion policy.
Ex-Treasurer Wad-
dell wailed the other
night in the N.S.W.
Assembly over the
horror of a proposal
to make citizenship
the basis for a muni-
cipal vote instead of
allowing the vote to
rest on the old-estab-
lished basis of Prop-
puty. The essence
of his howl was that
it was proposed to
give non - property
owners the right to
tax a landowner up
to the hilt, though
his land might, be
heavily mortgaged.
Yet Parliament has
much more authority
over Propputy than
a municipal or shire
council. It could, if
it felt that way,
raise all the State
revenue from taxa-
tion of land, and
order that estates be
cut into pieces and
offered for sale.
Bumble cannot do
anything half as
hideous as that.
Why, then, doesn’t
Waddell demand a
Propputy franchise
for the Assembly? Or
does he suggest that
a citizen of Sydney
who happens to be
without land is less
interested in the
health and sani-
tation of the city than the corpulent owner
of 75 slum cottages?
The Westralian Industries Assistance
Board, called into being to help the dis-
tressed cocky (was there ever a cocky that
wasn’t?) has at least helped its protege to
a hoivl of rage from one end of the State to
the other. The Board means well, but it
squats in an office in Perth and there tiles
to deal with an avalanche of appeals from
Westralian Cockydom. The result is that
the struggling farmer (all farmers struggle)
who sends an urgent wire for a plough gets
—if anything at all —a reaper and binder
which he may need, if God is good, six months
hence. At a big and carefully-engineered
meeting in the Perth Town Hall, some of
the genuine grass-seeds were brought down
to recite their tales of woe. Underwood,
M.L.A., who was temporarily in command of
the Board, was present, and interrupted
Cocky with ribald comments. The Westra-
lian farmer was so infuriated that the tact-
ful Johnson, on his return from Melbourne,
promptly gave the Honorary Minister another
job, and now makes the administration of
the Industries Assistance Act his own special
funeral.
A lot of people never quite realised the
horrors of war till they got their income-tax
assessments last week.
“I do not consider that 9s. a day is suffi-
cient for a man to keep his wife and family
on,” Iloyle, N.S.W. Minister for Railways,
announced last week. Well, what is Air.
Iloyle going to do? Is he going to suggest
to them that they buck in and earn more?
He was talking to a section of railway
employees at the time; and the best proof
that railway employees, as a whole, get all
that they earn at present and a bit over is
that the Department is working at a loss.
It looks as though at last the N. S. AVales
railways are to have, if not coal-mines of
their own, at any rate State mines from
which to draw their supplies. AVade. of
course, made a preliminary snap at the Bill
when it was brought in; but that was the
sort jof thing the party-Government farce
requires. The inter-State shipping cos. don’t
need half the coal that the railways do; yet
one after the other they have found it good
business to either make or buy a mine. That
circumstance ought to end the argument.
At the entrance to Currie Harbor on King
Island (Tas.) is the Kelp Rock, which acts
as a sort of breakwater. In 191 U the Tas-
manian AAMrks Department got the bright
idea that it would improve the port to
remove the rock and let the sea in more
freely. So it put a couple of years on the
job and spent £l9OO of loan money. Then
it was decided that the rock was much better
where it was; but as the top had been sawn
off, a beacon must now be put on what is
left of it, and £4300 is to be spent in this
and other “improvements.” But there is
this consolation for the kid who will have
to pay the bill —it would have cost more
than £4OOO, apart from the £l9OO, to shift
the whole of the Kelp Rock, and then there
would have been the job of putting it all
back again.

The renewed uproar about the oversea
Dominions “having a voice in Empire
defence” recalls the last voice that was
raised on the subject. The noise was the
property of lou Ward, Maoriland’s noblest
spender, who, at the Imperial Defence Con-
ference five years ago, sprang in with a
scheme for immediately slicing up
£270,000.000 (borrowed on a joint p.n.) on
00 additional Dreadnoughts. Ward reck-
oned that this would show the universe that
the Empire was determined to stand shoulder
to shoulder. When someone asked for de-
tails, Ward remarked that it was not a time
for recrimination, whereupon there was sub-
dued applause and Ward went back to
Maoriland—to be thrown out of his job at a
general election, as it may do Billy Hughes
some good to remember.

THE MILLENNIUM.
“ ....that the Government establish a Mont de Piete
for loans at a reasonable rate of interest.”
P.L.L. Conference motion (N.S.W.),
THE FINANCIAL GENIUS: “ Ooray , Bill! I’ve
got it! Let’s start a pub of our own.”
Here’s a pretty mess that our multiplicity
of Governments is likely to laud us in.
Pearce says the early closing of hotels and
clubs is necessary for military, efficiency.
Premier Holman says the people must (Wide
the hour of turning off the taps. Now,
then, supposing the N.S.AV. referendum re-
turns a verdict of 11 p.m., what does Pearce
do? Does he propose to let military effi-
ciency go overboard or does he turn the
people’s vote down?
S. Aus. is wondering who its new Rail-
way Commissioner is to be; an appointment
has to be made within the next month or
two. The State’s service—outside perhaps
the Schools —has suffered for years from
the reign of old men. As a rule nobody gets
to the top of the ladder in S. Aus. until he
is about to fall off from old age; and in
the year or two that he lasts he mostly
doesn’t think it worth while doing anything
radical or of much consequence. According
to present appearances the common rule is
to be applied to this railway job; and, if it
is. it will be one more inducement for the
young man of brains to go out of the S. Aus.
service if he has made a mistake in his
youth and gone in.
The war so far having been conducted on
comfortable lines for our side, Australia has
gone on methodically importing stamp-paper.
Now a shortage is in sight, and the general
public is urged to economise by not gumming
two penny stamps where one tuppenny will
do. Heaven only knows what Australia would
have done if Von Tirpitz had got out.
Really all the Freetraders and Low-tariffers
of Australia ought to combine to build him
a statue. A hungry, partly unclothed and
wholly annoyed Australia would probably
have hanged Billy Hughes and the rest of
the old' Freetraders had the old chap suc-
ceeded in stopping those imports which we
can’t do without owing to the past sins of
the no-tariffists and the low-tariffists.
One of the arguments against the referen-
dum is that a Bill receives much more criti-
cal attention from Parliament than the popu-
lace could possibly give it —that Parliament
votes after mature deliberation, while the
public would vote blindly without under-
standing. The N.S.W. Assembly showed the
force of all this the other night when the
Bakeries Bill, giving authority for setting
up a State monopoly, was submitted. Every-
body at once went to sleep while the Attor-
ney-General explained the measure. Then
he went to sleep himself, and the Sergeant-
at-Arms woke someone who made a few re-
marks, and at the finish roused the loudest
snorer to carry on the debate. So the dreary
farce went on to a ludicrous end. When a
vote was taken one man who intended to say
“No” tvas sleepily nodding his head, so they
put him down with the “Ayes,” and he had
to explain the matter the next day.
THE CALL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Don’t yeh hear them callin’ to yeh, callin’ to yeh,
lad?
Where the skyline’s smeared an’ grey with cannon
smoke,
There’s a crowd o’ chaps that knew yeh;
Don’t yeh hear them callin’ to yeh—•
Mates o’ yours with ’oom yeh used to drink an’
joke ?
An’ they crust yeh, lad; they trust yeh for the
friendship that yeh had.
Don’t yeh hear them callin’,
Callin’ to yeh, lad?
Can’t you see them beck’nin’ to yeh, beck’nin’ to
yeh, boy?
There’s a pal o’ yours that fell at Sari Bair;
An’ yeh cheered ’im when yeh parted,
An’ yeh felt a bit down-’earted;
Now ’e’s passed the game to you, to do yer share.
Oh, the job is reel dead earnest, an’ a gun is not a
toy;
Can’t yeh see them beck’nin’,
Beck’nin’ to yeh, boy ?
Don’t yeh know they’re waitin’ for yeh, waitin’ for
yeh, mate,
Hopin’, prayin’ that their countrymen are game;
All that brave an’ battlin’ crowd of
Men that in yer ’eart yer proud of—
Mates o’ yours that ’elped to make yer country’s
name?
Do yeh mean to dodge the trouble till the foe is at
the gate?
“Oh, it’s weary waitin’,
Waitin’ for yeh, mate!”
Can’t yeh see them lookin’ at yeh, lookin’ at yeh,
lad—
Women-folk of mates o’ yours that fought and fell?
Are yeh grumblin’ an’ protestin’ ?
Will yer mateship stand the testin’ ?
Have yeh read the message that those wide eyes
tell?
Have yeh heard grey mothers weepin’ ? Have yeh
seen young wives grow sad?....
Won't yeh have them prayin’,
Prayin’ for yeh, lad?
IX HOSPITAL,

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Malster, as a sire, galloped by deputy into
the limelight again at Warwick Farm on Saturday.
The old horse has been responsible for hundreds of
winners, but lately his family had been slow in
finding the winning post. This time four of them
won successive races and gave their father a big
boost for the yearling sales. The winners were Tin-
tometer, Alcomet, Maltasia and Hopoast. Alcomet
landed the big race and incidentally won £5OOO for
the lucky person who drew him in Tatt.’s sweep.
Maltasia is the property of Humphrey Oxenham.
Hopoast is owned by Mrs. Flemmich, a Queensland
station-owner who also races Cagou. As a year-
ling Hopoast cost 800 gs. Two years ago he
won a race in Brisbane, but since then had pursued
the bacon in vain. The other Malster two-year-old,
Tintometer, promises well. He is a good finisher and
ran second to Maori Mat in the Caulfield Debutante
Stakes.
* * * *
Two nags which have a hard contract on
hand if they want to be as respectable as their
mothers ran well forward at Warwick Farm (Sydney)
on Saturday. Golden Shoe, second in the two-year-
old event, is by Wallace from the famous Golden
Slipper, for whom 4500 guineas was once paid.
Maltasia, winner of the Farm Stakes, is by Malster
from Acrasia, heroine of the Melbourne Cup of 11
years ago.
* * * *
The A.J.C. lias decided to split the two
principal two-year-old races between the sexes,
making the Breeders’ Plate eligible to colts only
and the Gimcrack Stakes to fillies. A good move.
During the last couple of years each event has
attracted about 30 starters, the result being to make
luck an important factor in the flutter. It is
thought by some people that this limitation will
discount the value of young stock. But it won’t
discount the value of young stock that can win or
go near it.
* * * *
Victorian nags are coming across the
Murray in droves in pursuit of the cash hung up by
the A.J.C. Usually at this time of the year horses
from Peacock’s territory transpire in much smaller
numbers. Prospect of further axe work on the
stakes may be the explanation of the raid.
At the Mudgee (N.S.W.) Show meeting
Tehama, winner of the Trial Handicap, six furlongs,
was ridden by her owner and trainer, Tommy
Donoghue. Donoghue must be about the most ancient
horse-pilot in the world. He is 58, and the vigorous
style in which he kicked his prad round the course
earned him an encore in addition to the stake money.
“Barcoo”: After the par. appeared about
Jimmy Rodgers’s 70 placed mounts in North and
Central Bar.analand for the five months of the year
(in the last month of ’l5 the total was swollen to
90-odd), James was engaged as rider for one of the
big racing stables in M.L. Nearly 20 years ago the
game people secured Mark Ryan from the same
address. Mark was then a successful horseman in
N.Q., but not fashionable on metropolitan courses,
mainly, perhaps, because owners were prejudiced in
favor of riders with two eyes. Mark had only one.
But it was a good ’un, and over in the Trembling
Isles he was at the top of the tree for 12 years.
Rodgers is a capable, resolute horseman, and should
do as well.
* * * *
“Mujik”: Successes in Australia Dy im-
ported horses and the progeny of other nags from
oversea are not going to send up the price of Aus-
tralian-bred yearlings which have omitted to get an
imported strain in their pedigree. The really Aus-
tralian gee looks like becoming a commoner, and
Australian sports are getting into a habit of bidding
in England. Explanations range from vague allega-
tions about the absence of certain unspecified salts
from Australian grass to the assumption that the
blood in the English thoroughbred always runs out
in another country after a few generations. Also, it
is held by many that the speediest prads born in
Australia are destroyed for stud purposes by too much
racing. This last charge comes only third in order
of preference. If Australian breeders want to get at
the chief reasons for the imported nag doing so well
they ought to consider first the horsemastership of
Australia, and secondly the mania for breeding to
proved speed instead of to blood. Most Australian
trainers are only entitled to the name in the way
that a barber is entitled to label himself professor.
Their qualifications for training horses are mostly
that (1) they were able to ride in childhood ; (2)
they can still clean out loose-boxes, and otherwise are
attentive valets. This is all wrong, and the proof of
it is in the performances of horses and the number
that go wrong. Breeding wholly to stallions and
mares that have been galloped for years is just as
bad. Vitality is what is wanted in breeding, and the
vitality of a sucessful racing mare or stallion has in
almost every case been left on the track. Take Car-
bine as a racehorse and a stallion. Then consider
his sire and dam, Musket and Locket. The same
story _ runs right through the history of Australian
breeding, with the exception of two or three animals
of the same remarkable stamina as Old Jack.
* * * *
“Homo”: How is this for mixed luck?
A Westralian came on a visit to his brother in Vic.
and, just for old time’s sake, they had a combined
shot in Tatt.’s. The net result was some £2OOO odd
between them. They reinvested some of it in the
big gamble, and a little later pulled out £5OO each.
The day the Westralian received his second pot he
came home in the pink of health ; but that night he
was seized with an illness which killed him a few
days later. When he was laid in his grave Tatt.’s
big cheque still lay in the house, unbanked. Still
perhaps all this isn’t so very remarkable; if I kept
on winning money in Tatt.’s I know it would kill
me, too.
A lively dividend came to hand at a meet-
ing at Johannesburg recently when an unknown nag
named Everil unexpectedly bolted with a race. The
tote, a 10s. one, was, as usual, in two sec-
tions, the inside machine being for white sports
and the outside one for the black, brown and pie-
bald fraternity. Both machines, of course, are
coupled and pay the same dividend. When Everil
won it was found that one lone ticket, held by a
grinning Kaffir, had been taken but. Before the
amount of the div. was declared many sports rushed
the native with offers as high as £2OO for his winnings,
but the black clung to the pasteboard till he was
handed £357 6s. Has anyone ever heard of Noses
McShortodds even offering 7llf to 1 about the rankest
outsider ?
*/* * *
“Musketoon” : At Forbes (N.S.W.) races
the other day jockey Alick Higgins told of a queer
happening. While he was riding Panda in the
Orange Cup the mare fell, broke her shoulder, and
had to be destroyed. The same day at Grafton
(N.S.W.) his brother’s mount in the principal race
also fell, broke a shoulder, and had to be shot. And,
to keep the series of coincidences going, both jocks
escaped injury.
* -*..** * *
“Jobber”: Included in the programme of
the Gezireh Sporting Club’s races (Cairo) is an
event for horses owned and ridden by British
soldiers, and there’s generally a fair sprinkling of
our bloques on top. At one meeting when the horses
hopped out of the barrier none of the riders noticed
that the flag went up for a false start. They
battled it up the straight and fought out a good
finish, but it was declared no race, all bets to stand.
The “books” are Australian soldiers, with their
tickets printed, and working on the dead-level. Some
of them do a bit, too, in the leger, with crown and
anchor, spinning-jenny, etc. They’re hard-doers,
these bloques of ours.
* * * *
“Norbell”: Fibelly, Bauaualand’s Acting
Minister for Justice, has been tapping the corpulent
bag of the Q.T.C. In August last the Government
started to annex all totalisator fractions and un-
claimed dividends, with the Result that for the six
months up to February 29 the Treasury received
£l4BB from fractions and £847 in respect of
unclaimed divs. Whereupon Fihelly slapped his chest
and remarked that it was only a fair thing. For-
merly the clubs even retained the fractions of a shill-
ing, but now they shell out everything to investors
except the fractions of copper, and that goes to the
State. Ikey’s troubles about fractions. He just
takes all he can.
* * * *
Les Darcy met Les O’Donnell at Sydney
Stadium on Saturday. The heavyweight had, of
course, considerable advantage in tonnage, height
and reach, and he wanted them all for self-protec-
tion. Darcy gave him a torrid time in all but the
fifth round, when O’Donnell delivered himself with
force and speed on the opposing jaw. In the seventh
he was badly used up and the towel arrived at a
merciful moment. Darcy’s next engagement is a
second conflict with the Greek-American Brown, who
always enters the ring determined to knockemsteef,
but generally hasn’t been doing it.
HUGH THUMBLE,
Secretary M.C.C.
“Trot”: The whereabouts of the world’s
middleweight championship seems likely to quickly
become more mysterious than ever. Jeff Smith used
to masquerade as the holder, and was about as much
entitled to it as Bill Squires. When he went back to
America the worn-out George Chip made an exhibi-
tion of him until losing on a foul. Ahearn was re-
garded as the champion of England, and a week or
so ago Mike Gibbons settled him in one hit and
looked about the biggest thing in the game. Now
Jeff Smith comes along and outpoints Mike. This,
of course, shoves Jeff up a long way. Perhaps he
can beat Gibbons again. In that ease he would be
taken in America as the absolute topnotcher, particu-
larly as the records allow him a win over Darcy (the
one he got when Dave Smith threw the towel in at
Sydney Stadium during the first fight, in which
Smith fouled the Maitlander). It would be no use
anyone claiming the championship for Darcy, though
as a matter of fact his moral right to it would be
undoubted, since there can be no question at all about
him Being Smith’s master. McGoorty, too, could
wallop Jeff; so, too, could Jimmy Clabby, and per-
haps Fritz Holland. If Darcy is a wise youth he
will promptly interview Messrs. Gibbons and Smith.
If he doesn’t hurry that championship will never be
his. He will be no lighter directly. If he acquires
it, the subsequent arguments could proceed in Aus-
tralia, where the disturbances are nowadays—well,
nothing to make a noise about.
* * * *
“Swish”: “Smelling Salts” says (B.
23/3/’l6) that it is “the easiest thing in the world
for a man’s chief second to take a bribe from the
other side and throw in the towel,” and affirms that
‘the proper person to announce his desire to quit is
the boxer himself” when he has “had enough.” There
are some men who never know when they have had
enough—men in whom the combative instinct is so
powerfully developed that they would keep on bat-
tling to the hitter end and be cut to ribbons before
voluntarily quitting. Does “Smelling Salts” want
to 101 l back on the front seat and watch a brave
man pounded to pieces because he refuses to run
away ? _ Anyhow, in nine cases out of 10 a beaten
boxer is incapable of expressing his conviction that
the battle ought to end; right crosses to the jaw
transpire with great velocity, and his wits have
usually vanished; he doesn’t know what is happening
And few winning boxers like to give the final clout
in cold blood. More than once I have seen Fritz
Holland and McGoorty drop their hands opposite a
reeling opponent and look to the Law to hold up its
walking-stick. If “Smelling Salts” thinks it easy to
make money by acting as a second and taking bribes
he ought to get into the business and prematurely
fling in the wipe for, say, Darcy or Red Watson or
McGoorty. There might be cash in it, but there
would be a lot to happen to “Smelling Salts” before
he reached his dividend.
* * * *
“Cleido”: What’s “Sardonyx” (B.
16/3/’l6) giving us when he asserts that no boxer
to-day could have laid a glove on Peter Jackson and
Griffo in their respective classes. If he seriously
believes that Jack Johnson, for instance, at his best,
could not have stood up to any man that ever lived,
don’t let me detain him any further. Any specialist
will attend to his trouble.
* * * *
Those Yanks who were breaking into
loud, hoarse laughs at Jesse Willard as a humorous
circumstance were amused too soon. Their next
job is to bring someone along to beat him, for just
at present the prolonged cowboy has a real solid
claim to respect and the world’s championship. To
annihilate the adipose Mass’ Johnson was perhaps
not much of a feat, though there are few, if any, of
the loud talkers in the game who even now would
try to repeat it, but it lifted Willard into the lime-
light, and the walloping he gave Frank Moran last
week-end at New York suggests that the job of
shifting him is going to call for great exertion.
Moran was about the best of the possible contenders
for the title, and unless the cable is a liar he was
a lucky pug to be on his feet at the end of the
tenth round. On this performance it looks as if
Willard is in danger of remaining-champion until he
perishes from senile decay or highball. He can
now proceed to earn about 10 times more cash every
week by imitation biff than a grateful nation pays
President AVilson for writing notes.
* * * *
Arthur Mailey, of Balmain (Sydney), has
been getting a heap of wickets this season. In the
just-ended round (v. North Sydney) this “bosey”
bowler reaped 14 wickets for 96 runs (nine clean
bowled). Mailey’s tally for the season now stands
at 90, and as there is only another round on the
slate, a special effort should push him over the cen-
tury mark and put new records jup.
Dunedin (M.L.) reckons it has found an
all-round cricketer. His name is Gasey. In a recent
first-grade match with Albion, Crawford, formerly of
Adelaide, managed to accumulate 49, but the rest of
the team made only 34, Casey collecting seven
wickets for 39. He finished with the hat-trick, and
thep asking for a bat helped himself to 61 runs,
exactly half of the score made by his own side. A
bowler by the name o’ Casey coming from Whusky-
ville would be a cheerful circumstance. But has
there ever been a cricketer with a moniker like that?
Auckland (M.L.) has produced a kid
named Dacre who looks like being able to do some-
thing. He showed up so well in school cricket last
year that he was given a chance in a first-grade
team, and at once became beastly rude to the local
bowling. During the 1914 and 1916 seasons he has
only twice failed to score, and among his efforts
are 196 n. 0., 188, IT2, 143, 140 n.o. (twice), 139
n. 0., 114 n. 0., 108 n. 0., 107 n.o. (twice), and so on.
In two years he has made 37 journeys to the wicket,
has been 10 times not out, has banged himself past
the century 12 times, and has accumulated 2372
runs, an average of 87.8.
* * * *
“Sarto”: Someone wants to know (B.
23/3/T6) why the umpire always stands to the leg
side of the batting man. The only explanation is
that on the other side he would be in point’s road or
point would be in his. Also, the bat would obstruct
his view of the facts when a stumped decision was
called for. Fieldsmen on the leg side do not usually
hover round the umpire like they would have to do
on the opposite side. Still, it seems immaterial where
a lot of umpires stand.
* * » *
There is now only one survivor of the
pioneer, team of English cricketers that came to Aus-
tralia in 1861 —old Bill Caffyn, who has just had
his 88th birthday. Bill lives at Reigate, near
London, where he is proprietor of a barber’s shop.
A .number of Australian soldiers are quartered at
Reigate, and the old man’s great delight is in chat-
ting with them about their country. He did not
return to England with the rest of the ’6l team, but
remained for some years as coach to Melbourne and
Sydney cricket clubs. “I taught Spofforth, Murdoch
and others all I knew,” says old Caffyn, wherefore
he reckons himself the “father of Australian cricket.”
A dozen years ago he published his autobiography
under the title of “Seventy-Eight, Not Out.”

game. (< First innings, Evans 4 for 51, Garrett 5 for

Garrett. ‘That prize is? jnine.” And lit was, too
Averages, Garrett 9, Evans 9.1. The winner on
each innings had lost on the two.

W. H. Calvert, a Tasmanian country
cricketer, who plays a game occasionally when he
can spare time from running a big orchard, has a
fancy for collecting centuries and has put 23 of
them together. His best effort was 184 not out, but
he generally gets tired and retires after going a
little way past the 100. s s

Saturday, m Brisbane, playing for Fernbrae (4th

* * * *
Teams representing Vic. and South Aus.
soldiers lately played a football match according to
Australian Rules in London. Vic. won by six goals
to four. The Times describes it as “a curious mix-
ture of Rugby and Association.” While the game is
“informal and rather scrambling” it is “undeniably
fast and entertaining as a spectacle.” Also, it is
“companionable, each man being marked and shep-
herded about the field by an enemy, so that both
teams seem to be strolling in friendly couples ” Keen
enough from the start, the play became “almost fren-
zied in the final quarter.” But the Australians
taught The Times something more than a new game:
the vast majority of the spectators were Australians
and Maoriland soldiers who “yelled for ‘Vic ’ or
‘South,’ and gave advice to individual players with a
vigor and freedom quite unusual on British football
nelds.
* * * *
The people who are ruuning football in the
Speck don’t know where they are. The three senior
clubs at Hobart decided against playing the usual
piemierslup roster this season, and it was suggested
that school matches should take the place of the ordi-
nary premiership round. But at the annual meeting of
the League a motion in favor of the abandonment of
premiership matches was defeated, and it was left
to the committee to say whether they should be
played or not. The committee is pretty certain to
try to run a season if it can, so clubs may be called
on to play for the premiership when they have
unanimously decided that they don’t want to and
when the greatest honor belongs to the crowd that
has most of its members away.

Bill Corbett, champion swimmer of N s
Wales 25 years ago, remarked the other dav tw
30 seconds for 50 yards in the water was looked
upon as something to whoop over in his time The
statement was provoked by the speed shown'bv the
kids at a recent schools’ gala. A 13-year-old lad
named Minahan, of North Sydney, covered the His
ance in 30 3-ssec., and the same afternoon he trav
riled 100 yards. in lmin. 121 sec. Schools around
Sydney are teeming with bronzed youngsters capable
of fast times. They all adopt the crawl stroke the
origin of which has been the cause of much heated
argument among adherents of the Cavills, Healy and
Wickham. The king of the kids is Crakanthorp, of
Fort-street, who has landed fully a dozen champion-
ship pots this season. He added a couple to the list
on Saturday, dashing over 100 yards in lmin
6 4-ssec.
* * * *
S. Beck and Gordon Lyons swam well in
the “under 21” Sydney Club’s championship on Satur-
day, and out of the whirl of foam Beck took the
scrap o’ paper in 59 2-ssec., with Lyons half a tick
behind. These are two out of the” less than half-
dozen occasions in which the minute-hand on the stop-
watch has been unable to get right round during a
100yds. swim in Sydney this year.
* * * *
“Pick”: “Lance Private” (B. 16/3/’IG)
has evidently never heard of the Quetta wreck, or he
would not claim the 27 hours’ swim of Captain
Gregory off the W.A. coast as a record. When the
Quetta bounced on an uncharted coral reef off East-
ern Queensland some years ago a 16-year-old girl
went overboard. She swam to a floating spar, but it
became waterlogged by the weight of two lascars.
The girl then swam away and continued until she was
sighted from a passing steamer and rescued 36 hours
after the catastrophe. Leaving out of account Cap-
tain Webb’s 60 hours’ swim, done in 1879, in an
artificially-heated tank in London, the Quetta mer-
maiden easily holds the record for authenticated
swims.

“Moana”: Apropos the £7750 (B.
24/2/’l6) bagged by D. Resta at the game of whiz-
zing round the motor-racing tracks of U.S.A. That
represents what he won in prize-money, but it is
only a small part of his earnings at the hair-raising
business. Added to this there are appearance money,
a retaining fee, a salary and frequent bonuses.
Altogether Resta collected over £30,000 during last
season.
* * * *
“Sarto” : Ever attend a bush sports meet-
ing ? I did the other day and learnt something. I
forget how high they tossed the sheaf—4oft. or
400yds., or something. Anyhow, they expect it to
come down about next Thursday. In the ladies’
stepping, 50yds., the winner stepped the distance
exactly. This is how she-did it. She surreptitiously
hobbled herself about the knees with a piece of string
measured to allow her to step the exact yard. 1
know because—well, never mind. In the tug-of-war
the woodcarters pulled themselves into the final. So
did the tomato-pickers. When the teams lined up
the woodcarters were a man short. They asked for
a substitute, and started to explain that the missing
carter was “crook” ; whereupon the captain upended
himself and said, “What’s the good of telling lies
over it—he’s gone on the shicker. Will you give us
another man in his place?” As shicker was a thing
that might reasonably happen to any of them, being
bushmen and Australians they said “Righto.” But
it availed the carters nothing. They had borrowed
a surplus tomato-picker.
* • * »
“Jwosp”: A returned soldier, who was
sent there to convalesce, has given Rotorua a boost.
One day when he was out of a job someone told him
about angling and trout. He bought a rod and paid
money for something that looked like a bent pin and
a piece of a feather. Then he proceeded to the
stream and hauled out 65 speckled specimens. This
basket is claimed to be a record for one rod.
* * * *
Four red deer have been presented by
George Chirnside, of Werribee Park (Vic.), to the
Rotorua (M.L.) Rod and Gun Club. The animals
reached Sydney from Melbourne by steamer last week
and were sent on to Auckland. It is expected that
this infusion of new blood will assist in improving
the herds wherever the strangers are liberated.
* * * *
As a result of the war foot-running'
is at a low ebb in Tas. At the St. Patrick’s
Day Sports at Hobart the committee had to cut out
the Sheffield and the Maiden Handicaps because there
was nobody to run in them. As the geegees haven t
gone to the Front the committee filled the hole m
the programme by shoving in more trotting races.

“Row A”: Pity the poor chorus-gent in
these explosive days! Lots of other men are
engaged on work that is almost as useless ;
but they haven’t got to do it in full view of
the public and in time to music. Before light
opera -became featherweight farce stage
“business” wasn’t so flagrantly foolish and
unworthy of a grown man —and there were
musical compensations in the scores. The
chorus gent then was generally a brigand or
a smuggler, and when he encountered his >
sisters of the chorus he merely had to put an
arm round some girl’s waist and conduct
himself in a more or less sane fashion. Now-
adays when the jigging music starts he has
to waggle his head or nod it, or hop and hold
his hands in a begging-puppydog attitude, or
shuffle in sideways at arm’s-length as one of
a line. Generally speaking, he is a walking-
stick for the show girl in the show dress, and
forced to do things that would be a degrada-
tion for a small boy. From this side of the
footlights I would say that he was acutely
conscious of the humiliations he is subjected
to.
“Te Babarantiko”: The most remarkable
piece of balancing I ever saw was superin-
tended by a handsome Abrahamic juggler
in Pagoda-road, Rangoon. The patriarch
placed three empty beer bottles, one on top
of the other, on a smooth spot on the road.
Then he spoke to a goat —an ordinary-look-
ing grey-and-black William with a nice
beard and a Methodist smile. Promptly Wil-
liam leaped into the air and landed with
hoofs bunched together on the neck of the
topmost bottle, without visibly disturbing
the pyramid. There he stood with the same
benign smile on his face, till, at a sign from
the juggler, he hopped to the ground, leaving
the bottles standing as before. And here’s a
delicate trick I was shown on a ship’s deck
at Penang. The juggler’s boy, a graceful
slip of burnished brown, poured two small
heaps of sand about two inches apart on a
deck plank. Into the apex of each heap he
stuck'a safety match; then, flinging his legs
aloft he walked on his hands up to the
heaps of sand, daintily picked up the
matches, one between each set of eyelids,
dropped on his feet, and after a moment let
the matches fall into his hand.
All actors don’t gather at Poverty Point.
Will Whitburn, who drove First Trip to vic-
tory in the Melbourne Derby Thousand for
trotters the other day, is the man who used
to black his face and sing comic songs at the
Tivoli. Whitburn has always taken delight
in steering trotters and has had a lot of
success at the business.
“First Old Man”: In the home of the
“movies,” which is Los Angeles, Cal., a bene-
fit performance for the British Red Cross
was recently got up by English actor Beer-
bohm Tree, who is touring the United states.
The piece of the occasion was Tree’s own

—in London. In the cast was Charlie

From London: Australian dramatist
Arthur Adams lias no reason to complain of
any lack of energy or resource on the part
of Bourchier or his press agentUm the matter
of advance booming of the London produc-
tion of “Mrs. Pretty and the Premier.”
Bourchier has taken the unusual course of
publishing a “foreword” to the
makes it a defence against some criticism.
For instance :
Some of those who have seen the play have taken
exception to the curiously free and easy and familiar
style in which the man in the street addresses his
Premier and the members of the Cabinet, but so far
from this being untrue to life it is literally a tran-
script from the actual. I was defending myself the
other night at a dinner party on this very question,
when quite unexpectedly a stranger to me
came to the rescue. “Oh,” he said, “Mr. Bourchier
is quite right. People would call his Premier ‘Bill’ in
Australia, and why not? They had all been familiar
with him in earlier days ; why should political rank
bar him off them in such a real democracy as Aus-
tralia?”
Bourchier is also anxious that London play-
goers should bear in mind that “Mrs. Pretty,
like all women in Australia, has a vote,
which explains why female suffrage plays
such an important part in the piece.” The
lady is played by Miss Kyrle Bellew, and
Bourchier considers himself lucky in having
the advice and assistance of Norman Page,
just arrived from Australia, who is- seeing
to “all matters of local color, manners and
customs.”
Ola Jane Humphrey when last heard
from was at her old home in Los Angeles
visiting mother and chattering to interview-
ers. She still calls herself Princess Ilassan,
in spite of the fact that she declares she has
left her Turk forever. Hubby, who wanted
to push Ola into the back row of the harem
as one of the extra ladies instead of giving
her the leading r6le, as the actress expected
he would, is now a general in the Turkish
army guarding Constantinople. Ola says
she is going to wait until after the war
before pursuing him with divorce papers.
But perhaps the Russians will pursue him
first with cold steel. That w uld save a
lot of expense and be just as effective.

THE PLAY BEGINS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Awake! the fiddlestrings of morn
Are tuning for the song of day,
And stirring notes on fresh winds borne
Show Earth all ready for the play.
A murmur comes of vamping strings
And players waiting in the wings.
The sun smiles just above the sea —
A stage director, pleased, it seems;
A tramcar waits upon the lea ;
Afar a touring milkman screams;
And, sign that sleep from all should pass,
The dew is rising from the grass.
The stage is well and truly set,
The orchestra and players all
Are waiting; we may not forget
We must be ready for the call.
The play begins, for I can hear
Jones murmur: “Come, let’s find a beer!”
N.S.W

taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on the MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.

TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC)
CORRESPONDENTS.
D’A.L.M.: Poor little trifles. ... G. 8.: Why is the
wooden boy drawn larger than the wooden old man?
•... M.R.: Stop it!. .. .Miss C. J.: Sorry no value.

“Homo”: Let me introduce the homesick
bushwoman. She had been brought in from
Old .Tunee to Wagga (N.S.W.) hospital to
be divorced from an attack of diphtheria.
For a few days she fretted in the ward, and
then in the early morning she wrapped a
blanket over her nightdress and stole away
for home. She had only 20 miles to walk,
and that was a mere detail.
“Chasuble”! Although the ugly old brown
stone Melbourne Cathedral of St. James
has been bodily shunted from the south-west
to the north-west of the city, it does not
succeed much better. Archbish Clarke sent
Archdeacon Hindley to England to engage
a missioner, who would transform St.
James’s and St. John’s into thriving gospel
warehouses; but the war hangs all this up.
The dismal St. John’s, Elizabeth-street
north, never has more than a handful of
people, but is buoyed up by immensely
valuable Elizabeth-street and other pro-
perty. The Collins-street Independent
Church leans heavily on its rents, and so
does St. Luke’s, South Melbourne, where
the Presbyterians turned a wretched little
tomb-like bethel into a new edifice by a
property deal in their Clarendon-street front-
ages. Trinity Church, East Melbourne, is
supported by the rent of mansions, and the
North Melbourne Wesleyans were low down
until they realised the brilliant idea of
building houses. The old Crown grants cut
the place up generously in a religious age
when all around was wilderness. Now the
only wilderness is inside the churches, and
there on the rents of mammon the droning
parson, calls the empty pews to repentance.
There is something wrong somewhere.

IN BANANA LAND
Passed out in the Big Smoke the other
day, D. T. Seymour, one-time Commissioner
of Police. He came to the colony in com-
mand of the 12tli Regiment away back in
’63, and his sub-tropical experiences so wore
him down that he was but 86 when the
order came to rejoin his colors aloft.
Wm. Kellet, a well-known South Bris-
baneite, passed out last week. Born <6
years ago in Dublin, he levanted to Aus-
tralia at a very early age. He was manager
of Emu Creek station for a while, looked
after the Grange horse-breeding station for
the Bells, and then dropped for a spell of 10
years into Parliament, where he legislated
on good old Tory lines and talked a lot
about the risks of Capital. Afterwards he
went into the stock and station agency busi-
ness. He was full of prejudices and kindli-
ness to the last.
An agitation has been started at Charters
Towers to amalgamate the City Council, the
Water Board and the Queenton Shire Coun-
cil. The shrivelled population of the field is
now only about 1500, and it is burdened
with three chairmen whose annual allow-
ances total £350, and the same number of
office staffs. Unification would save several
thousands a year.
“S.P.": “F. Paul's” remarks (B. 2/3/T6)
do not cover all the woes of the canegrower
in the Babinda State Mill area. In addition
to the order preventing the small grower
planting further cane —owing to the big
man having monopolised the full capacity
of the mill —there is a prohibition against
selling or leasing land in the area without
the Minister’s approval. Seeing the obstacles
that have to be clambered over before appro-
val can be obtained, the prohibition is almost
absolute. On top of all this the small farmer
is hit by a special tax on the land he is
prohibited from using. It’s enough to drive
one back to the city ; and if things don’t soon
improve the district will be in the hands of
many Italians and a few big home-grown
land-grabbers.
The meat interests are furious because
Premier Ityan is determined to keep prices
down. He lias fixed the price for Imperial
supplies at 4 7-Sd. per lb., and that required
for the State shops at 3RI. per lb. After
failing to get an increase on the export figure
a vigorous attempt was made to have the
local rates levelled up. But Ityan stood his

ground; and the Courier, which has been
hurling bricks at the Premier for having
failed to redeem his promise to reduce the
cost of living, is now frantically recalling
that it had always warned electors that
Labor Government meant confiscation and
■ robbery.
“Itan” : When Tommy Tanna was ordered
back, home, there were many who, not being
under indentures, could not be uprooted.
They mostly remained in the sugar districts,
and it was feared that they would develop
into mendicants and nomads. Instead, for
the most part they have settled down as
cane-growers on their own account, and are
developing into as good; citizens as it is
possible for Colored Brother to be: Their
salvation has been the prohibition of liquor.
In the beginning the Kanaka could not
understand the use-of mqney; he based the
rvalue .on the size of the coin rather than the
color of” the metal. ' But he has developed
into a thrifty farmer, and often has a better
idea of the science of tillage than tlnr sur-
rounding whites. He is also, for the most
part, honest and iridustrious, ambitious that
hisf children should get education and morally
healthy.- It: is all a fine reaction from the
heathen whose main desire was to put the
first white he met in the communion-pot and
gnaw his cooked bones.
"Ossian” : Two coincidences anent Premier
Ryan, Crown-Solicitor McCawley and Under-
secretary McDermott, who have left by the
Ventura for London on State business. All
three have been schoolmasters, and the trio
can trace ancestry to the 3000-odd Irish
kings. McCawley’s chief business in London
is to instruct Ryan in the “Eastern” case
an appeal to the Privy Council involving a
claim against the Government for a modest
£lOO.OOO. Ryan has also a squatter’s brief
before the Woolsack, and it is whispered that
bis fee is a tidy one. McCawley. who is also
Under-Secretary for .Justice, is a legal young-
ster who, by rare ability, won the Crown
Solicitorship* at the age of 28. He is a
barrister of nine years’ standing only, with
an official and professional record which is
the envy of the older type of civil servant.
McDermott, Under-Secretary to the Chief
Secretary, is about 55 years of age. He has
an amazing memory on the literary side —
Earl Bryce when here a couple of years ago
said McDermott was the best-read man lie
had met. Peter knows his Johnson almost as
intimately as the obsequious Boswell knew
the Great Bear. Ilis forte is literature — a
fact which brought him from the teaching of
State-school children in the woolly west to
close association with Premier Byrnes, and,
later, ensured his present appointment in
succession to Brunton Stephens. He accom-
panied Denham to London two years ago, and
his voice was apparent in several, of Den-
ham’s post-prandial utterances in Fogland.
Australia has three good representatives in
Pyan, McCawley and McDermott.
A pig fattened at one of the sugar farms
was brought into Bundaberg the other dav,
and sold for £9 15s. This is said to be a
reco.tl for Bananaland. Other hogs may
have grown - fatter; but they have been
mercifully kept off the market.

What Billy Didn’t Say
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We’ve got the finest land on earth,
A land that shouldn’t dream of dearth,
And yet, unless you lend and lend,
That we may spend and spend and spend
We’d soon be stumped or up a tree,
As helpless, stiff as stiff can be.
It’s pawn and pawn
* From dusk to dawn,
And then again,
In fear and pain,
From dawn to dusk
It’s chew the husk;
And when we’re short of corn ar.d wine,
It’s curse the Kaiser and his swine!
We’re always lifting wages up,
And running o’er the brimming cup;
On “good conditions” we insist
And idolise the Horny Fist;
And if you cry: “It's grand, but, o>
How do you keep it jigging so?”
I simply say: This is the way—
It’s pawn and pawn
From dusk to dawn,
And then again,
In fear and pain,
From dawn to dusk
It’s chew the husk;
And when the booze is turned to brine
We scour the trough beside the swine.
We strive and storm, we strike and strike,
As often as we well like; -
We down the tools for simple fun,
And howl for joy that toil is done ;
This life is all a joke to us —
We keep it up without a fuss.
If you will leud what we will spend,
From dusk to dawn
We’ll pawn and pawn;
And then again,
In fear and pain,
kt. .-.-fYoSi pawn to Musk
WVII chew the husk;
And. when the booze is turned to brine
AVe’ll wolf the straps beside the swine.
QUEENSLAND,
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP
Brisbane, March 25,1915
My Dear T i ufttoo min dah, —
This has been a military week. (W a
uons began on Monday night, when his F*
led a force into Enoggera to open the ‘W
"hioh the Y.M.C.A. has built' with W,l,
and Eliza Ilall money to make Billji m com
fortable in his temporary home. On Wednea
day a couple of thousand reinforcement
advanced upon Bell’s Paddock and played th!
war-game under the critical eye of our Maid
Governor, who has participated in three dis
turbances and got into despatches. Xext -
C. of E. Help Society contingent, led bv
chief aerial pilot Donaldson, and reinforced
by Brigadier-General Lee, Chaplain-Colonel
Garland, the Arthur Morgans and a host of
camp-followers, invaded the rifle-range camn
on Thursday, to open a “hut” in place Jr'
the Society’s tent that took a box-seat on a
tornado a little while back.
Friday was the Y.M.C.A.’s Club Hut
Day, the proceeds of the raid going towards
building huts wherever there are Austra-
lian soldiers. Isles-laue, opposite the
G.P.0., was turned into an arcade, and all
the near-by business houses lent basements
Three of the stalls were decked in the colors
of Queensland regiments—the Ninth and
Fifteenth Battalions and the Second Light
Horse. His Ex. visited the street bazaar
in the afternoon and left with bulging
pockets, which included a brooch, consisting
of a Club Hut badge and a boomerang!
handed him for the baby.
To-niglit more funds were raised for Club
Huts at the Exhibition, where the Brisbane
Operatic Players, under Leonard Francis
made their first public appearance. The
team has been together for only six weeks
so the success of the concert is all the more
creditable. Mrs. S. 1». Harris, Miss Tessie
Muleahy, Miss Webber, Lieut. Marcus, and
Magnus Fisher (in a brand-new khaki suit)
were the soloists.
Last Monday afternoon war-correspondent
Harry Gullett and his wife entertained a
party of friends, mostly of the press, at
Lennon's; and on Wednesday the Brisbane
journalists hit back with tea and cake at
Johnson’s Cafe.
The Master of King’s and Mrs. Scott
Fletcher entertained the Council and stu-
dents at the unveiling of the College
Honor Board, which already holds 20 names.
Lieut. F. T. Small (who won his commis-
sion while on active service with the
Engineers) and Pte. A. H. Jones had been
invalided back and were at the party. Major
Pendlebury did the unveiling. The popu-
lar master and Mrs. Scott Fletcher are
leaving Brisbane next week, and will pitch
camp in X. S. Wales.
To-night the Premier was given a send-off
banquet at Johnson’s Cafe. Mrs. Ryan is
going to England, too, and P. J. McDermott,
Under-Sec. of the Chief Sec.’s Dept., and T.
Mc-Cawley, Under-Sec. for Justice, are to be
of the party.
D. J. Abercrombie, Brisbane manager of
the Commercial Bank of Sydney, saw the
veteran T. A. Dibbs into retirement, and
then decided it was time he went too. The
men who had worked with him gave him
an address last week, and there was a dia-
mond brooch for his wife. A. L. Dawson,
his successor, made the presentation.
The death at Toowoomba of Mrs. Tooth,
widow of W. B. Tooth, once of Clifton
Downs, brings back memories of the days
when squatterdom ruled the country and aped
the English squirearchy. The Tooths were the
most hospitable of all, and they had a wide
reputation for their kindly treatment of
travellers, great and small.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Wallace Brownlow is now assisting to
run the State'home for decadent drunks at
Peel Island. Wallace got the billet when
the previous occupant set out to help strait-
jacket the Potsdam drunk.
Methodists are about to undertake the
salvation of the nomads of Arnheindand.
The beat of these Binghis covers an area ot
about 350 by SO miles, and they are almost
as migratory as a flock of birds. The Rev.
J. Watson intends to accompany the blacks
on their wanderings, but he says he hopes
ultimately to bring them to a settled me.
Which is likely to be about the unkindest
service he could render.
Councillor Outridge, of Tingalpa Shire,
has hit upon a remedy for one of the States
greatest curses —the flying-fox. He told his
brother councillors at a recent meeting tna
the young fox was really a dainty dinner.
The councillors laughed, but Outridg
s}lenced them by remarking that they ha
all partaken of the beast under a fancy nam
at a recent luncheon and had apparen
enjoyed it. If somebody will now disco'
that priekly-pear is a delicacy in vegetali -
the high-cost-of-living problem would
solved, and the curses would become two
Bauanaland's great natural resources.


MEGGITT
The Big' Oil Manufacturers
- of Parramatta.
a
This photograph was taken the morning after the fire, almost two
years ago. The mill was totally destroyed, and it was agreed on
every side that work would not start again for at least six months.
This did not suit Meggitt Ltd., however, who, by the aid of a
trilling body of men, shifted 2000 loads of rubbish, cleaned and
sorted the bricks, pulled down the machinery, cleaned, repaired and
erected it, while at the same time a temporary tin building was
erected, and operations were again in hand exactly 6S days after
the morning of the fire.
By “Scrutator.'’
T
'IIE large mill of Meggitt Ltd., of Parramatta, is in many ways a structure unique in
Australia. It is a standing monument to the enterprise of its proprietors, and ought
to he a stimulus to the whole industrial community. It is an effective refutation of
the common pessimistic wail: “It can’t he done in Australia. We haven t got the population.
It would he too risky. We might lose our money.” Well Meggitt Ltd. didn’t take that view.
They were Hot afraid to lose money—what they were after was making money. And they have
made it —the wails of the pessimists notwithstanding. The small, insignificant plant which set on
tears ago no doubt provided the pessimists with material for scoffing is no longer insignificant.
On the contrary, it has silenced the pessimists, and evoked the admiration of all who have seen
it. For it is the concrete embodiment of enterprise, capacity and courage. In a word, the
mill has had an honorable past—has a creditable present, and in all human probability can look
confidently forward to a magnificent future. Meggitt Ltd. have (lone two things they have
made money for themselves and set an example to the whole of Australia. They deserve the
money and Australia needs the example.
Facts am) Figures.-—As facts and figures are more impressive than generalities, let it
be said that the business, which began seven years ago with a plant not worth more than a few
pounds, has now magnificent buildings, plant, new machinery arriving, enormous silos, and huge
storage reservoirs, amounting in all to close on f.>0,000.
The Mill. —The mill is the only one in Australia in which linseed is crushed for the
production of oil and of linseed-oil meal. The oil has won a reputation for purity all over
Australia, and the by-product, linseed-oil meal, is highly popular in England and is beginning
to win its wav to popularity even in Australia. The big works are very handsome, but the
chief merit is the efficiency. At the present moment preparations are being made for the
installation of machinery for crushing linseed equal to anything else in the world. lhe
Mill Manager recently visited America for the purpose of importing similar plants, ant
declared on his return that he saw nothing like it. not even in the great United States What
.trikes one most on entering# the very beautiful offices, facing Macquarie Street, where the
clerical work is carried on. It is quite evident that the proprietors are not of opinion that
business and aesthetics are in antagonism. At one side of the office building, near a piece
of beautiful green lawn, can be seen growing the linseed plant. This is but an experiment, but it goes to prove that there is no tie
linseed in Australia. At present Meggitt Ltd. import their linseed from India, but they are quite convinced that it could be «iown ■ - ‘ . m . ival ' of the
one is struck by the huge concrete foundations that are being laid for the erection of silos, which will be capable of holding - U s • • rhro hau
seed it is emptied into a pit. whence it is conveyed by bucket elevators into the silo. It then gravitates oyer crushing roHei., « . S - comes froin these

The whole process works like clockwork and enables the article to be marketed at a minimum of labor and, tberefor « e * a oil"for the factory and feed

V Goon Staff—While Meggitt Ltd. are proud of their achievements, they do not ignore the fact
that they have been largely due to the good and faithful services of their employees. "Both in the
office and in the mill,” said the Managing Directoi.
Mr. Harold Meggitt. “we have splendid workmen—
employees who put both heart and brains into theii
work.”
Invitation to Farmers. —The oil produced by
Meggitt Ltd. has already established itself in
Australia, but the oil meal has not yet won the
reputation which it enjoys in England and which
it ought to have here. Fortunately, however, the
article will be on exhibit at the forthcoming Show,
and it is to be hoped that our farmers will then
embrace the opportunity of becoming acquainted
with its high merits. Indeed, Meggitt Ltd. are so
confident of the high value of their linseed meal
that they extend to all farmers a cordial invitation
to visit the mill.

Writes a friend from London, after seeing
seme of the MS.: “Australians will re-
gard ‘Reid’s Reminiscences’ as something in
the nature of a cookery book —so many are
roasted in it.”
The rejuvenated Tasmanian Government is
going in for more State Socialism. To see
that its cubs’ teeth are looked after the
tiger is appointing a couple of State dentists,
who will put in their whole time, with the
help of suitable assistants, attending to the
youngsters’ gnawing machinery.
At Ballarat the other day Mary Godsell,
charged with vagrancy, appealed with some
eloquence to be sent to Pentridge if she
must go to any prison. “I have tried most
of them,” she said, “and Pentridge is the
only one where a lady is treated like a lady.
As for your Ballarat gaol, I never go there
without feeling I am sacrificing my self-
respect.”
Arthur Robinson, Vic. M.L.C., having
volunteered for the A.I.F. and been turned
down by the medical examiners on account of
a farsighted near ear or some such fatal
obstacle, has allowed the rest of the firm off
the chain and accepted the entire burden of
the concern’s law-business. Robinson’s sol-
dier-partner is Forrest Davies, now in camp
and nearly ripe for the Front.
Barker is the only Labor member of the
last Tasmanian Assembly who is not stand-
ing for re-election. The Labor League has
a rule that its candidates shall forward
proof that their nominations are in order,
and a time is fixed prior to the date of
selection. Barker forgot the rule until it
was too late and loses a sure seat in conse-
quence. Incidentally, he was the white-
haired boy of the early-closing-of-bars crowd.
There seems to be a moral in that, some-
where.
Geelong (Vic.) Council is no., doing some
hard thinking, and the citizens are likely to
do some harder mental exercise presently. A
few years ago the Council bought the old gram-
mar-school site for a town hall at a cost of
£ll,OOO, but before it could get the hall built
the citizens tossed the job lot out and the
hall scheme was dropped by everybody but
the contractor, who handed in a bill for
£2OOO. Last week the site was offered at
auction, when the highest bid, recorded in
solemn silence, was £6500.
“B.”: A threatened shortage of the right
kind of paper for stamp-making has alarmed
the Postal Department, and the public has
been asked not to use two penny stamps where
one tuppenny will serve. This precaution
may be all right, but what about saving sup-
plies by dispensing with the system of pay-
ing for telegrams with postage stamps? This
system was, of course, adapted and is main-
tained for audit purposes, it being much
easier to chbck receipts if stamps are
offered as evidence. Still, Private Enterprise
can sell boots and sausages without requiring
its customers to lick a lot of stamps and
plaster them on the bill, and the Dept,
ought to be able to evolve a scheme whereby
the amount of king’s dial Squandered on
telegraph forms could be banked. Inciden-
tally, such a scheme would save a big print-
ing bill, and would lessen the danger to the
public s health from the übiquitous germ.
The rumor that there is a war on has
reached King Island, and a large portion
ot the able-bodied male population is at
the I ront or on the way. But according
to the King Island News those who stay
on the Bass Straits speck don’t let the war
get on their minds too much. It warbles :
After years of manly struggle witti odds against
galore.
We’ve kept our aspirations to the pitch of asking
more,
And now we’ve got what’s wanted, by efforts tireless,
The wary Radio Department has given us wireless!
And another cheer :• —
The Naracoopa jetty and the telephones are hasten-
ing, too, along
To completion and commencement, where prosperity
looms ahead.
Isn’t it ever so much better than being' as-•mod as
dead ? ” •
So it is, brother—so it is !
“Barcoo”: Twenty years ago I ran into
the original of “Dr. Nikola.” lie was the
late Dr. Fitzgerald, of Irisford, on the Barcoo
(W.Q.). With him I met the then unknown
Guy Boothby. Few of. Fitzgerald’s friends
knew that in his youth he had trav-
elled much in China and Thibet. llis admin-
istrators, examining his effects -after his
death, came on a locked diary, which let a
light on his early career and, incidentally
identified him as “Dr. Nikola.” Boothby!
when I met him, was picking up bush iim
pressions and contributing to one of the
Sydney weeklies. It was during this period
-that he put in six months with the Irisford
medico.
“Nemo”: Most people have heard of
Balfe’s “Bohemian Girl” ; this par. is about
Balfe’s Tasmanian Boy. The father of the
lad is Oscar, for years editor of a Launceston
paper, and closely related to the composer—
Dublin men all of them. When the war
started young B. O. Balfe joined the 3rd
Engineers, and was in the landing at Gaba
Tope. In peace time he is a dentist, but in
the war he has been content to be a sapper.
After several months’ hard toil enteric fever
took him off the Peninsula. lie was some
weeks in hospital in Lemnos and then was
sent to England ; but lie’s all right now, and
crying to get back to the scrimmage.
When the war broke out the Remington
Arms Co. was manufacturing weapons Li-
the sporting trade. " It did not wait for
orders, but knowing they would soon flood
the market it spent a million dollars on
extensions, and in less than eight months a
plant had been laid down in which 36.000
people are now employed, turning out 10.000
rifles a day and ammunition by the carload.
One_ of the first orders received was for a
million rifles for Russia. The output is
reckoned to be worth £500,000 per week,
which is almost large enough to attract the
attention of the gentleman to whom N. S.
\\ ales pays £4 4s. a day for remaining in
America. It is a while since that pleni-
potentiary did anything pf visible value, but
he might earn a month’s screw by telling us
plainly and categorically the points of dif-
ference between the Remington works and
methods and those of Lithgow.

The Australia Hotel
BUSINESS AS USUAL.
The Management of the “Australia”
Hotel are gratified to state that, not-
withstanding the upset caused by the
recent fire they are able to announce
“business as usual.” The service in
the Grand Dining Room, on the
Balcony, and in the Winter Garden
is being conducted in the same style
that has made the “Australia” Hotel
so popular with its patrons, while the
arrangements for Private Dinners and
Suppers are working with their accus-
tomed satisfaction.
Throughout the holiday season
arrangements for Afternoon Teas will,
as usual, be carried out in the Winter
Garder), which has been running con-
tinuously throughout.
J. URE SMITH, Manager.

crown Svo, ss. net.

for observing the work of the British Expedi-
tionary Force in France and Belgium, and the
devastating effects of the war in those coun-
tries. His book consists of a series of vividly-
written sketches of me® and things which came
under his observation at the Front.

ment Act 1913, you are hereby called upon to
