Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, sucb as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi-
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay),
EUDENTA Is therefore regarded as “the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free 1/3. Free sample posted.

The Inky Way.
“The Deacon's Apprentice”: Startling
item from a religious publication which, I
grieve to say, doe.s not get into every home:
After four months in bed, God heard their prayers,
and I recovered.
But perhaps the grateful convalescent meant
to say that he spent the four months in bed.
Prayer otherwise would be almost as slow
as the telephone.
.T. M. Perrier, who lately died at Cronulla
(X.S.W.), wandered up and down the Inky
Way, knocking a crust out on Ma State
papers as far back as Bourke. Me drifted
to Australia in the ’so’s, and though he
joined in the rushes on Maoriland, Victoria
and N.S.W. goldfields, he got little dust out
of them. His only son (Jack) is shouldering
a rifle at a Front somewhere. The old man
was seven years over the three-score-and-ten
mark when the Editor closed down.
* * * *
“D.F.”: The picture-show people are not
the only experts in the art of faking. A
year or so ago the ferry steamer Derwent,
which plies across its namesake river in
Tasmania, caught fire while lying at the
wharf at Bellerive, and had most of her top
works burnt off. A month or two ago a
picture of her as she appeared after the fire
was published in Melbourne. But she was
not a ferry-boat this time ; she was a British
transport which had been knocked about
and set on fire by Turkish shell-fire and then
beached at Lemnos ! She looked like it, too,
but they should have taken out the telltale
background.
* * * *
In an article dealing with food-conditions
in Germany, the Argus quotes a travelling
Dutchman who had a meal at Munich :
I paid 4s. for one horse d’oeuvre, consisting of a
herring, a couple of radishes, a small piece of
celery, half an egg with a sausage, and a couple of
shrimps, which would certainly not cost more than
Is. in Holland.
Of course the horse was in the sausage.
* * * *
“J.F.11.” (London) : Any Australian Inky
Wayfarer ever attempted to discover the
most popular newspaper title in the Com-
monwealth? In Great Britain and Ireland
Advertiser heads the poll with 160. Times
comes second with 150. News is a near
third with 149, and Gazette and Chronicle
run a dead-heat for fourth place—l2B each.
* * * *
“Batman”: Young Egypt is picking up
English in good style, and we get some of
our own back thereby. The other morning
a diminutive purveyor of the daily sausage-
wrap piped this outside the O.C.’s tent:
“Paper! Verra nice news! Cap’n Murphy,
the damn scoundrel! Paper!” Of course,
nobody had carefully instructed the youth-
ful alien beforehand as to what he should
say outside the captain’s tent in order to
make sure of a sale!

English war economy. As a result of their
decision to print their minutes without punc-
tuation the Northfleet (Kent) council will
save Is. per week. So the ratepayers
can look for something like this: “The Mayor
unveiled a tablet to the memory of Alderman
John accidentally shot as a mark of affection
by his brother aldermen.” „
Lieut. Sid Addison, who has been battling
along the Inky Way in Tasmania for a good
while and has edited the Huon Times for
the past seven years, is going up in the
world. The military authorities have selected
him to undergo a course of instruction in
flying, and he hopes to be on the wing to the
Front before long. <■

* * * *
Manchester Courier has stopped the press
after 90 years. This is the paper that trans-
formed Alfred Harmsworth into Lord North-
cliffe; his peerage was the reward for taking
it on when it was in very low water and
when the Manchester Conservatives were ex-
ceedingly anxious to keep it afloat. When he
tackled it, at the earnest request of the local.
Tories, it was taken for granted that he”>
would make it go; but the Libeial Guardian, l
was too strong a rival for even Harmswortli
to hustle off the path.
HYMN TO AURORA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You that were pink in the dawning—•
That dawning old and clear—
hen the kings of the earth came fawning
Out of a night of fear
To kiss the glint of your sandal lacj
As the young sun breathed on your happy face!
You I have used in my verses—
A serf to ink and pen—
\\ ith your useful light on the hearses
That bore forgotten men
Down to the brink of a paper grave—
A goddess once, you are now a slave!
lou, through the blind and the shutter,
Whimper an old refrain,
Till the little birds are a-flutter
Close to my window-pane—-
Oh, curse your peevishness, let me be
Till your handmaid brings me my morning tea !
NJS.W.

Little Australians.” She has a

appeared as lead in over twenty
dramas and comedies.
Miss Spaull is a true
Rexona girl and writes as
follows:—■
Rexona is a glori-
ous soap. I regard
it as absolutely the
best I can buy. It
seems to protect the
skin against sun
and wind and re-
moves any tempo-
rary skin blemish
caused by ill-health.
I also think the
world of Rexona
Ointment. For
cuts and skin
troubles of all kinds
there is nothing to

lieves and then

Rexona Soap and
Rexona Ointment
are indeed twin

THE RAPID HEALER
This Week s TOILET HINTS
INTERESTING SELECTIONS FROM THE WORLD’S SMARTEST BEAUTY ARTICLES.
SIMPLE RECIPES MOST EFFECTIVE.
How to Discard an
Unsightly Complexion.
“Toilet Club Notes.”
How many women exclaim as they behold
their ugly complexion in the mirror, “If I
could ouly tear off this old skin!” and, do
you knowq it is now possible to do that very
thing? Not to actually remove the entire
skin all of a sudden; that would be too
heroic a method and painful, too, I imagine.
The worn-out cuticle comes off in such tiny
particles, and so gradually—requiring about
ten days to complete the transformation—it
doesn’t hurt a bit. Day by day the beautiful
complexion underneath comes forth. Mar-
vellous! No matter how muddy, rough,
blotchy or aged your complexion, you can
surely discard it by this simple process.
Just get some ordinary mercolised wax at
your chemist’s, apply nightly like cold cream,
washing it off in the mornings.
Why Have Grey Hair?
When a simple, old-fashioned and harmless recipe
will correct it.
Few people know that grey hair is not a
necessary feature of age—that it can be
avoided without resorting to hair dyes. A
very old, home-made remedy will turn the
hair back to a natural color in a few days.
It is only necessary to get from the chemist
an ounce of concentrate of tammalite and mix
it with four ounces of bay rum. Apply this
simple lotion to the hair for a few nights with
a small sponge, and you will soon have the
pleasure of seeing the greyness disappear.
This recipe is perfectly harmless, is neither
sticky nor greasy, and has given perfect
satisfaction for many generations to those in
possession of the secret.
A Strange Shampoo.
“Cosy Corner Chats.”
* * * I was much interested to learn from
this young woman with the beautiful glossy
hair that she never washes it with soap or
artificial shampoo powders. Instead she
makes her own shampoo by dissolving a
teaspoonful of stallax granules in a cup of
hot water. “I make my chemist get the
stallax for me,” said she. “It comes only in
£lb. sealed packages, enough to make up
twenty-five or thirty individual shampoos,
and it smells so good I could almost eat it.”
Certainly this little lady’s hair did look won-
derful even if she has strange ideas of a
shampoo. I am tempted to try the plan
myself.
Blackheads Instantly Go.
The new sparkling face-bath treatment gives instant
relief.
A very simple, harmless and pleasant pro-
cess is now used to remove blackheads and
correct greasiness and large pores in the skin.
You have only to drop a tablet of stymol,
obtained from the chemist’s, into a glass of
hot water and bathe the face with the liquid
after the effervescence has subsided. The
blackheads will then come right off on the
towel. The enlarged pores immediately con-
tract to normal and the greasiness disap-
pears, leaving the skin smooth, soft and cool
and free from blemish. But to make sure
that this desirable result is permanent, it is
advisable to repeat the treatment several
times at intervals of say about four or five
days.
Is Powder Necessary?
“Practical Suggestions.”
I say emphatically, No! There is a simple
lotion which can be easily and cheaply made
at home, and it is at the same time both
effective and beneficial to the complexion.
Cleminite is a splendid substitute for face
powder, which is at the bottom of many
complexion troubles. Get about an ounce
from the chemist’s and dissolve in four table-
spoonsful of water. The result is a fine clear
liquid, which instantly gives the face, neck or
arms that peach-like bloom of perfect health.
There is nothing to equal it for greasy skins,
and the result lasts all day long under the
most trying conditions. [4

Brian at Clontarf.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
OCH, ’tis weary we are from the wars,
And ’tis time to be sleeping
With the thatch between us and the
stars,"
Or the skies grey with weeping.
But a man couldn't help it all,
For his dreams brought the hunger
Of deeds, and his shadow was tall
When the morning was younger.
I went by the road of the sun—
’Twas a day warm with laughter—-
’Twas lightsome the thing that I won,
But the grey ghosts came after.
’Twas a dance with the fairies at noon,
But I heard the Slice calling
In the cold of the dark with no moon
When the night mists were falling.
For the word that I spoke was a chain
Past the power of my breaking,
And the oath of my pride grew to pain
On the road of my making.
Och, ’tis swords that are sharp for the
death,
But above the red sinning
What more has a king but his breath,
A thin breath, for the winning?
Holy Brigid of Lamps, I am old,
Make the pulse of me stronger!
Och, give me the hand that can hold
The staff a while longer !
The thing that is done is so small,
But the big dreams are calling—
Och, sure, a man’s shadow is tall
When the red sun is falling !
n.s.w.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
Travellin'.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hand the snow was lightly failin’
Hon ’er shoulders all the while.
f M HAT was some twenty-seven years
| agone. We were going at Christmas-
time in a special holiday train over
the Blue Mountains ; and the singer was a
slight, rather undersized bushman of the
Nearer West, as it was then, of the better
class, who leaned back luxuriously in his
seat in the second-class carriage and enjoyed
his singing. Luxuriously, I say, because it
seemed to be a relief from the everlasting
saddle-way of travelling.
He enjoyed his singing, I said, and so did
I, a young workman sitting opposite him,
who was going “up to the Mountains” that
had neither novelty, rest nor holiday for
him; for he had a job there before, and was
going up to another.
But we were happy travelling in those
days, second-class or first; and the further
some of us were going together the happier
we were. Ways were never long and weary,
nor dull and hot and dreary; and no one
bothered about his watch or time-tables, or
asked how far it was to any station or
what time we’d get there. The whisky flasks
went round and the bottled ale went round ;
and very soon Christian names like Bill
and Jim and Jack went round; and blokes
and coves and fellers and chaps were given
nicknames that lasted them to the journey’s
end and sometimes beyond it, amongst men
who’d never seen each other in their lives
before and might never meet again; and
the brotherly joke went round at each
other’s expense, and there was all the best
of good-fellowship, and we all were mates.
We never bothered about “refreshments”
on those journeys. We were too healthy and
happy to eat —merely “scoffed” a sandwich
or two occasionally, or tore at the leg or
wing of a cold fowl. And that only out of
politeness to the giver, perhaps some lovable
and provident elderly party of the jovial,
well-to-do-digger persuasion. The sight of
stout, black-coated persons of full habit of
life, seriously, earnestly and even nervously
anxious at their soup, with napkins up to
their fat, white throats in the refreshment-
rooms, always filled me with a strange sort
of pity. The others regarded them (or
didn’t regard them) with utter indifference
or as beneath contempt, or above it, if you
like.
A peep into first-class carriages, when we
got out to stretch our legs, mostly revealed
stout, red-faced squatters of the old school
who lived on their runs and had their
women-folk in reserved carriages away from
them; and jolly, good-natured, healthy and
honest-looking commercial-travellers telling
their yarns and cracking their jokes whereof
the humor redeemed the smut. And young
lioneymoon couples evidently in a bad way.
In the next compartment there was a
merry party of all sorts —young fellows
from the city, but mostly in what are called
the “humbler walks of life.” But they
weren’t humble; they were too care-free to
be humble, or anything else except happy.
They had a keg of beer with a wooden tap,
and it had a place on the seat no matter
which one of them had to stand or squat on
the floor. They took great care of that keg
of beer, and their humorist (there was
always one in those days) rested his arm
and head lovingly on top of “Bobby” and
dozed between taps.
Now and again they'd hand a billy out
through the windows into the next com-
partment for’ard of theirs, where a section
of the party were; and then they’d hand a
smaller billy past, per favor of us, into the
next compartment aft of ours, where one or
two stray members of their party were;
and, anon, they’d hand the larger billy in to
ms And so on, after what a French friend
of mine used to call “ze propaire interval.”
They got off at a thirty-minutes’ refresh-
ment station, and the last we saw of them
they were grouped behind the joker of the
party, who was standing on Bobby selling
the train, from engine to brake-van, with its
contents, not forgetting the honeymoon
couples; and the last we heard of them they
were giving us three ringing cheers from the
platform.
And the dust was lightly falling
On her shoulders all the while.
And the sods are lightly lying
On her coffin all the while.
Ah, well! Some of those merry, careless
boys of ’BB and ’B9 may be some of the hard,
silent, sullen, suspicious elderly travellers I
see now. I noticed the change in travelling
before the war —long before. Coming down
from Mudgee, second, Easter before last, and
oyer those same mountains, there were only
silent, sullen, resentful men in our carriages,
and a dusty, shabbily-creased, dispirited and
uneasy shade of a jockey from the Mudgee
racecourse. His taded and sickly-brown
clothing had little three-cornered rents in
it, as if he’d run through barbed-wire
fences from the course to the railway sta-
tion. Probably he had good cause to run,
as Mudgee (and all other) racing goes now.
He seemed too furtive and unsubstantial
even for it to be possible to take him up for
anything. And there was the faded ghost
of an old-time “commercial” who clung to a
Gladstone protected by a holland cover, evi-
dently to help the illusion that it was still
new. And a cranky Yorkshire man—and
they can be cranky. And a disgruntled
Kentish assisted-immigrant, returning from
a job they’d got him in the J.unee district,
and telling the Yorksliireman how he’d
knocked the boss off the plough and got his
bundle and left. There was also something
about “these Australians.” I tried to pro-
mote conversation-—it was a dead, blank
failure. I asked a civil question and, fail-
ing to get an audible reply, I barked at
them that I was deaf. It only elicited a
sickly grin. The Australian seemed to have
vanished and to be extinct. There teas a
tall, lank, sad, silent, elderly man, grey as
a badger, who might have been a grinful
Australian once ; but he had grown too old
and weary and careworn for the job, and
had given it up in despair.
And now for another trip.
The Little Landlady is in a first-class
for’ard with Nigger the cat. Charley the
dog is in a compartment of his own in the
brake-van aft. Charley belonged to the
country—l don’t know which part—and
to a young soldier who took him down to the
city and left him with “friends” and went
to the Front. Now he is going back to the
country with a master who, I hope, will
treat him as well as the first one did.
There are various little ways of doing our
little bits. The Little Landlady, for in-
stance, wanted to boil a bullock’s-heart and
bring it with her, so that I might give
Charley a slice or two at various stations.
Behind me a fortnight of nightmare, clear-
ing up my own affairs and packing and
crating furniture under the advice of a
little woman who was worse when she tried
to assist.
Farewells of, and to, belated friends; the
last bell; the last kiss; the last clasp of
hands ; the whistle, and we are moving with
all our loads of sins and sorrows and pains
and cares, but still with a blessed relief
and —thank God from whom all mercies fall!
The sleeping-car attendant is an old man
—so old and faded that I shall not remember
whether he had a moustache, side whiskers
or a short beard. The Old Guard. The Old
Guard says they won’t serve him at Moss
Yale. So l have a beer, and produce another
glass and put it on the bar and smile at the
laughing girl there as if I know her. Pro-
bably I do. Anyway, I get a stiff nip for
the Old Guard and slip aboard and put it
under one of the seats; and presently—very
presently—l see the Old Guard’s shadow
stoop to that seat, and then his shadow,
glass in hand, reaches down the water
carafe, dilutes sparingly and drinks.
Shadow of a Shadow, Old Guard! Plow
many hands in the dead, dead past may
not have lifted glasses to drink to you !
We move again. The Old Guard seems
a trifle less shadowy and comes and sits by
me thoughtfully till I go along and turn in.
But not to sleep. Then the long roar—roar
—roar-r-r, with the undercurrent of hum and
buzz, with millions of z’s in it, down into
the Goulburn Valley.
Goulburn held no interest for me, except
as regarded getting a beer; and I turned
in again and tried to sleep, and failed. So
I watched the embankments, grey fences and
gbost bush race past in the half moonlight;
and after that went into the smoke com-
partment and sat with several others who
had murder, rape and embezzlement on their
minds —or seemed to have. No one spoke.
Wherefore I turned in again, and this time
I woke to sunlight.
Dry gullies; bleached and fire-blackened
logs and stumps ; blazing watercourses —just
ashen clay-gutters in the scalded ground,
where water seldom comes and never stays
an hour; unutterable scrub. The posts of
the very fences are woolly grey and seem
old, old as the dead trees and as forgotten.
On clearer slopes for miles and miles the
grass from the last rains is dry beyond
dryness and tough beyond toughness-
Everywhere the bleached pale gold of
stubble. The land seems old, old and older
to me. God! ours is the oldest land on
earth —it is old beyond anything.
I didn’t wake in time for Cootamundra,
and the Old Guard must have wanted his
morning’s nip, poor old chap ! However, I’ll
fix him up all right at Junee. Whereat he
confides to me concerning “commercials.”
“If they want to give me a bob,” he says,
“let them give me a bob. And if they don’t
want to give me a bob, let them don’t. But
they always begin about here to find fault
as an excuse for not givin’ me anything.”
They look like that.
Wheat, wheat, wheat! Wheat on both
sides as far as the eye can reach, and every-
where, if I’m not mistaken, the bronze of
Farrer’s wheat. The sun is up, but the
homes are still at rest excepting where blue
smoke from a kitchen chimney hints of a
hearty breakfast. This is not sordid, slaving,
cow-cocky country. It is Wheat!
Presently the “commercials” begin to come
out. stonily suspicious of everybody and of
each other.
We change at Junee; and I spot dog
Charley chained to a lamp-post on the plat-
form and on the best of terms with the
guards. He is specially demonstrative
towards a young fellow in khaki who is
going down by the next train. Perhaps he
recognises the uniform. Something also
seems to move this soldier-boy from the
country, for he makes as if he’d almost take
Charley nr» in bis arms, bisr ns lie is.

DROPSY
Watery Swellings under the Eyes and in the Ankles, Limbs and Body.
The fluid you take with your food,
in a single month , weighs as much as
your entire body.*
Most of this fluid has to pass out
through the kidneys, bladder and
urinary system.
Imagine what would happen if the
outlets for this water were stopped up !
In a few hours the limbs and body
would swell to a great size with the
collected water.
That is what actually takes place in
dropsy.
Dropsy is the name given to the
soft, watery swellings which appear in
the ankles, limbs and body, under the
eyes or in the eyelids, or on the backs
of the hands. If you press your finger
into the swollen flesh the pitting slowly
fills up with the water again.
*(».e. —If you are of average weight.)
DROPSICAL EYES.
DROPSY is not a disease in itself,
but it is serious because it indicates
serious disease.
It may show that the kidneys are
failing to separate the waste water from
the blood, or that there is something
obstructing the urinary canals and
preventing the waste water passing out;
or it may be caused by defective action
of the heart.
Tapping takes away the water from
under the skin, but more water soon
collects, because tapping cannot reach
the cause of the dropsy, and as long
as the cause remains the dropsy will
continue.
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills have
a quick and direct action on the kidneys
and bladder. They promote a free flow
from the system, washing out clogging
impurities from the passages, and
draining out the collected water through
the natural channels. They gently
lead the kidneys back to health and
activity, and thus reach the cause of
most cases of dropsy.
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills have
no action on the heart, nor on the liver,
stomach or bowels ; they are solely for
the kidneys and urinary system, and
are, therefore, of the highest value in
dropsy, gravel, stone and all diseases
arising from kidney and bladder trouble.
Doan’s Backaolie Kidney Pills are sold by all
chemists and storekeepers at 2/9 per bottle, or
post free by Foster-McClellan Co., 76 Pitt Stree ,
Sydney.

PILLS.
On catching sight of me he sets up such
a barking as I never heard from a dog
before. Every dog has his day, but Charley
must have had a very uncomfortable and
anxious night of it. However, his troubles,
which he never shows by any sign to any-
body—except perhaps at times to me —will
soon be all over now.
Wheat, wheat, wheat, wheat, wheat,
wheat, wheat! Seas of it on both sides, and
all to feed a great, ant-swarming, mostly-
useless city like Sydney, or to be sent abroad
to feed soldiers who are murdering each
other for one man’s hateful greed !
Narrandera, with hints of rocky outcrops,
ivied red brick, picture-book little churches,
and real homes and greenery; and so on to
Yanko and Leeton, the irrigation area —to
a new life in an old dead land that is being
made to live again.
HENKY LAWSON.
The Imperative Apricot.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
fruit harvest is hurrying citywards
by train and waggon. In tons it is
reaching the entrance of temples
raised to JAM. Amid a helter-skelter of
borses, lorries, boys with trucks, men with
barrows and buckets it reaches the interior
and pours itself over the benches and tables
for “treatment.” The women who annu-
ally swarm the jam factories as casual help
are watching for the “ads” in the dailies.
And presently they see it.
Treating fruit. One hundred women wanted to earn
big money.
Now I would earn money—in any quantity.
So, armed with nothing save my inexperi-
ence, I approach the portal, guided thereto
ay slovenly boys who pass to and fro with
;rucks of jam, tins, trucks of labels, trucks
af lids.
A glimpse within shows ninety-nine of the
vomen already at work. Will the boss take
ane more to make the tally even? He will.
‘Or’right,” he says. “Go up an’ change.”
Up a narrow stair, the handrail sticky with
iam, through a door smeared with jam, past
ivomen whose skirts, hands and hair are all
rglue with jam, into a dining-dressing
room where hot water makes tea and, inci-
lentallv, cleanses one of jam.
The walls are lined with wire-gauze com-
partments within which, like spiders in webs,
ittendants lurk who take our clothes through
trapdoors and imprison them under lock and
key.
Comment upon this to other “big money”
iarners who are peeling off silk blouses and
rlimbing into ancient duds in anticipation of
being employed as fruit “squashers” wins an
explanation. “Pretty mixed lot here in the
busy season —lot o’ strays, ye know. Alight
:ose even your corset if yeh didn’t cage it.
Woman ’ad a baby stole ’ere once, so I’ve
bin told.”
Sensation among us, and a derisive growl
from a mother who is struggling out of a
petticoat and into a kind of sack. “No
'casion to steal that sort o’ property,” she
says acidly.
“Mind, I’m not sayin ’ nothing,” adds the
narrator of past crimes, with a glance at
the attendants. “Things are aw-right now,
I b’lieve; and”—virtuously—-“long as you
keep yourself to yourself an’ don’t mix with
the rough lot, you’ll be right.”
Descending we join the ninety-nine. A
forewoman pins blue pay-slips to our shoul-
ders, and, picking our way through much
loose fruit which litters the floor over, we are
driven to where mounds of apricots pile the
table and clamor for immediate treatment.
Someone pitches to each of us a wooden
bucket and a mallet, both stiff with cold
fruit-juice. We surround the too-quickly-
ripening apricots, and, lo! we are established
—full-blown “squashers,” in the way of earn-
ing “big money.”
“Treatment” of the enemy consists of
grabbing a handful and disembowelling it in
the fraction of a second, pitching the pulp
into one bucket and the stones into another.
Being a novice I don’t for the moment realise
that my fingers are my tools; but a voice
shouts in my ear, piercing the din of
machinery. “Fire away !” and I fire. At
least the apricots do. They slither in all
directions when I, picking up a handful in
clumsy imitation Of experts, try to extract
all the stones at once. Off they go like
fireworks, flying high among the belting that
whizzes overhead. I make frantic dabs at
them. No use ! I am no juggler; they none
of them return to me, but, falling low, join
the lost legion already on the floor. After
that I try more delicate methods, stoning a
single apricot at a time as one would at home,
with as little handling as maybe. This is
easy but not conducive to big money. I am
squeamish, too, in the matter of apricot
juice. I don’t like it in my eye—or my hair.
And bits of soft pulp squirted over the nos-
trils produce apricot apoplexy in no time.
A smart knock on the eyeball from a fly-
ing half-green apricot produces ophthalmic
distress and tears, both of which have to be
treated acrobatically with the shoulder,
owing to the hands and arms being golden
and gluey to the very elbow with the juice
of a million apricots clamoring for the jam
room. All this counts for delay, and delay
in bucket-filling means a loss of ljd. or
thereabouts, and how shall I make good with
losses of this magnitude?
I am struck in the eye so often with these
flying missiles that I become suspicious of
my neighbors. Have they got me “set”?
Are they doing it on purpose? My gorge
rises and I try what can be done with some
very pulpy stuff, two handsful of which I
squash savagely. The obvious result should
have been to squirt the juice of it at least
into my aggressor’s eye; what actually
happens is that the beastly stuff turns on
me, leaving me gasping, while x paw round
for a handkerchief or a bit of sacking as an
eye-wiper. And the other “squashers,”
innocent alike of vendetta or revenge, go on
“speeding up,” performing miracles with ten
fingers, ten apricots, ten nuts, filling three —
yea, four—buckets to my one.
As the day wears on they develop a
perfect fury of speed. Grabbing handfuls
of mushy fruit they hold it over the waste
bucket, gouging out the nuts by some play of
hand-muscle quite unknown to anyone save
a seasoned “squash.” They drive a finger
right through an apricot, the nut shooting
out like a bullet and hopping neatly into
its appointed place.
The supply of over-ripe fruit temporarily
giving out, they roll forward dozens of half-
ripe and green, and with well-directed blow r s
of the wooden mallet smash them to a
workable condition. There is much com-
plaint over this. How can “big money” be
earned when fruit is unstoneable? Where-
fore the hatted and sticky youth who walks
round incessantly dumping boxes of fruit all
over us is exhorted to “bring better stuff.”
“Rats !” says he, and swamps us with more
Unripe. “Y’got to do ’em,” says he. “Take
the bad with the good. Y’ can’t have it all
rotten !”
Yet even as he speaks he relents, flooding
us with another ton or two —this time
frankly over-ripe. Whose the blame? These
poor apricots have been misused —have been
waiting, yes, waiting for us! They have
been outside, stacked in a hot right-of-way,
and were forgotten while greener stuff was
given pride of place. Fair is fair, and w T hen
a consignment of fruit is left in the sun till
it begins to boil, who is to blame if Stynko,
the.youth before mentioned, must scoop off
the top layer of fermenting mush and fungi
with a practised hand before he leaves us to
the situation?
When my first bucketful is ready I
stagger with it across the juicy floor, join-
ing in a procession of women similarly
armed. Slowly we converge upon a Sphinx-
like figure squatting at a desk and armed
with a snip. As we pass in single file she
takes the end of the blue slip depending from
our shoulders and punches a hole in it.
That hole represents ljd. earned by the
“squash,” or maybe three half-pence.
Before me is a little fat woman whose
every protuberance is glistening yellow with
juice, and whose pay-slip has twenty-four
holes in it already. By night-fall she would
would be a Jay Gould even at fd. per bucket.
Once past the Sphinx we press on to a
row of trollies holding wooden tubs into
which we pour our lid. worth. The con-
temporaries of Stynko wallow over these,
perpetually pressing down each instalment
till, the tubs being full, other Stynkos haul
them away to the jam room.
Through curves of belting that flog the
dusty air, wheels of whirring gooseberry
cleaners, massed effect of toiling squashers,
swift yet leisurely week-workers stemming
cherries and halving peaches, crash of tinny-
sounding machinery and the everlasting
mushy flop of fruit pulp, I glimpse an army
of Stynkos, priestly in jam-strewn overalls,
smiting the bubbling contents of cauldrons
with rods as Moses smote the rock, while
girls in leather leggings and coated all over
with wet sugar are the handmaidens. And
beyond this Inferno another impressionist
picture—a court-yard, a fallen horse, an
avalanche of apricots falling from an over-
turned lorry, and on a fresh lot of tables a
landslide of other apricots—apricots green,
apricots ripe that won’t wait, apricots rotten
that have waited too long, and a distracted
foreman looking doorwards, hoping to find
there another hundred Peris at the gate
anxious to earn big money!
Ench time I return from the “snipper” r
fmd the cunning “squash” aloneJX 1
has annexed some of my working a fe a Z
she has the space of two for the better „i
of hands and fingers in the pursuit S? 87
tune. But in return for thil robTer? &
she is a just jade) she has given me f ar l fl
of her refuse, so that an accumulation if
mouldy skins, cobwebs, orchard insects
fungi is cluttering up mv bench 1 d
I protest—timidly, it is true, for her eve
is somewhat fierce. ye
Rats!’ she barks, and squeezes out
twenty nuts at a blow of the mallet Then
this weapon upraised, she turns unon
Without loss of time I apologise unconditil:
ally, abjectly; and, the entente cordiale re
gained, she very kindly presents me with
another instalment of rubbish.
It is all one, however, for Stynko blindlv
bumps his way to us again and literallv
buries the hatchet for us with another
torrent of the accursed apricot. Sighin?
despairingly, I shake the perspiration from
my face. Instinctively I crave some easily
won El Dorado replete with punkah and f an
—far. far from the reek and clatter of this
jam factory, where nine hundred women
appear to have bathed in fruit pulp; where
giving up all attempt at neatness, they wine
their hands on their hips, their hair, and
lounge their bulky outlines upon mounds of
fruit in utter weariness, for standing ten
hours a day begets a slackness of poise hard
to overcome. The remaining hundred
engaged on the quest for wealth remain
decently clean. Maybe they are more expert
in diverting the fruit’s effusiveness into the
proper channels. *
An inquiry concerning “knock off” time
elicits no answer from the twelve women
who surround my bench. It’s an unpopular
subject where everyone is anxious to work
24 hours a hay in the race for gold, so ah are
conveniently deaf. Instead of answer a thin
voice cuts into the clatter of machinery, like
a million tin-cans in conflict. “How old
would you take me to be?” it asks.
“They’ll answer that, at any rate,” I
mutter. But no; not even that bait will
draw the majority of squashers from the pur-
suit of big money.
The theme gains listeners only among the
very young or the very old. I take no part
in it myself. It is a ticklish subject.
What more concerns me is “knock off”
time, for I have a mind to abandon big
money.
The foreman, being questioned, breaks
almost into tears. “Knock off! You don’t
knock off. never, not till the fruit’s done!
Knock off! Don’t you know you work over-
time, 6.30 to 8.30? Sleep? You don't sleep
—not in. the season ! Carn’t you see the
apricots! You carn’t keep ’em waiting.
They’re ripe! D’you want ’em to rot on
me? They got to be done right away. All
them”—he waves his hands inclusively over
the tables—” ’sides twenty loads waiting in
the lane, an’ three truckloads in the four
o’clock ‘goods.’ ’Struth! D'you think
we’re a school —in at nine an’ out at four?
Y r ou carn’t knock off! Apricots won’t wait.”
They do, though—for me. For, abandon-
ing my inexpert pursuit of big money, I
hand in my pay-slip, and receive—eleven-
pence.
